<page no="3x3v">
<heading>To the right Honorable And
my euer most Honored Lord 
Edward Lord Zowch Lo War
den of the Cinq Ports and one
of his Ma<expan type="">jesties</expan> most ho<expan type="">norable</expan>
Priuie Counsell/
</heading>

This Discourse which I wrote in those lodgings wherewith it
pleased your Lordsipp so honoured mee I helde it my dutie 
(in acknowledgment of that and many other your Lordsipps most 
free and honourable favours) to present vnto your Lordsipp <expan type="w+ch">which</expan>
the faithfull and affectionat seruice of

Your Lordsipps most humble
And most obliged seruant

Henry Maynwaringe
</page>

<page no="4x4r">
</page>

<section type="Glossary">
<page no="1">
<heading>
An Abstract and exposition 
of all things Perteyninge
to the Practique of 
Nauigation:/</heading>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="A"><alpha>A</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="aft(a)" lexeme="abast(a)">Aft or Abast</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="come aft(v)" lexeme="go aft(v)">Is a sea terme properly vsed abord the
shipp to distinguish betwixt thing<expan type="es">es</expan> done or placed 
fromward<expan type="es">es</expan> the Stemm toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the Sterne of the 
shipp. As when they saye come aft goe aft, or the like: That
is goe Toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the Sterne. Hale <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sheate Aft: The
mast hang<expan type="es">es</expan> Aft That is toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the Sterne. A shott Raked
the shipp fore and Aft. That is came in before and went
out a Sterne Alsoe we vse to saye Abast the foremast Soe
that the word is not <expan type="p-_">par</expan>ticularly lymitted to anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of 
the shipp: But from anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipp hath 
rela<expan type="">cion</expan> to anie thinge done or placed toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the
Stearne in respect of anie that are toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the 
Stemme: As for example when wee hoise our 
<correction type="d" deleted="saile"></correction> yarde wee saye stretch forward<expan type="es">es</expan> the mayne halle&shy;
yarde whose place is abast the Mainemast And 
contrarywise Stretch aft the fore halliar<expan type="es">es</expan><expan type="wch">which</expan> 
are placed before the Maine Mast but Abast the 
foremast. When shipps meete (or beinge in Consort)
desire to knowe how the Companie doth they vse to 
demand how they doe all fore and Aft The reason
whereof is for that the whole shipps company is
divided, <emend err="("></emend> both in respect of their laboure &amp; C<expan type="">ommaund</expan><expan type="es">es</expan> 
into Two <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan>. The Boate swayne and all the Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>
Saylo<expan type="+r">r</expan>s vnder his comaund to, be before the Maine 
Mast The Captaine master. Masters Mate 
Guners Quarter Masters Trumpeters &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> to bee
abast the Maine Mast:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="2">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="aloof(a)">Aloofe</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a terme used in Condinge a shipp, when 
goes vpon a Tacke, and is Com<expan type="m+_o+_n+_">mmon</expan>ly spoken from 
the mouth of the Conder, to the Steeres man, when
he suffers the Shipp to fall off from the wynde
and doth not keepe her soe neere by a wynd as 
she maye well lye:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="a'main(ex)">Amaine</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a terme used by the men of warre (and 
not by Merchant Men) when they encounte???FBlock???
a shipp for that implies asmuch to the other
as to bid him yield. Amaine is used in this same 
alsoe, when anie thinge is to be lett downe by a Tacke
into the hold or elsewhere or that a yard is to be loward
or the like; Then when they would haue it come downe
as fast as they came; They call Amaine<expan type="wch">which</expan> is to lett
goe that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Rope<expan type="wch">which</expan> they held before to lett 
it downe easilie and by <emend err="degees">degrees</emend>. It is alsoe an adiunct
to the greatest and cheifest of some <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the 
shipp vizt The Maine mast the Maine saile the 
Maine Beame &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> In Men of warr wee vse Wauinge
Amaine<expan type="wch">which</expan> is either <expan type="wth">with</expan> a bright sword or anie other
thinge to make a signe to them that they should 
strike their topp saile<expan type="wch">which</expan> Com<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly they doe 
either on the for topp or on the Poope, To strike 
amaine is to lett fall their Topsailes:
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="3">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="anchor(n)">Anchor</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="kedger(n)" lexeme="bower(n)" lexeme="kedge anchor(n)" lexeme="sheet anchor(n)">The forme and generall vse of an Anchor is
comonly knowen. But the severall <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> propor<expan type="">cions</expan>
distincc<expan type="">ions</expan> and apellac<expan type="">ions</expan> vnderstood by very fewe
but practised and experienced Seamen The 
Anchor doth consist of these severall <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan>. The Ringe
<expan type="y+e">the</expan> Head <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Eye <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Nutt <expan type="y+e">the</expan> shanke <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Beame or Arme, <expan type="y+e">the</expan> 
Flooke to<expan type="wch">which</expan> belongeth astocke The propor<expan type="">cion</expan> wch
it hold<expan type="es">es</expan> in it selfe is the shanke<expan type="wch">which</expan> is thrise as longe
as one of the flookes, and halfe the beame. The 
p<expan type="p">pro</expan>por<expan type="">cion</expan> in respect of shippinge is To a shipp of 500
T<expan type="">unn</expan>es wee allowe 2000 weight for his shanke Anchor 
the bigest shipp in England<expan type="es">es</expan> Anchor is but 3300
The distin<expan type="">ccions</expan> are <emend err="are"></emend> made by the vse according
to their propor<expan type="">cion</expan> they beare to the shipp in wch
they are imployed for that<expan type="wch">which</expan> in one shipp would
be called but a kedger or kedg Anchor<expan type="wch">which</expan> <emend err="("></emend>by 
occation of their severall vses receive severall names
and appellac<expan type="">ions</expan> are first a Kedger<expan type="wch">which</expan> is the smalest
wch by reason of that lightnesse is fittest to stopp the shipp
in kedginge downe a River: The next a streame Anchor
wch wee vse in deepe waters to stopp a tyde wthall 
in faire weather The others they call by the name of
first second and Third Anchor. All these beinge 
such as the shipp may ride by in anie reasonable 
weather seagate or tyde. These are somewhat bigger
then another an other and usually when they saile in anie Straight<expan type="es">es</expan>
or are meere a Port they carry two of these at the Bowe
wch is the couse they are alsoe called by the name of 
first Second and Third Bowers:/
The other<expan type="wch">which</expan> is the biggest and that<expan type="wch">which</expan> the Sea
men call their last hope and is neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> vsed but in 
greate extremitie is called the sheate Anchor 
this is the true Anchor a spei for this is their last
refuge. The Anchor is a Cocke bell when the 
Anchor hang<expan type="es">es</expan> right vpp and downe by the shippes side
And there is appoynted by the <expan type="M+s">Masters</expan> when they are
Ready to bringe the shipp to an Anchor Lett fall
</page>

<page no="4">
the Anchor let it goe downe into the Sea The
Anchor is a Peake: That is when heaveinge vpp the
Anchor the Cable is right <expan type="p-_">per</expan>pendiculer betwixt the
Hause and the Anchor The Anchor is fowle 
That is when the Cabell by the turninge of 
the Shipp is gott about the flooke<expan type="wch">which</expan> will 
not only Cutt the Cabell asunder but make
the Anchore not to hold. And therefore when
ever we come to an Anchor where there is tyde
we laye out Two Anchors soe as that vpon
the turninge of the Tyde, The shipp wind vpp
cleere of either Anchor. Cleer the Anchor 
That is gett the Cabel off the Flooke: or 
generally when they lett fall the Anchor they
vse this terme to see that the Buoy Rope or 
noe other Ropes belonginge to the shipp doe
hang aboute it, Fetch or bringe home the 
Anchor, That is to way it in the Boate and
bringe it a bord the shipp. The Anchor comes
home That is when the shipp drives a waye 
wth the tyde or Sea, This may happen either
because the Anchor is to smale for the burthen
of the shipp or for that the ground may be too
soft and oozie In such places we vse to hewe
the AnchorThat is to putt Boord<expan type="es">es</expan> of the
Flooke in the forme of the Flooke and make it
much broader then before. 
In Porto Ferrene by Tunis I sawe the 
experience of Tallowinge an anchore <emend err="whe">when</emend>the
ground beinge soe soft wee shodd our Anchors 
and yet they all come home, and the shipps
</page>

<page no="5">
drave a ground, only anie shipp<expan type="wch">which</expan> had an 
Anchor Tallowed road fast The reason 
I could never truly knowe but suppose that
it might because the Tallowe suncke deepe in 
the Ooze and found some harder ground at 
the bottom then the other, Boate the 
Anchor. That is putt it into the Boate:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="anchor(v)" lexeme="anchorage(n)">Anchoringe or 
Anchoridge</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when we lett fall an Anchor or more into the 
Sea <expan type="wth">with</expan> Cabells to them soe that the shipp 
may ride fast by them wee saye there is good
Anchoringe when there is Shoule water for 
In deep waters, the Sea hath more force against 
a Ship, and the Anchors are very longe a wayinge
upon anie occasion: Ground that is not to soft 
or Oozie in<expan type="wch">which</expan> the Anchors can have noe-
fast hold Nor too hard and knotty soe that it
ride in is a Stiffe claye or a hard sand 
where they may ride out of the waye of the 
`tyde And lastly where they may ride Land-
Locked, soe as that the Sea gate can have noe
power against them: To<expan type="wch">which</expan> may be added that 
the Lee Shore on every side is soe soft, that if 
a shipp come aground she can catch no hurt 
or else that they may have Sea roome to sett
Sayle If their Cabells breake or their Anchor
comes home That place<expan type="wch">which</expan> hath all these 
Comodities is good to ride in and here we saye
is good Anchoringe or Anchorige Bad Anchor-
ing is in a place where all or many of <expan type="y+t">that</expan> Contrary 
Conditions are to be found:
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="anchor stock">Anchor stocke</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a peece of Tymber fitly wrought and 
</page>

<page no="6">
<emend err="and"></emend> fastened at the Nutt<expan type="es">es</expan> crossinge the flookes
the use whereof is to guyde the Anchor vpon 
the ground that one of the flookes may be sure
to fasten in the ground wthout<expan type="wch">which</expan> the Anchor
would lye flatt vpon both the flookes, and take
noe holde. The propor<expan type="">cion</expan> whereof is usually 
taken from the length of the Shancke:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="arm(n)">Arme</form>
<xpln lang="en">This is not used as a word of Allarum att 
Sea as it is a land: for att Sea, wee oft vse to saye
make readie the shipp<expan type="wch">which</expan> implies the fittinge of
all thing<expan type="es">es</expan> belonginge to a feight. A shipp that is
full of Munition small and greate and her feight<expan type="es">es</expan>
and ordinances well disposed and placed is called
a shipp well armed To arme a shott is to bynd
some Ockhand or Rope, yarne, or ould clout<expan type="es">es</expan> &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>
a bout one end as in crosebarr shott it is most
co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed, That that end <expan type="wch">which</expan> goes fast out
of the peece should nott catch hold in anie flawes 
of the Peece whereby it be in danger to breake it
The same wee vse to anie Peece of broken iron
of one or two foote longe<expan type="wch">which</expan> we vse when we come
bord &amp; bord, in feight and of our greate Ordinance
wee vse alsoe to arme some smale shott for 
musket<expan type="es">es</expan> like our Crosebarre.
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="awning(n)">an Awninge</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a saile or <emend err="or"></emend> anie other thinge made of Canvas
or the like <expan type="wch">which</expan> is spread over anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te or all of the
shipp a bove the deckes to keepe a waye the Sunne
That thereby in hott Countryes men maye take
the ayre and yet not be soe subject to the beames
of the Sunne: In all hott voyages, This is of infinite
vse both to keepe men from the Sunne by daye, and
the dewes by night, <expan type="wch">which</expan> in some places are 
wonderfull infectious:/
</xpln>
</wordentry> 
</page> 

<page no="7">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="axletree(n)">Axeltrees</form> 
<xpln lang="en">The Axeltree is the same in a Carriage as in
a Coach or Carter And doth support the Cheekes
of the Carriage whereon the Peece doth lye 
Alsoe we call the Iron <expan type="wch">which</expan> goes through the 
Wheele of the Chayne Pumpe &amp; beares the
Waight of it: The Axeltree of the Pumpe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="B"><alpha>B</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bale(v)">Bale</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to lade <emend err="is to lade"></emend> water <correction type="i">out</correction> of the shipp holde 
wth Buckett<expan type="es">es</expan> Cannes or the like, This because
it is more labour<expan type="">ious</expan> &amp; tyres men sooner and doth
not deliver soe much as all the Pumpes will
wee never vse but in great extremetie when 
either a leake doth over growe the deliu<expan type="+r">er</expan>inge of the 
Pumpes or else that the Pumpes doe faile vs - 
wch happens manie tymes in extraordinarie longe
Pumpinge: That the Pumpe <expan type="wth">with</expan> over much
weareinge draw winde or Chance to be Stoaked
or else the Pumpe Boxes Irons or the like doe
faile vs:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ballast(n)">Ballast</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="ballast shoot(n)" lexeme="pouch(n)">Is that Gravell Stones <correction type="i">lead</correction> or anie other good<expan type="es">es</expan>
wch is layd next to the keelson of the shipp to
keepe her fast in the Sea; Of Ballast That is
</page>

<page no="8">
best <expan type="wch">which</expan> is heaviest, lyes Closest and fastest and 
is drest both for the shipps bearinge a saile 
stowinge of good<expan type="es">es</expan>, The health of the Companie 
and saveinge of Caskes or other good<expan type="es">es</expan> whereof
if a shipp have too much shee will drawe too much
water; If too little she will beare not sayle To
trench the Ballast that is to divide the Ballast
in anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipps holde, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Comonly done
to finde a leake in the bottome of the shippe or to 
vnstocke the shipp: The Ballast Shoot<expan type="es">es</expan>, That is
runnes over from one side to an other, and 
therefore Corne and all kinde of gaine is dangerous
loadinge for that will shoote, but only that they make
Poutches as they are called, that is Bulkhead<expan type="es">es</expan> 
of board<expan type="es">es</expan> to keepe it vpp fast, that it doe not
runne from one side to another as the shipp 
doth heele vpon a Taske:/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bay(n)">a Baye</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="headland(n)">Is when twoo Loyntes or Head Lands
lie soe farr of in to the Sea, that drawing
a straight lyne from the one to the other
there ys made towardes the Maine-Lande
a hollownesse or part of a Circle <expan type="wch">which</expan> is filled
<expan type="wth">with</expan> water, bee yt more or lesse, that same
is called a Baye vnlesse there be anie pas&shy;
sage Navigable thorowe for then it beares
the name of a Straight and not of a Baye
But comonlie wee doe not giue it the name
of a Baie vnlesse there be some emynent
depth and Indraught as tis vsuallie tearmed
And yt matters not whether the distance be&shy;
twixt the pointes bee little or much) for the
Baie of Biscaie, the Baie of Portingale,
the Baie of Mexico and divers others are
manie score Leagues over from head Land
to head Land And also in depth and Tar&shy;
bey in Deuonshire wch maine the like is not
aboue    Mile ouer 
</xpln>
<lemenote>
Word-entry absent from British Library Sloane MS 207 but found in British Library Additional MS 21571, fol. 15v.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="beak(n)" lexeme="beak-head(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Beake or 
Beakehead</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="beak's-head-steeve">Is that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te <expan type="wch">which</expan> is fastened to the Stemme of the
shipp and is supported <expan type="wth">with</expan> a knee <expan type="wch">which</expan> is fastened
into the Stemm and <correction type="d" deleted="loll"></correction> this is called the mayne
knee To this is fastened the coller of the 
mayne staye: In the Beakehead the foretackes
are brought to bord, And is the p<expan type="">r</expan>oper stand
where men doe handle most <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> of the sprit sayle 
and Sprit sayle top saile Rigginge, And it is 
alsoe placed for the fashion and grace of the shipp
The Beakeshead Steeves or stand<expan type="es">es</expan>, Steeveing,
That is stand<expan type="es">es</expan> very much <expan type="wth">with</expan> the outwardmost end 
vp toward<expan type="es">es</expan> to Boate spritt The Beakehead<expan type="es">es</expan> of the
Venetian Argores &amp; Spanish Gallions doe soe
very much - by <expan type="wch">which</expan> we knowe them afarr off./
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="9">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="beam(n)">Beame</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Beames are those greate Crosse tymbers 
wch keepe the shipps sides asunder &amp; doe support
the Deckes or loopes: accordinge to whose strength
a shipp is much the better or worse, able to carry 
Ordinance: All stronge and greate shipps have
a Tyre of Beames in hould,, that is a Rowe of 
Beames wherein lies noe decke The Maine 
Beame is ever the next to the Maine mast, at wch
place wee recon the bredth of the shipp. And
from this wee call the Beames, <expan type="wch">which</expan> forward &amp;
aftward by the name of first second, third &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> 
begininge from this wee call the Mydshipp 
Beame:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bear(v)">to Beare</form>
<xpln lang="en">This word in some cases is taken in the 
ordinarie sense and as for carryinge much, As 
when we saye a shipp will beare much Ordinance,
that is carry much by reason of her strength: Alsoe
the beareinge or Stowinge of much good<expan type="es">es</expan>, from 
whence when we describe the greatenes of a shipp
we saye she is a shipp of such a burthen But
is is vsed in many senses different accordinge
to the diversitie of the Phrases: to Beare saile
that is as much to saye she is a stiffe sided shipp 
and will not lower downe of a side <expan type="wth">with</expan> a greate 
deale of saile: a A shipp to beare out her ordinance
That is ment her Ordinance lie soe high, and she 
will goe soe vpright that in reasonable feightinge 
wether she wilbe able to keepe out her lower tyre
and not be forct to shutt in her Porte One 
shipp overbere the others That is was able in 
a greate Gale of winde to carry out more saile
then the other vizt A <xref>Toppsaile</xref> more or the
like To beare vnder the Lee of A shipp is 
</page>

<page no="10">
when that shipp is to weather comes vnder the other 
<emend err="the other"></emend> shipps Sterne and soe gives the winde to
her that is the greatest Curtesie that a shipp 
cann give an other att Sea:, The Peece will 
Beare more shott or not soe much The Peece will 
Beare more , That is she is
overcharged or else will endure a greater charge
The Peece doth come to beare: A terme in the 
vse of Ordinance by <expan type="wch">which</expan> is ment that now shee
lyes right <expan type="wth">with</expan> the marke:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bear in(v)">Beare in</form>
<xpln lang="en">when a shipp sailes before or <expan type="wth">with</expan> a large winde
into a harbor or Channell or else sailes large 
toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the land we saie she beares in <expan type="wth">with</expan> the
Channell Harbor or land: But if she saile 
Close by a winde we vse not that speech:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bear off(v)"> Beare off</form>
<xpln lang="en">when a shipp would not come neere a land or
an other shipp but goe more roomyer then her 
Course doth lye we saye that she beares off
from the land Alsoe when wee tell how our head&shy;
land Iland shipp or the like<emend err=")"></emend> doth lye from an 
other (That is vpon what poynt of Compasse) 
wee saye they beare right East or west or otherwise
off one an other: In hoysinge anie thinge into the 
shipp If it catch hold <correction type="d" deleted="of anie thinge"></correction> by anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
of the shipp or Ordinance or the like, They saye
Beare it off from the shipps sides, soe if they 
would have the Breech or mouth of a Peece of
Ordinance or the like, putt forwar<expan type="es">es</expan> on, They
saye Board off or beare about the Peece, Soe
that generally Seamen vse this word beare off
</page>

<page no="11"> 
in business belonginge to shippinge in steed
of the word Thrust off <expan type="wch">which</expan> to the like sense is
most Commonly vsed a mongst others:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bear up(v)">Beare vp</form>
<xpln lang="en">This is a word we vse in the Condinge the
shipp when as wee would have her goe larger or
more before the winde then she did Beare vp 
Round That is to saue to putt her right afore
the winde, or to bringe her before the Lee The
manner of doeinge it is noe more but thrustinge
the Helme vp to windward as farr as it will 
goe toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the shipps side:
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"> 
<form lexeme="bed(n)">Bedds</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the deckes lye to lowe from the Port<expan type="es">es</expan>
soe that the Carriages of the Peeces <expan type="wth">with</expan> the
Trunkes Cannot mount the Ordinance fittingly
butt that they will lye <doubtful type="u">too</doubtful> neere the Portlase
or Gunwall Then we make a false decke 
for soe much as the Peece will require for her
Traversinge to raise it higher, This we call
a bedd Alsoe in the Carriage of the Peece
That Plancke <expan type="wch">which</expan> lyes lowermost next the
Carriage vnder the <doubtful type="u">B</doubtful>reeck of the Peece 
Whereon the loynes do lye is called the Bedd. 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="belay(v)">to Belay</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to make fast anie runninge Rope when it
is halde as much as you would As the 
Hallyiards when you hoise a yard or the sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>
or Tack<expan type="es">es</expan> &amp; soe that it cannot Runn forth 
againe till it be loosed:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="12">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bend(n)">A Bend</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the outwardmost tymber on the shipps side and
is alsoe called a wale These are the Cheife 
strength of the shipps sides to <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Fluttockes
and knees of the Teames are boulted and they
are called by the name of first Second &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> beginning
wth that next the water:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="bend(v)">to Bend or Bent</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is taken in the Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> sense as when the 
Shancke of the Anchor is <expan type="wth">with</expan> overmuch swayinge
crooked wee saye it is bent But it is otherwise
vsed As when they saye is the Cabell bent 
That is when it is seized and made fast to
the ringe of the Anchor Vnbend the <correction type="d" deleted="Anchor"></correction>
Cabell That is vnbende it <expan type="wch">which</expan> we are C<expan type="">ommon</expan>ly 
when wee make accompt to be longe at sea before
wee come into Harbo<expan type="+r">r</expan> To bend two Cabells 
or Ropes together, that is to tye them together
wth a knott and soe make their owne end<expan type="es">es</expan> fast 
vpon them selves: This is not soe sure as spliceing
two Ropes together but it is sooner done and 
most Comonly vsed when wee meane to take 
them asunder againe as when a warpe or anie 
rope is too short for the p<expan type="+r">re</expan>sent vse:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="berth(n)">a Berth</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Conveinent distance &amp; Roome to Moore 
a shipp in Alsoe when they would goe cleere off
a Poynt or a Rocke, They saye take a good Berth
That is goe a pretty distance of to seabord of it 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="berthing(n)">Bearthing</form>
<xpln lang="en">They call the raysinge or bringinge vpp of 
a shipps side the Bearthinge of her As they
saye a Clincher hath her sides Bearthed vpp
before anie Beames be putt into her;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="13">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bight(n)">a Bight</form>
<xpln lang="en">By a Bight is meant anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of a Rope as it is
taken Compasinge. As when we cannot or meane 
not to take th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nde in hande, (be it of a Cabell or 
other smalle Rope beinge Quoyled vpp) we saye
give mee the Bight or hold by the Bight That is by
one of the fakes <expan type="wch">which</expan> lyes roled vpp one over an 
other:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="bilge(n)" lexeme="bulge(n)">Bildge or
Buldg</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Bildge of the shipp is the bredth of the floore
whereon the shipp doth rest when she is aground
A shipp is Bilged, That is when she stickes on
a Rocke, or an Anchor or the like and breakes
anie of her tymbers or Planckes there and
soe spring<expan type="es">es</expan> a leake:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bilge water(n)">Bildge-water</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the water <expan type="wch">which</expan> by reason of the shipps 
breadth &amp; depth lies in the Bildg and cannott 
come to the walle And therefore the Flemish
shipps <expan type="wch">which</expan> have generally broader &amp; lower 
floores then our shipps have besid<expan type="es">es</expan> the ordinarie 
Pumps at the Mayne Mast two Bildg Pumps/ and
these Pumpes are Co<expan type="mo+_">mmo</expan> placed forwards
on by the Bitts: When the Shipp is trymmed
most a Head, then Shee houldes most Bildg.
</xpln>
<lemenote>
From "and these Pumpes are" to the end are absent from British Library Sloane MS 207 
but found in British Library Additional MS 21571, fol. 19v.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bittakle(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan>Bittakell</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Close Cubbord placed in the Steeridg 
before the Whipp or Tyller whereon the Com&amp;
passe doth stande <expan type="wch">which</expan> is not fastende together
wth Iron nayles, butt woodden pinnes because
the Iron would drawe the Compasse soe that
it would never stande true These are soe to
be Contrived That they maye carry a C<expan type="">an</expan>ddell
or light in them to give light to the Compasse 
soe as they disperse not anie light nor gett to 
lett anie bee seene about the shipp:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="14">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bitter(n)">a Bitter</form>
<xpln lexeme="bitter">Is noe more but a turne of the Cabell about the
Bitt<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> is vsed in this kinde: when we come to
Anchor in anie greate tyde or Current, or
winde especially in deepe water after the Cabell
is runn out a Convenient waye we take aturne
wch is about the bitt<expan type="es">es</expan> that wee may by little &amp; little
veere it out att ease for otherwise if a stopper should
chance to faile, the Cabell would runn all out or
(as the Phrase is) End for End, Now the turne of
the Cabell is called a Bitter And when the shipp
is by this meanes stopped, we saye the shipp is 
brought vpp to a Bitter:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bitter end(n)">Bitter end</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that end of the Cabell <expan type="wch">which</expan> is vsed to be wthin
borde still att the Shipp, when the shipp rid<expan type="es">es</expan> at an
Anchor: Soe that vpon occation when they would 
have that end bent to the Anchor they say bend
to the Bitter end:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"> 
<form lexeme="bitt(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Bitts</form>
<xpln lang="en">are the two mayne square peeces of tymber
wch stand piller wise and are Comonly placed 
abast the manger in the loose of the ship and 
for noe other vse but to make fast and as it were
to belage the Cabell vnto, when wee ride att an 
Anchor The lower <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of them is fastened in hold to
the riders, but the middle parte doth beare for their
better strength And are bolted in greate shipps
to two beames <expan type="wch">which</expan> Crosse to the Bowes of the shipp
And therefore in sometymes in extraordinary
stormes wee are fayne to make fast the Cabell
to the Mayne Mast for the better releiveinge 
the Bitt<expan type="es">es</expan> and safetie of the Bowes <expan type="wch">which</expan> have
in great Roadsteds are violently torne from 
the after <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipp:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="15">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="block(n)"">Blockes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale woodden thing<expan type="es">es</expan> wherein all the runninge
doe runne and are called accordinge to the names
of the Ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> they serve, as the Sheete
blocke &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> Blocke and Blocke, That is when yo<expan type="+u">u</expan> hoyse
blockes: If they meete you can hoyse noe more 
as in hoysinge the Topsails or else in hoiseinge in
good<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Tackles:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="blow(v)">Blowe</form>
<xpln lexeme="blow">Every man knowes when the wynde blowes
But there are some speeches vsed att Sea <expan type="wch">which</expan> are
not generally understood as the winde blowes home
or blowes through, that is when the winde doth not
cease or growe les, till it come past the place
Alsoe blowe thorugh is some tymes vsed 
when they thinke the winde wilbe soe greate that 
it will blowe asunder the sayles In some places
as I have seene at St. Ecrimis in Barbarie the
winde beinge right of the sea and afresh gale
asmuch as wee could beare our Topsayles when
we came wthin les then a league of the Rode we
had a little or noe winde att all, and it is infall&shy;
iblie euer soe, The naturall cause whereof I
could neuer finde out. For it cannot be the
height of the land since all that Baye is lowe
lande, only the Capp <expan type="wch">which</expan> is not very high 
And we knowe that at the Peekes of Tennerif
and Fiall <expan type="wch">which</expan> are the highest lands in the world
it doth the Contrary. Nor cann it be the heate
of the land <expan type="wch">which</expan> should duller the winde for this
happens there in the winter alsoe, and besid<expan type="es">es</expan>
wee see the Contrary in hotter Cuntries 
when a winde increases soe much that they 
cannot beare anie Toppsayles then they vse
to saye That they were blowne into their Courses
</page>

<page no="16">
That is could only have out those sailes It
blowes hard Fresh stiffe high all word<expan type="es">es</expan> easiely
knowne when they expresse an extraordinarie 
winde they saye it will blowe the sayle out of 
the Boltropes: If the touchhole of a peece bee
guld much powder will Flame out and that is
alsoe called Blowinge:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="buff(a)" lexeme="buff-headed(a)">Buffe or 
Buffeheaded</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when a shipp hath but smale Racke 
forward<expan type="es">es</expan> on and is buyltt <expan type="wth">with</expan> her stem as it
were vpright <expan type="wch">which</expan> will make her seeme as if 
she had a broad face like a Venetian Owle 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="boat(n)">Boate</form>
<xpln lexeme="boat">The Boate belonginge to a shipp is either
called the Shipps Boate or the Long boate
and th<doubtful type="u">is</doubtful> is ever intended to be able to carry
forth and wey her Sheate Anchor Other smaller
boat<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> they Carry for lightnesse to hoise in &amp;
out quicklie are called Skyffes or Shallops 
accordinge to their forme: A good Longe-boate
will live in anie growne sea if the water bee
sometymes freed except the Sea breake very
much. The Rope by <expan type="wch">which</expan> it is towed as the shippes
Stearne is called the Boaterope To <expan type="wch">which</expan> to keepe
the boate from Sheeringe wee add an other
wch we call a gestrope, we doe alsoe (to save the
Bowes of the Boate) <expan type="wch">which</expan> would be torren out 
wth the twitches <expan type="wch">which</expan> the shipp vnder sayle 
would owe <emend err=")"></emend> vse to Swite her: That is to make
fast a rope round by the Gunnwale, and to that
make fast the Boaterope, free the Boate
That is flinge out the water: Man the 
Boate: That is some men goe to Rowe the
</page>

<page no="17">
Boate, The Boate Ginge: That is those wch
vse to Rowe in the Boate, <expan type="wch">which</expan> are the Cockson 
and his Ginge to whome the Charge of the Boate
belong<expan type="es">es</expan> Fend the boate, That is save her from 
beatinge against the shipps sides Wynd the boate
That is bringe her head an other waye: a A Bould
Boate: That is on that will endure a rough sea
well A shipps boate is the very Moddell of ashipp
and is buylt <expan type="wth">with</expan> <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> in all thing<expan type="es">es</expan> answerable
vnto those <expan type="wch">which</expan> a shipp requires both for saylinge
and beareinge asaile and they beare the same
names as doe all the <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of a shipp vnder 
water: as Rake. Rum. Stem. Sterne. Bowe 
Bildge &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bolt(n)">Bolt or Bolts</form>
<xpln lexeme="bolt(n)">Are Iron pinnes belonginge both to the
buildinge and riginge of a shipp of <expan type="wch">which</expan> there
are divers kindes as Ring bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> <emend err="("></emend>wch are of infinite
necessarie vse both for the bringinge to of
the Plankes and wales of the shipp as alsoe
the Cheife thing<expan type="es">es</expan> wherervnto wee fasten the
Tackles and breechinges of the greate Ordinance
Driue bolts are longe bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> to drive out other
bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> or T<damage></damage> Sett bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> vsed in the building
fore fireinge the planckes and other workes
close together Sharpe Bolts <expan type="wch">which</expan> are sharpned
att one end and Ragged that they may not be 
drawne out Clench bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are Clenched wth
a Revettinge hammer; to p<expan type="+r">re</expan>vent draweinge out
Forlock boltes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made at thend <expan type="wth">with</expan> an 
eye wherevnto into aforelocke of Iron is driven
over a ringe to keepe it fast from Starkinge
backe Fender Bolts <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made <expan type="wth">with</expan> longe
</page>

<page no="18">
head and beate <emend err="beate"></emend>into the outward most bend of 
the shipp to save the shipps side if an other shipp
should lye abord her Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> are many tymes called
accordinge to the place wherevnto they are vsed, as 
Chayne bolt. Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> for Carriages and the like: The
vse of them are soe greate that wthout them a shipp
cannot be built stronge for they bynd togeather all
the tymbers knees and the like <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe strengthen
the shipp, /
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bolt rope(n)">a Bolt Rope</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the rope vnto <expan type="wch">which</expan> the sayle is sowed or 
made fast it is of 3 strond rope and greate and not
twisted soe hard as others; of purpose to be more 
plyant to the sayle , as alsoe that they may sewe 
the sayle into it the better;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bolt sprit(n)">Bolt spritt</form>
<xpln lang="en">To this is fastened all the stayes, <expan type="wch">which</expan> belonge
to the foremast and foretoppmast and fore
toppgallant &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> their bowlinge Tackes
besid<expan type="es">es</expan> the rigginge <expan type="wch">which</expan> belong<expan type="es">es</expan> to the <expan type="p-_">par</expan>ticuler
sailes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are only two vizt <xref>Spritt sayle</xref> and 
<xref>Sprittsaile toppsaile</xref> If a shipp spend her
boltspritt, or as the more proper speech 
is If the Boltspritt dropp by the board the 
foremast will quicklie followe If it be arough
Sea especially if you goe by a wynd: This 
beares the same propor<expan type="">cion</expan> for length
and bignesse as the fore Mast doth;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="19">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bonnet(n)">a Bonnett</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is belonginge to an other sayle but is <expan type="">common</expan>ly
vsed <expan type="wth">with</expan> none but the Meysen, Mayne, &amp; foremast
sayles and the spritt sayles: I have seene (but
very rare) a toppsayle Bonnett and hold it
very vsefull in an easie gale Quarter wynd<expan type="es">es</expan>
or before a wynd; This is Comonly a third <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
as deepe as the sayle it belong<expan type="es">es</expan> to; There is
noe certayne propor<expan type="">cion</expan>; for some will make
the mayne saile soe deepe That <expan type="wth">with</expan> showle
Bonnett they will Clothe all the Mast wthout
a drabler, Others will make the Mayne 
sayle showler, That they may in foule weather
beare it safer and then the Bonnett wilbe
the dee<expan type="p-_">per</expan> Lase on the Bonnett or bringe to the
Bonnett is putt it to the Course Laseinge is
there very pro<expan type="p-_">per</expan> because it is made fast
wth latchett<expan type="es">es</expan> into the Eylett holes of the saile
Note that when we doe speke of the saile
in anie Correspondence to the Bonnett wee
call it the Course and not the saile As wee
saye when a sheepe hath those sayles out Course
and Bonnett, and fore saile Bonnett: shake 
of the Bonnet, That is take it off
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="boom(n)">A Boome</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is alonge Pole <expan type="wch">which</expan> we vse Commonly to 
spread out the Clewe of the Studdinge 
saile <expan type="wth">with</expan> yet sometymes wee boome out the
Clewe of the Maynesaile and foresaile to 
spread them out soe much the broader to receive
more winde. When wee saye a shepe comes 
Boominge toward<expan type="es">es</expan> vs; It is asmuch as to saye
she comes <expan type="wth">with</expan> all Saile she cann make
Note that Boominge of sailes is neuer vsed
butt in quarter wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> or afore a wynde; 
</page>

<page no="20"> 
For by a wynd studdinge sailes: and boomeinge the 
sayles is not vsefull In comeing into the harbor
wher the Channell is narrow and Crooked and
the land aboute it overflowne they vse to sett
Poules <expan type="wth">with</expan> bushes or Baskett<expan type="es">es</expan> at the topp to
direct howe men should direct the Steere 
alonge the Channell by them And there are
alsoe in many places called Boomes, but in
some others they are called Beacons;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="board(n)" lexeme="abord(adv)">Bord or 
abord</form>
<xpln lang="en">By this is not only ment dealeboard<expan type="es">es</expan> or the
like but otherwise when we vse the word 
Aborde at sea, It is as much as to saye wthin 
the shipp: To goe aborde; that is to goe into
the shipp Bringe the Tack Close a bord: That
is pull downe the Tack vnto the Chestree or
the Gunnwall, Borde &amp; Bord That is when 
two shipps touch each other The weather
bord That is as much as to saye To wyndward
To make a board or as we vse to saye to bord it
vpp to a place, Is to turne to wyndward, wch
wee doe by standinge sometymes the one waye
sometymes the other for the gaginge aplace 
to wyndward In <expan type="wch">which</expan> note That the farther 
ye stand off vpon one poynt of the Compasse
the better Borde ye shall make, And it is better
makeinge longe Bord<expan type="es">es</expan> then short bord<expan type="es">es</expan> if you 
stand a greate waye off before you tacke or 
turne. A short bord is when you stand off but
a little A good bord is when wee have gott vpp
much to wyndward For sometymes wee take 
</page>

<page no="21">
a greate deale of paynes &amp; gett little either
by occation of a Current or tyde that may take
her on the nether bowe, or by reason of a head
sea, <expan type="wch">which</expan> may drie her to the Leeward and 
hinder her waye or for that the shipp may bee
a Leeward shipp: Sometymes againe when it
is a smothe sea a Current vnder the Lee bowe
and a good shipp by a wynd she will gett a poynt
or two more in the wind<expan type="es">es</expan> then wee expect Here
Note that a Crosse saile shipp in the sea cannot
make her waye neere then 6 poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> vnlesse
there be tyde or Current <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe sett to wynd&shy;
ward Withinbord; without bord, ouerbord,
By the bord all termes obvious to Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> sense
To leave a Land on backbord is to leave it 
afterne or behinde For the Backbord is that
wch in boat<expan type="es">es</expan> or Skyffes wee leane our Back<expan type="es">es</expan> 
againste In seight to Bord a shipp is to bringe -- 
the shipp to touch the other where you must Note
the advantages or disadvantages in every place
in Bordinge, And knowe that when two 
shipps feight the Defen<expan type="">dant</expan> may Chuse whether
you shall bord him or noe, or but only in the quarter
wch is a badd place to Bord for men can worst
enter there, In respect it is thee highest place
of the shipps hull And for that there is only
the Misen shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> to enter by. As alsoe for
that shipps are hottest there And men beinge
entered there cann doe little good and are 
easilie scowered off <expan type="wth">with</expan> Murderrers from the
Close feight; The best bordinge for enteringe
is if you cann, to Bord on the Bowe for then 
you may quickly bringe all yo<expan type="+r">ur</expan> broad side to 
the Butt: The greatest advantage is for 
vse of Ordinance is, to Board a thwart his 
</page>

<page no="22">
hause for then you may vse all yo<expan type="+r">ur</expan> Ordinance on
one side and she can only vse her Chase and her
prowe peeces:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bow(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Bowe</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipp <expan type="wch">which</expan> is broadest before
and begins from the loose till it come Compassinge
a bout toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the Steme the propor<expan type="">cion</expan>inge
of this <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te is of greate importance for the 
sayleinge of the shipp For this first breakes
off the Sea, and is that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te <expan type="wch">which</expan> beares all the 
shipp forward<expan type="es">es</expan> on <expan type="wch">which</expan> is in a manner all the 
bearinge of the shipp: If the Bowe be to broad
the shipp will not passe easilie through the Sea
but carry a greate deale of dead water before her
If it be to leane or Thynne she will pitch or beate
mightily into a hollowe Sea for want of Sea breadth
to beare her vpp Soe that there must be a 
discreete meanes betwixt both these, The shapeinge
of this <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te doth much importe the shipps goeinge
by a wynd: yet I have seene shipps of both sort<expan type="es">es</expan>
goe well by a wynd but most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly, Those that
have good Bould bowes: And yet it is certayne
That ashipps waye after one is of more importance
for her sayleinge by a wynd: A bould bowe is a 
Broad Round Bowe a leane Bowe is a narrowe
thynn bowe /
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bowling(n)">Bowling</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is fastened to the Leech or out <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
of the one side of the sayle the vse whereof is
to make the saile stand the Closer or sharper
by a wynd: The Auntient<expan type="es">es</expan> as it is reported did
ever saile before winde The reason whereof I 
conceave to be because they had not the knowledge
of this Rope It is fastened by 2.3.4 or more <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> 
</page>

<page no="23">
to the sayle <expan type="wch">which</expan> they call the Bowlinge Bridle 
only the Misen Bowlinge is fasten to the lower
end of the yard This Rope belong<expan type="es">es</expan> to all sailes
exceptinge Spritt saile anie Spritt saile 
Toppsaile <expan type="wch">which</expan> have noe place to hale a Bowlinge
forward by And therfore these sayles cannot
be vsed close by a wynd Sharpe the mayne Bow&shy;
linge sett taught the Blowinge Hale vp the 
Bowlinge all there are to make it be puld vpp
harder or more properly be held more foreward
on Ease <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Bowlinge: Check or Com vp the Bowling
that is lett it be more slacke;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bower(n)">a Bower</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is anie Anchore <expan type="wch">which</expan> shipps doe vsually carry
att the Bowe and from thence hath it<expan type="es">es</expan> name
for our greatest Anchore wee Carry in holde
and for better Sowadge some tymes vnstocke
them:/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bouse(v)">Bouse</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a word they vse when they would have men
pull together And is cheifely vsed by the 
Gunners when they would have them hale vpon their
tackles to thrift a peece out at a Parte they
will Cry Bouse hoa: That is pull more vpon 
the Tackle And then they knowe to pull all 
together And soe when there is occasion to 
pull more on our tackle then the other they
will saye Bouse vpon that Tackle:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="brackets(n)">Bracketts</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are certayne little peeces in the nature of
knees <expan type="wch">which</expan> belonge to the supportinge of 
Galleries or Shipps head<expan type="es">es</expan>:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="24"> 
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="brace(n)">Brases</form>
<xpln lang="en">These Ropes doe belonge to all the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> excepting
the Misenyard They have a pendant <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
seized to the yard Armes <emend err="("></emend>for to every yarde
belong<expan type="es">es</expan> two Braces, and att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of the 
Pendant a blocke is seised through <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Rope 
is Reeved <expan type="wch">which</expan> they call the Brace The vse 
whereof is to square the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> and travers the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> Square the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> or rights That is to make
it stand iust <expan type="wth">with</expan> Crosse the shipp to make right
angles <expan type="wth">with</expan> the length of the shipp All the Braces
doe come afterward<expan type="es">es</expan> on as the Maine Brace to
the Poope. The Maine toppsaile Brace to the 
Missen topp and soe to the Maine shroud<expan type="es">es</expan>: The 
fore and foretopsaile Braces downe by the Maine
and Maine toppsaile stayes and soe of the rest
The Missen Bowlinge doth serve for a Brace
to that yard but the Crosseiackbraces are brought
forward to the Mayne shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> when wee goe 
close by the winde /
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="brail(n)">Brailes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale ropes reeved through blockes
wch are seised on either side the Tyes some
smale distance off vpon the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> and soe come
downe before the saile and are fastened to the
Creengles at the Skyrte of the sayle The vse 
whereof is to hale vpp the of the Bunt of the saile when 
wee doe farthell our sailes a Crosse <expan type="wch">which</expan> are in this
Comodious for a man of warre That he may 
instantly make vp his sailes and lett them fall if in
feight he should fall a sterne For Note that in
feight we desire to vse as fewe sailes as we can
both for the trouble in tryminge them, for 
saveinge our sailes for hinderinge our feight and
for a voydinge of fyre <expan type="wch">which</expan> might hath in them
</page>

<page no="25"> 
therefore when we saye we will stripe our selves
into our feightinge sayles It is ment that we
have only the Misen Maynetoppsailes and 
fore saile <expan type="wth">with</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> sailes a Shipp will worke 
every waye these Brailes vse only belonge to 
the Two Courses and to the Missen hale vpp
the Brailes and braile vp the saile is all on
when Marchant men will seeme to brave aman
of warre if he Chase them they will braile vpp
their sailes <expan type="wch">which</expan> is as much as to make asigne
they will feight <expan type="wth">with</expan> them:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="breech(n)" lexeme="breeching(n)"">Breech 
Breechinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Breech is the aftermost <expan type="p-
_">par</expan>the of the
Gunne from the Touchhole <expan type="wch">which</expan> is in Brasse 
Ordinance ever allowed to be as thicke as the
Diameter of Bullett And those Ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are
bigger then the tackles that doe make or lace
fast the Ordinance to the shipps sides beinge
brought aboute the Breech of the Peece are
called Breechinge, These wee doe not vse in
feight but at Sea and cheifely in foule weather:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="breeze(n)">a Breice</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a wynde <expan type="wch">which</expan> blowes out of the Sea and doth
daylie in seasonable faire weather keepe his 
Course begininge lightly about ix in the morninge
and lastinge till <correction type="d" deletetd= "a little"></correction> it be wthin little of night
wee doe not Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly call all wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> that blowe off
the Sea vpon anie Coaste Breeses vnles it be 
there where this Course is certaine, or rarely
mysses, but in Stormes and foule weather: As
fore examples here on our Coast the wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> are 
neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> certayne But on the Coast of Barbary and
other places more Southerly they are certayne
to have the wynd of the land all night and of
</page>

<page no="26"> 
the Sea all daye This Breeice is alsoe called
a Sea turne,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="breastfast(n)">A Brestfast</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is fastened to some <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipp
forward on and soe doth holde fast the shipps head
to a wharfe or anie thinge else and a Stern Fast
is the same for a Sterne,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="broom(v)">Broominge</form> 
<xpln lang="en">Is when a shipp is brought aground or on 
the Carreens to be tr<expan type="y+_m+_e+_">ymme</expan>d That is to bee
made Cleane. They bringe off the old weed<expan type="es">es</expan>
or Stuffe <expan type="wch">which</expan> hath gathered filth <expan type="wch">which</expan> they 
vsually doe either <expan type="wth">with</expan> reedes, Broome old Ropes
or the like:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="budge barrel(n)">a Budg Barrell</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a little Barrell not altogeather soe bigge
as a barrell <expan type="wch">which</expan> hath an hundred weight of 
powder, and hath a purse of leather made 
att the head of it: <expan type="wch">which</expan> is to shutt over the Powder
to keepe it from fyreinge: we vse to laye Ordinance
wth this in the Harbo<expan type="+r">r</expan> for healths and the like, but
att Sea in feight not if we can gett Carthrages <expan type="w+th">ith</expan>out
wch this is the safest waye There are alsoe 
Latin Budg barrells <expan type="wch">which</expan> are the best:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bulk(n)">Bulke</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Bulke of a shipp is her whole Content
in holde, as to saye she is a shipp of a greate
Bulke that is will stow much good<expan type="es">es</expan> Sometymes
</page>

<page no="27">
it is taken for the Marchant<expan type="es">es</expan> good<expan type="es">es</expan> As when
they saye lett our stocke goe in Bulke together
To Breake Bulke is as muchas to saye to
open the holde and sell or <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te the good<expan type="es">es</expan> in 
hold: As the Indie shipps maye sell anie 
good<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> they have betwixt the deckes. But
they must not breake Bulke till they have order
from the Companie, That is they must not
open the Holde to medle <expan type="wth">with</expan> anie Marchan&shy;
dize therein Conteyned:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bulkhead(n)">Bulk-head</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is generally anie division <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made Crosse
the shipp <expan type="wth">with</expan> bord<expan type="es">es</expan> whereby one Roome is
divided from an other. As the Bulkhead 
of the Cabin. The Bulkhead of the decke 
The Bulkhead of the Breadroome Gunnroome
or the like:/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="bunt(n)">Bunt</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Bunt of asaile is as it were in Compa&shy;
rison of the wind, the Codd of the Nett <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
receives all the fishe, and may aswell be 
called the very bagg of the saile, and therefore
wee give a Bunt to all sailes to the intent
they may receive much winde <expan type="wch">which</expan> is the 
Anima sensitua of a shipp: If a shipp have
to much Bunt it will hange to much to Lee&shy;
ward and as they call it hold much Leeward
winde <expan type="wch">which</expan> will hinder the shipps saileinge
especially by the winde If it have to little either
it will not hold winde enough and soe not give
the shipp sufficient waye: The difference is
rather <expan type="p-_">per</expan>ceived in topp sailes then the other 
for Courses are Cutt square or at least wth
allowance of small Compasse:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="buntline(n)">Bunt Lynes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale lyne <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast to the bottome
</page>

<page no="28"> 
of the saile in the midle <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Boltrope to
a Creengle and soe reeved through asmale 
blocke seised to the yard. The vse whereof is to
Tryse vpp the Bunt of the saile for the better
farthellinge and makeinge vpp of the sailes 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="buoy(n)">A Bouy</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that peece of wood Barrell or the like wch
float<expan type="es">es</expan> right over the Anchor and is made 
fast by the Buoy rope vnto the Flooke The 
vse whereof is not only to take knowledge where
the Anchor is: But alsoe to weight the Anchor
wth the Boate <expan type="wch">which</expan> is sooner done then to weigh
it <expan type="wth">with</expan> the shipp Streame the Buoy That
is before they lett the Anchor fall whilst the 
shipp hath waye They putt the Buoy into the
water soe that the Buoy Rope may be stretched
out straight and then the Anchor will fall Cleere
from entanglinge it selfe in the Buoy Rope
Buoyant Is anie thinge that is floateing or apt
to floate To Buoy vpp a Cabell, That is to
make fast anie peece of floatinge wood Barrell
or the like to the Cabell somewhat neere to 
the Anchor That the Cabell may not touch
the ground This wee vse in foule ground<expan type="es">es</expan> where
wee feare the Cuttinge of our Cabells. There
are Buoyes alsoe <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe not belonge to shipps
And those are left att an Anchor in the Sea
to showe where anie danger is of sand<expan type="es">es</expan> or 
Rockes These are specially most needefull 
to be vsed where the sand<expan type="es">es</expan> doe vse to alter or
where wee can have noe fittinge landmarke;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="butt(n)">A Butt</form>
<xpln lang="en">By this word taken indifferently is meant
avessell or Caske, as a Butt of wine &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> But
</page>

<page no="29">
in sea language thus A Butt is properly
th<expan type="">e</expan> end of a Plancke ioyninge to an other of 
the outward side of a shipp vnderwater To 
springe a Butt that is when a Plancke is 
loose att one end and therefore the Boult in
most great shipps, all the Butthead<expan type="es">es</expan>, By 
butthead<expan type="es">es</expan> is ment the end<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Plancke;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="buttock(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Buttock:/</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Buttocke of a shipp is the Breadth of
the shipp right asterne from the Tack vpward<expan type="es">es</expan>
and therefore acordinge as she is buylt broad
or narrowe att the Transome or layinge out
of the Sterne we say the shipp hath a broad
or Narrow Buttock:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cable(n)">a Cabell</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a three strand Rope intended to be sufficient for
a shipp to Ride by att an Anchor for otherwise it is 
Counted but a hawser: For a greate shipps hawser
will make a little shipps Cabell Cabells have severall
appella<expan type="c+_o+_n+_s+_">cions</expan> as the Anchore And are called the 
First Second or third as they growe in greatnesse
begininge <expan type="wth">with</expan> the least till it come to Sheate Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>
Cabell: the best Cabells are those <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made
Of the whitest stuffe: and therefore Straights
Cabells are the best: The next the Flemmish and
Russia; the last ours The makeinge of a Cabell is 
termed the layinge, as to saye this Cabell was 
well laid: Serue the Cabell or Plate the Cabell
is to binde some olde ropes Clout<expan type="es">es</expan> or the like to save
it from gawlinge in the house Splice a Cabell is
to fasten two Cabells togeather <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Splice: Coile
a Cabell is to laye it vpp in Rowles one a bove an
other Cable tire is the Cabell soe layd vp in Roules
Pay more Cabell That is when they Carry out 
an Anchor and Cabell in the Boate to turne
over into the sea some Cabell that the Boate
may Rowe the Easier and the Cabell be slacke
in the water: Pay Cheap. That is fling it over 
the water a pace Veere more Cabell, That is
lett more goe out Shott of Cabell vide <xref>shott</xref>:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="caburn(n)">Caburnes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale lyne made of spunn yarne, to b<expan type="i+_d+_">ind</expan>d 
the Cabells or to make a Bend of two Cabells
or to sease the wyndinge tackles or the like;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form number="1" lexeme="calm(n)" lexeme="becalm(v)">a Calme &amp;
Becalme</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when at sea wee have not anie wynde: And
then wee add to it these Epithitons: flatt dead 
or Starke Calme: A Calme is more troublesome
to a Seafareinge man then a storme if he have
a stronge shipp and Searoome enough In some
</page>

<page no="31">
places (as in the Str<correction type="i">a</correction>ight<expan type="es">es</expan>) when it is extraordinary
greate storm <expan type="wth">with</expan> much wind<expan type="es">es</expan> and a wroughte
sea on the suddaine there wilbe noe winde but
a flatt Calme yett an extraordinarie billowe, wch
is wondrous troublesome and dangerous, for then
haveinge noe vse for sayle to helpe her stedy 
on aside, the greate sea will make a shipp roule
soe that unlesse she be a very fast shipp in the 
water she wilbe in danger to rowle her Maste by
the board or her selfe vnder water Becalminge is
when anie thinge takes a way the winde from 
another As when one shipp is vnder the Lee of an 
other, The Windermoste shipp doth becalme the 
Leeward Mast; Alsoe when wee are neere the 
land <expan type="wch">which</expan> keepes the winde from vs, we saye 
it doth becalme vs:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="camber(v)" lexeme="cambering(a)">Camber or 
Camberinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">We saye a decke lyes Camberinge when it is
higher in the middle then att either end and soe
doth not lye vpon aright line This word is most
Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly applied to the shipps Keele Beames 
and other Roundinge Peeces in the shipps frame
Camber keeled, is when the Keele is bent in the 
middle vpward<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> happens many tymes by 
a shipp vneven lyinge vpon the ground when 
either her aftermost <expan type="p-_">par</expan>,te or foremost doth not
touch: But the most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> cause and cheife
reason of Camberinge in great and longe shipps
is the sharpnesse of the hull afore &amp; abast, and
the fullnesse of the floore a myde shipps <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
haveinge noe breadth to beare vpon the water
is harder to sinke then both end<expan type="es">es</expan> before &amp; abast 
wch by reason of the sharpenesse, and greate
weight over head, ther Rakes <expan type="wch">which</expan> over hang<expan type="es">es</expan>
the grounds worke, sinkes faster into the water,
and soe their weight forces the Keele &amp; whole
worke in the Mydshipps to weigh vpward<expan type="es">es</expan> wch
is <expan type="y+e">the</expan> mayne reason of the shipps Camberinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="32">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cap(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Cap:</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the square peece of tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> is putt over the
head of anie Mast, <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Round hole for to receive
into it the Topmast, or flagstaffe by <expan type="wch">which</expan> the
Toppmast is kept steddie: For is the head of 
the Mayne Mat be too shorte soe that the Capp
stand to neere the heele or bottome of the Top&shy;
Mast: The Topmast will neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> steddie 
and beside the weight of the Topmast will strayne
the head of the Mayne Mast soe much that
it wilbe in Danger to spend it, or beare it by the
bord Euery Mast hath a Capp, if it Carry an&shy;
other, or but a Flagstaffe at the topp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cap-square(n)">Cap-squares</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the broad peeces of Iron <expan type="wch">which</expan> belonge to 
either side of the Carriages of a peece of 
Ordinance to locke over the truniyons of the 
Peece over <expan type="wch">which</expan> they are made fast in an 
Iron Pinne <expan type="wth">with</expan> afore lock The vse whereof is 
to keepe the Peece from flyinge out of the
Carryadge when it is shott off the Mouth of it
lyinge very Lowe or as the Phrase is vnder Mettle;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="capstan(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan>Capstaine</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are two kind<expan type="es">es</expan> of Capstaines: The one
called the Capstayne or the Mayne Capstayne
and is that peece of tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> is over placed
right vpp and downe abast the Mayne Mast
the Foote standinge in a Stepe in the Lower
decke and the head beinge betwixt the two 
vpper deckes; the <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> are these: The foote 
the Spindell, the whellpes, <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Barrell &amp; the 
Holes For the Barres To <expan type="wch">which</expan> alsoe belonge
the Paule of Iron The vse of it is Cheifely 
to weigh our Anchore and generally to hoise
or strike downe topmast or to heave in anie
thinge of weight As ordinance and the 
</page>

<page no="33">
or indeede to strayne anie Rope that requires
greate force The second is a Geere Capstaine
wch is placed in the same manner betwixt the
Mayne and foremast<expan type="es">es</expan>. The vse whereof is 
cheifely to heave vpp the Geere Rope or else
to hold off by when wee heave the Anchor Att
the foote of this there are whelpes placed in alesser
propor<expan type="">cion</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> is to heave vpon the violl for 
the help of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Maine Captaine in weighinge 
a greate Anchor. Com vp the Capstaine That
is these <expan type="wch">which</expan> are at the Cappstaine must goe
backward<expan type="es">es</expan> and slacken the Rope or Cabell wch
they did heave not. In the same sense they alsoe
vse these word<expan type="es">es</expan> Launch at the Captaine; That
is to stopp <correction type="i">it</correction> with the Iron Paule <expan type="wch">which</expan> bearinge
against the whellpes keepe the Capstaine 
from turninge back :/
</xpln>
<lemenote>
The catchword on the previous page reads "like".
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="card(n)" lexeme="sea card(n)">Carde or
Sea Carde</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Geographicall descrip<expan type="">cion</expan> of Coast<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
the true distance height<expan type="es">es</expan> and Courses or 
wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> layd downe in it not describinge anie
Inland <expan type="wch">which</expan> belong<expan type="es">es</expan> to Mapps The differences
and vses of them will require a longe discourse,
and they are sett downe in most bookes wch
write of Naviga<expan type="">cion</expan> and therefore I leave
them to those bookes:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="careen(v)">Careene</form>
<xpln lang="en">Careeninge is the best waye of trymminge 
</page>

<page no="34">
a shipp vnderwater Both for that the Carpenter
maye stand vpon the Sea Fold<expan type="es">es</expan> most comodiouslie
to Caulke the Seames, or doe anie thinge that shalbe
requisit Alsoe for saveinge the ground tymbers
wch especially in shipps of greate Burthen and 
weight must need<expan type="es">es</expan> be much wronged though they 
be layne neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> soe stronge Besid<expan type="es">es</expan> it is amost 
necessarie trymminge for greate shipps wch
are either olde or weake built: And alsoe for anie
shipps <expan type="wch">which</expan> have but smale flore, and are built soe
sharpe vnderwater that they wilbe in <emend err="dange">danger</emend> of
overthrowinge when they shalbe brought aground
This Careeninge is to be done in harbor, where
the slower the tyde runes the better, and it is
most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed in such places where there are
noe deckes to tryme ashipp in Nor noe good places
to grave a shipp on, or else that it doth not ebb soe
much that a shipp may sewe drye: For the manner
of Careeninge it wilbe to longe and vnnecessarie 
to sett downe att the perticulers: In generall
it is this: They take out all or heave but little
of the Prouision Ballast, Ordinance or the like
in the shipp: And you must have alower shipp
by her, <expan type="wth">with</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> she must be haled downe on aside
and righted againe <expan type="wth">with</expan> tackles <correction type="i">yet</correction> <expan type="wth">with</expan> the weight
of Ballast a bove or belowe they doe effect the
Cheife force of the busines and soe neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> straine
the shipps Maste much Note that all shipps are
not of a like Condi<expan type="">cion</expan> to Careene for some shipps
wilbe very hard to come downe though they have 
noe ballast in: And those are Stemming<expan type="es">es</expan>, built
wth two standinge streakes These must have 
some weight vpon the streake to help them downe
And yet these will right them selves very Easily
and therefore neede not much in holde to helpe to
right them: Some (as our English built &amp; the like)
</page>

<page no="35">
will come downe easie and be hard to right -
And therefore wee keepe somewhat in all 
these to right them in holde &amp; haveinge noethinge
on the deckes some will come downe easilie and
right them selves well. Some will doe neither, soe
that there is not one waye for all, but as we see 
the Condi<expan type="">cion</expan> of the shipp wee fitt thing<expan type="es">es</expan> and 
worke accordingly: And kind<expan type="es">es</expan> of bringinge
the shipp over to lye on one side she beinge on
floate is called Careeninge though it be but 
a fewe streakes If a shipp lye downe much 
wth a saile They saye she sailes on the Careene/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="carriage(n)">a Carriage /</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that whereon we mount our Ordinance
The <expan type="p-_">ar</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> whereof are the two <xref>Cheekes</xref> The
<xref>Axeltrees</xref> the <xref>Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan></xref>, the <xref>Capsquares</xref> The
<xref>hookes</xref>, the <xref>fore lockes</xref>, the <xref><emend err="Truckes">Trunckes</emend></xref>, and the 
<xref>Lyne pinnes</xref>, vide every one of these in his pro<expan type="p-_">per</expan>
place: the fashion of these Carriages we 
vse at Sea, are much better, then those of the 
land yet the Venetions and divers others vse 
the other in their shippinge A Peece carryes
a shott well That is shee shoot<expan type="es">es</expan> farre &amp; Right wch
</page>

<page no="36">
is a signe that she is smooth and well mettled;/

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cartridge(n)">a Cartridg:</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a bagg made of Canuas <expan type="wch">which</expan> is reasonable good
beinge made vpon a former, The diameter 
whereof must be somewhat smaler then the Sillender
of the Peece and of such a length and depth as that
it shall conteyne iust soe much powder as is the
Charge of the Peece: This is wonderous necessarie
for our greate Orindance in feight both for the
speedie ladeinge our Ordinances and alsoe for 
saveinge the Pouder: <expan type="wch">which</expan> is in danger to be 
fyred, if in feight we should vse aladle: These 
Cartidgs are manie tymes made of paper
parchment or the like but are not soe good 
as the other There are alsoe other Cartidgs
or more pro<expan type="p-_">per</expan>ly they are to be called Cases 
for Cartidges <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made of Latine in wch
wee vse to putt These other Cartrig<expan type="es">es</expan> to bringe
a longst the shipp soe much the safer from 
the fyre till wee putt them into the Peeces
mouth, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is acare that in a fieght there 
cannot be to much diligence &amp; order vsed,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="carvel(n)">Caruells</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are vessells <expan type="wch">which</expan> goe <expan type="wth">with</expan> Missen sailes in steed
of Maine sailes: These will lye neere the
winde then Crosse sayles, but are not soe co<expan type="m+_o+_">mmo</expan>&shy;
dious to handle: we have herelittle vse of them
and therefore I speke not much;/
</xpln>
