<page no="37">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="case(n)">a Case</form> 
<xpln lang="en">Is C<expan type="">ommon</expan>ly made round of wood, hollowed &amp; fitt
for the bore of the Peece: By <expan type="wch">which</expan> most conveniently
we can putt murderinge Shott in to the Peece:
wee likewise vse bagg<expan type="es">es</expan> to the same purpose /
but they are apter to Catch hold by the 
waye in the flawes of the peeces: Alsoe some
call the Sheathinge of a shipp the Caseinge
of her
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="case shot(n)">a Case shott</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is anie kinde of old Iron stones Muskett
Bullets <correction type="i">or</correction> the like <expan type="wch">which</expan> we putt into Cases to
shoote out of our greate Ordinance These
are of greate vse and doe much execu<expan type="">cion</expan> 
amongst men that ply their smale shott 
when we come neere or lye Bord &amp; Bord;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="casket(n)">Casketts</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale string<expan type="es">es</expan> made of Sinnett flatt
They are made fast to the vpper end of the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> in little Ring<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> they call Grommetts
Their vse is to make fast the saile to the yard
when wee farthell it vpp The biggest &amp; longest
are placed iust in the middle of the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> 
betwixt the Tyes These doe make vp the Bunt
of the saile and are termed the Brest casketts:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="catharping(n)">Catherpings</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are a smalle Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> runnes in little block<expan type="es">es</expan>
like a Minum from one side of the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> to
the other neere the vp<expan type="p-_">per</expan> decke; The vse where
of is to force the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> taughter for the better
ease and safetie of the Mast in the Rowlinge
of the shipp They are only vsed to the Maine
shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> &amp; fore shroud<expan type="es">es</expan>: Alsoe att the settinge on
of the Buttockes of the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> they are vsed
But heare they are neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> made fast &amp; doe not
Runne in Blocks;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="38">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cat(n)">Catt</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Catt is a peece of tymber fastened aloft
right over the Hawse and hath att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd thereof
Two Sheevers wherein is Reeved a Rope wth
a Blocke wherevnto is fastened a greate hooke
of Iron after the manner of a double tackle:
The vse is to Trice vp the Anchor from the
hawse to the tope of the fore Castle where it 
is fastened <expan type="wth">with</expan> astopper: Catt the Anchor is
to Hitch the hooke in the ringe of the Anchor
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cat hole(n)">Catt holes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are two little holes a bove the Gunnroome
Ports a sterne for that this vse That when 
wee have occasion to heave the shipp a sterne
by a Sterne fast, They bringe in the Cabell or
hawser by them to the Capstaine. The sterne
Ports are not soe good because they lye not soe
Even <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Capstaine;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="caulk(v)">Caulke</form>
<xpln lang="en">Caulkinge is the dryveinge of Ockham Spunn&shy;
haire and the like into all seames, Rents and
treenailes throughout the shipp wthout the 
wch it is impossible for a shipp to be made tight
to Swyme and keepe out water;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="chase(v)">Chase</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a man of Warre doth followe anie shipp
out of his Course or else when anie other 
shipp doth alter her Course, soe as to vse all
the meanes they cann to fetch vpp and speke
wth an other shipp wee call that Chaseinge
and the shipp soe followed we call the Chase
As (meaning by her) we saye The Chase 
stand<expan type="es">es</expan> thus, or the Chase hath taken in her Top&shy;
sailes or the Chase is strooke or Hull &amp; There 
</page>

<page no="39">
is greate experience and Iudgment to be vsed
in Chaseinge For if Two men be equall 
Marriners and knowe how to saile and direct
his shipp yet if one be a Practique man of
warr and the other not, The man of warr
will doe much better The pretense in any
Chaseinge is to make the shortest waye of it
that they cann: <expan type="wch">which</expan> is by iudginge the Chases
Course and soe to shape yo<expan type="+r">urs</expan> that you may 
meete in the nearest Angles there is noe 
certayne rule for Chaseinge for wee must
many tymes be ruled by the Condi<expan type="">cion</expan> of our
shipp: As if the Chase clap close by, a wynd
it beinge a head Sea And the Man of Warre
shipp be a short shipp that beat<expan type="es">es</expan> off to the Sea 
and a Leeward shipp Then if he clap close by
awynd his shipp will make noe waye And
therefore he must goe alittle more large 
Though he Chase vnder the Lee of the other
In Chaseinge well alwayes covett to gett 
a wyndward in resspect that it is advantage
in feight and for that we cannot Bord a shipp
to Leeward, but som tymes (as if it be) toward<expan type="es">es</expan> 
night to keepe sight of a shipp or the like we
must be content to come vnder his Lee and 
gett as neere as wee cann: The Sterne Chase
That is when wee followe her right asterne
and she &amp; wee goe right vpon one poynt of
the Compasse To lye <expan type="wth">with</expan> her fore foole is the
neerest and shortest That is as y ou would say
to lye iust a Crosse her waye soe that both 
keepinge on their Courses they shall meete
att a certayne poynt: Chase peeces are those
wch lye right forward or right Aftward on: when
wee saye a shipp hath a good Chase in <emend err="differenly">differently</emend> 
it is ment of her Chase forward, And that is
when she is soe contrived that she can carry
</page>

<page no="40">
many peeces to shoote right forward for to the other
they ever vse to add the word Sternechase, The
Peeces of Ordinance <expan type="wch">which</expan> lye right foreward on are 
called Chase peeces;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="chafe(v)">Chafe</form>
<xpln lang="en">Or Chafinge is when anie Rope is galled or 
fretted As wee saye The Cabell is Chafed in the
Hawse or when a Rope rubbs against anie thinge
wch is not smooth and even, wee saye, it will Chafe
the Rope:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="chain(n)">Chaines</form>
<xpln lang="en">By nameinge the Chaynes of a shipp indifferently
is ment those Chaynes by <expan type="wch">which</expan> the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> are 
made fast on the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>: Alsoe those that 
belonge to the toppmast shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> are called 
Chaines: In feight wee vse to slinge our yard<expan type="es">es</expan> in 
<correction type="d" deleted="yard"></correction>, Chaynes for feare least the <doubtful type="u">B</doubtful>ill should bee
Cutt, and soe the yard pull downe: And these
Chaynes are called Slyng<expan type="es">es</expan>:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="chain wale(n)">Chaine wale</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a broader tymber sett on the out side of 
the shipp then the ordinary walls and is made
soe of purpose to spread out the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> wyder
that they may the better succour the Mast;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="chamber(n)">Chamber</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Charge mayde of Brasse or Iron <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee
vsed to putt in att the breech of anie Murderer
or <doubtful type="u">fowlie</doubtful> and conteynes iust soe much powder 
as is fitt to deliu<expan type="+r">er</expan> a waye the Murderinge or 
Case shott conteyned in that Peece Alsoe the
Chamber of a greate peece of whole Ordinance
is Counted soe farre, or soe much of it as 
doth Conteyne the whole Charge it hath;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="41">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="channel(n)">Channell</form>
<xpln lang="en">By Channell is ment the deepest <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of anie
Riuer in harbor<expan type="es">es</expan> mouth: As when we saye Steere
in the Channell, is ment the deepest <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the
ryuer, In paces where there are loose Sand<expan type="es">es</expan>
The Channells doe alter much accordinge to 
the extraordinarie wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> come and drive
the sand<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> the sea somtymes on one syde and
somtymes on an other: As when I came into 
Mammora the Channell laye in East South East,
and West North west <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Fiue poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> of the 
Compasse. Some tymes we call narrow Seas
Channells: as the English Channell betwixt
France and England And St. Georges 
Channell betwixt England and Ireland But 
beinge in those Seas If wee saye Steere into 
the Channell it is ment into the mid<expan type="es">es</expan> of the 
Sea: /
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="charge(v)">Charge</form>
<xpln lang="en">We use to saye Charge a Muskett, but load or
Lade a Peece of Ordinance A shipp of great
Charge is Co<expan type="m+_o+_">mmo</expan>nly ment by a shipp that drawes
much water: And sometymes for an vnwieldy
shipp that will not Veere and Steere for then 
shee is dangerous and Chargeable upon a shore
alsoe every mans office in a shipp is Called 
his Charge: /
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cheek(n)">Cheekes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are two peeces of Tymber, <expan type="wch">which</expan> are fitted
on each side of the Mast from beneath the honnd<expan type="es">es</expan>
to the upper end of the mast and they are 
made of Oake to Strengthen the Mast there
about<expan type="es">es</expan> both for the beareinge of the Topmast
and hoisinge the yarde. In these are the honnd<expan type="es">es</expan>
made for the tyes to runne in The Knees wch
fasten the Beakehead to the Bowe of the
</page>

<page no="42">
Shipp are called Cheekes alsoe the side of anie 
blockes are Called the Cheekes likewise the side
of the Carriages whereon the Trunnyons of the 
peece doe lye are called the Cheekes of the
Carriages:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="chess tree(n)">Chestrees</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the two smale peeces of tymber wth
whol<expan type="es">es</expan> in them in <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Mayne tacke doth 
Runne and to <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Tacke is halled downe: These
are placed a little abast the loofe of the shipp
the one on the side, The other on the other:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="choke(v)">Choake</form> 
<xpln lang="en">when a Runninge Rope stickes in a Blocke either
by flypinge betwixt the Cheeks &amp; the Sheever 
or by anie other occasion, <expan type="y+t">that</expan> anie thinge be 
gott about it; Or that I have a kynke soe <expan type="y+t">that</expan> 
it cannott runne, and be halde through it we
saye the Blocke is Choaked:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="clamp(n)">Clamps</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those tycke tymbers, <expan type="wch">which</expan> lye fore &amp; Aft
Close under the Beames of the first Orlap &amp;
doe beare them up at either End And are the
same that the Rising<expan type="es">es</expan> are att the other end 
vide <xref>Risings</xref>:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cleat(n)">a Cleate</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale wedge of wood fastened on the yard<expan type="es">es</expan>
to keepe anie Roapes from the stopinge by where 
that is fastened, There are alsoe diuers other
uses of it. As to keepe the Earinge of the 
sayle from slypinge of the yard:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="clew(n)">Clewe</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Clewe of a saile is the lower Corner
of a saile <expan type="wch">which</expan> reaches downe to the place 
</page>

<page no="43">
where the Tackes and Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> are made fast
to the saile And it is Counted that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te wch
comes Goareinge out from the square of the
sayle towarde the lower Corners: when 
a Saile is much goareinge then she hath
a greate Clewe: when a little goareinge 
then <correction type="i">she hath</correction> a little Clewe: when it cutt right square
then it hath noe Clewe: And yet that lower
Corner of the sayle shall retayne the lower
Clewe of the sayle. A ship A ship spread<expan type="es">es</expan> a greate
Clewe, That is hath very broad yeard<expan type="es">es</expan>, and soe
spread<expan type="es">es</expan> much Canvas It is good to allowe 
a good Clewe to a Mayne saile for by that meanes
the Tacke will come the better a boarde and the
Sheate will come farther Aft where by the 
saile will hold more wynde:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="clew garnet(n)">Clewe Garnett</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast to the Clewe of the
sail, and from thence runnes in a Blacke
wch is seased to the Middle of the yard The
vse whereof is when wee farthell a Maynesaile
or foresaile (for the name of Clewe Garnett
doth only belonge to the Clewe lynes of those 
Two sayles) Then this Rope doth hale vpp the
Clewe of the saile Close to the Middle <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
of the yarde:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="clew line(n)">Clewe lines</form>
<xpln lang="en">This is the same to Topsayles, Topgallant
sayles and spritt sailes That the Clewe
Garnett is to the Mayne saile and hath the
very same vse: Note in fowle weather &amp; Gust
when wee take in our Topsailes wee hale home
the Clewes lyne first, because the sayle wilbe
taken soe much the Easier:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="clinch(v)">A Clinch</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to batter or Ryvitt a bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> end vpon a Ringe
Or turne backe th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of anie nayle soe as it 
</page>

<page no="44">
make it fast att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd <expan type="wch">which</expan> is driven through wee 
alsoe call that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Cabell <expan type="wch">which</expan> is seized about
the Ringe of the Anchor the Clinch of the Cabell/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="clincher(n)">a Clyncher:/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale shipp: Barke or Boate, whose planck<expan type="es">es</expan>
or Board<expan type="es">es</expan> are larded over one an other and 
Clinched or nayled one through an other <correction type="d" deleted="and 
Clinched"></correction>wth nayles and Reeves whose outesides
are Birthed or wrought upp wthout Tymbers 
framed as wee doe in other shipps <expan type="wch">which</expan> worke is
called Carvell worke:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="clinching(n)">Clinching.</form> 
<xpln lang="en">Is as you would saye a slight Caulkinge And is
most used when wee are att Sea &amp; suspect fowle
weather, soe that we may take in water at the
Port<expan type="es">es</expan> wee use to Co<expan type="m+_a+_">mma</expan>nnde the Carpenter
To Clinch the Port<expan type="es">es</expan>: That is to drive alittle
Ockham into the Seames of the Port<expan type="es">es</expan> wch
May be done to serve forme either wthin 
Board or wthout Bord:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="clothe(v)">Cloath</form>
<xpln lang="en">Wee saye a saile doth Cloathe the Mast
when it is soe longe that it touches <expan type="y+e">the</expan> grating<expan type="es">es</expan>
or Hatches: soe as that noe winde cann goe 
betwixt the sailes and the hatches or grating<expan type="es">es</expan>
A shipp spread<expan type="es">es</expan> much Cloath that is when
she hath much Broad sailes:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cloy(v)">Cloyde</form>
<xpln lang="en">When thereis anie thinge gott into the touch
hole or Breech of the peece soe that the pryminge
powder cannot come to give fire to the rest
wee saye the Peece is Cloyed;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="45">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="coak(n)">Coakes,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are little square thing<expan type="es">es</expan> of brasse <expan type="wth">with</expan> a hole
in them putt in the middle of some of the greatest
woodden sheevers to keepe them from splytinge
and gallinge by the Pinn of the Clocke where&shy;
on they turne;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="coaming(n)">Coamings</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Coaming<expan type="es">es</expan> or Coaming of the Hutches
or the grating<expan type="es">es</expan>, is that peece of tymber or
Plancke <expan type="wch">which</expan> beares them vpp higher then the
deckes, soe as they doe not lye even <expan type="wth">with</expan> <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Decke
The vses whereof are to keepe the water from
runninge downe at the Hutches to give some
ease for men to stand vpright betwixt the 
lower deckes, If the deckes belowe and
neere togeather And alsoe in the Coaming<expan type="es">es</expan>
they may fitt holes to vse Muskett<expan type="es">es</expan> and soe 
serve for a Close feight;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="coat(n)">Coats</form>
<xpln lang="en">Those peeces of Tarrd Canvas <expan type="wch">which</expan> are putt
About the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Partures &amp; the Pumps
att the decke that noe water runne downe 
by them are called Coat<expan type="es">es</expan> The same is vsed
to the rudderhead:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="collar(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Coller;</form> 
<xpln lang="en">Is that rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast about <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Beake&shy;
head, wherevnto the dead-man Eye is seased
vnto <expan type="wch">which</expan> the mayne staye is fastened There
wch is called a Coller or a Garland &amp; is
there placed to save the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> from gallinge
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="comb(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Combe</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale peece of tymber sett vnder <expan type="y+e">the</expan> lower
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Boate head neere the middest wth
</page>

<page no="46">
Two holes in it and is iust in the Nature and hath the
same vse to the fore tackes, that the Chieftrees hath to
the mayne tackes <expan type="wch">which</expan> is to bringe the Tacke a board;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="compass(n)">Compasse</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that moveable instrument <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Flye whereon
are discribed the 32 poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> or wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> by <expan type="wch">which</expan> we direct
or Steere our Courses att Sea: The fashion is 
knowne to all, and for the vses they are handled 
at Large in many bookes <expan type="wch">which</expan> write of Naviga<expan type="">cion</expan> 
There are 3 kind<expan type="es">es</expan> first the playne Meridionall 
Compasse, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is the ordinarie one: The second 
a Compasse of Varia<expan type="">cion</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> shewe the varia<expan type="">cion</expan>
of the Compasse form the true North and South
The Third is adarke Compasse <expan type="wch">which</expan> beinge but an
ordinarie Compasse in vse, is only soe called because
the Flye hath the poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> soe discribed <expan type="wth">with</expan> noe 
collo<expan type="+r">ur</expan>s, as the other have, but only <expan type="wth">with</expan> blacke and
white beinge most Convenient to be seeme when 
wee Steere by night wthout anie light but only 
starrlight;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cond(v)">To Cond or Cund</form>
<xpln lang="en">I thinke this word comes of <etym lang="la">Conducere</etym> in latine
for it import<expan type="es">es</expan> as much as to leade or direct the 
shipp <expan type="wch">which</expan> waye she shall goe It is Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly <expan type="p-_">pro</expan>nounced
thus Cun the shipp, <expan type="wch">which</expan> implyes as much as to
direct him att the Helme, how to steere in longe 
Courses, when wee are off att Sea There is not
soe much heede taken of it for then they direct 
their Course vpon a poynt att the Compasse and
soe lett him att the helme looke to Steere right
on that poynt of the Compasse: There the Master
Mate or some other standinge aloft doth give 
direc<expan type="">eccion</expan> to him att the Helme, and this wee call
Cundinge or Cunninge: Sometyme hee whoe 
Cond<expan type="es">es</expan> the shipp wilbe speakinge to him at
Helme att every little yawe, <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Seafaringe
love not as a kinde of disgrace to their 
Steeridge: Then in mockinge they will saye sure
The Channell is narrowe he Cond<expan type="es">es</expan> soe thicke
</page>

<page no="47"> 
whereby you may gather that in Narrowe
Chanells it is necessarie and vsuall to Cond
thicke Note that accordinge as the shipps
sailes are trymed either before or by a wynd
soe they vse seu<expan type="+r">er</expan>all termes in Condinge and to
vse other were vnproper, and rediculous 
a mongst them If ashipp goe before a wynde
or as they terme it, betwixt two Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>: Then
he whoe Cond<expan type="es">es</expan> vseth these termes to him att the
Helme Starrbord: Larbord the Helme amidd<expan type="es">es</expan>
shipps: Note that when we say Starbord, the
meaninge is that he must putt the Helme to 
the Starrbord side and then the shipp will goe
to the Larbord, for the shipp doth over goe
contrary to the Helme If the shipp goe by 
a wynd and quarter wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> They saye Alloofe
or keepe yo<expan type="+r">ur</expan> loofe or fall not off weare noe more
keepe her to have a Care of the Lee latch: 
Touch the wynde All these doe implie, the
Same in amanner and are to bidd him at the
Helme to keepe her neere the wynd Ease the
Helme no neere; Beare vpp; these word<expan type="es">es</expan> doe
appoynt him to keepe her from the wynd, and
make her goe more large or right before: 
Some speeches are Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> to both as steddy
That is keepe the shipp from goeinge in and out
But iust vpon the poynt that you are to 
Steere, and as you goe, and such like;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cook room(n)">Cooke roome</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Cooke roome is the place where they 
dresse there victualls, and this Roome is to 
be placed in diu<expan type="+r">er</expan>s places in the shipp according
to the shipps imployment: In Marchant 
men whoe must imploye all their hold for the
stoweinge of their good<expan type="es">es</expan>, and soe stowe all 
</page>

<page no="48">
victualls betwixt their deckes it is best to have the Cooke
roome in the forecastle especially beinge Contrived
in furnaces for the saveinge of wood in longe 
iorneyes as alsoe for <expan type="y+t">that</expan> in feight they bringe their
Sterne and not their Prowe to feight And 
therefore wilbe the lesse dis<expan type="c+_o+_m+_">comm</expan>odious to them
Besid<expan type="es">es</expan> they doe not carry soe much ordinance wth
them forward on: And therefore the weight of 
the Cooke roome is not soe offensive But in a 
Man of warre: It is most Convenient to have
it in the foreshipp or fore Castle: My reasons
are these First That it wilbe (be it placed aswell
as it cann be) hinder the vse of the Ordinance:
Secondly it will lye over the Powder: Thirdly
beinge a Man of warr that p<expan type="+r">re</expan>tend<expan type="es">es</expan> to 
feight <expan type="wth">with</expan> his Prowe; That <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te is likelyest to 
receive Shott <expan type="wch">which</expan> if anie chance to come 
amongst the Brickes of the Cookeroome They
will spoyle more men, then the shott: And
besid<expan type="es">es</expan> the Cookeroome it selfe (for the voyage)
is spoyled there beinge noe meanes to repaire
it att sea, and then they must need<expan type="es">es</expan> vse an other
Soe that I thinke noe man of discretion will 
Command or vse that for most sufficient <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
most subiect to be destroyed and Cannot bee
repaired: Fowerthly A man of warr ever
Carryes much Ordinance there, and there&shy;
fore it is fitt to avoyd as much as may be
anie waight that may charge her foreshipp
Fifthly it is dangerous for fyreinge the shipp 
for beinge made vp to the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> soe that
men cannot goe round about in longe continu&shy;
ance and much heateing they may fyre the
shipp as vn awares Sixthly it takes awaye
the grace and pleasure of the most impor&shy;
tant, and pleasantest <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of all the shipp
For anie one whoe comes abord a Man of 
warr will principally looke at the Chase
</page>

<page no="49">
beinge the place where the Cheife offensive
force of the shipp should lye And to Conclude
I doe not knowe anie C<expan type="o+_m+_o+_">ommo</expan>ditie it can give
to a Man of warr; wherefore in my 
oppinion the Best placeinge the Cooke roome
is in the Hutch waye vpon the First Orlope,
not in the hold as the king<expan type="es">es</expan> Shipps doe wch
must need<expan type="es">es</expan> spoyle all the victualls <expan type="wth">with</expan> to&shy;
much heatinge the hold, or the leastwise force
them to stowe it soe neere the Stem &amp; Sterne
That it must wronge and wringe the shipp
much and loose much Stowage: And it 
beinge there placed (as it doth a voyd all 
former inconvenience both of the hold and 
fore Castel and yet shalbe as serviceable 
Soe hath it this benefitt more That it doth
Wonderfully well ayre the shipp betwixt the
deckes <expan type="wch">which</expan> is a greate health vnto the Com&shy;
panie: But if I were to goe to sea: as a Man
of warr I would have noe Cookeroome at
all but such a on as I would have contrived to
be removed and strooken downe in howld if I
lift and yet should wast noe more wood 
then these doe, and dresse sufficient meate
for the Company, and Roste and Bake some
Compleate companie for the C<expan type="o+_m+_a+_">omma</expan>nndr
Or anie <expan type="p-_">per</expan>son of qualitie;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

of a shipp is by a generall appella<expan type="">cion</expan> called
Cordage;/
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="counter(n)">Cownter</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that hollow Archinge <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te in the shipps
sterne betwixt the Transome &amp; the lower <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
of the Gallery <expan type="wch">which</expan> is called the lower Cownter
The vpp Cownter is from the Gallery to the
Lower <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the vpright of the Sterne;/
</xpln>

<page no="50">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="course(n)">Course</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is taken for that <correction type="d" deleted="parte"></correction> poynte of the Compasse
wch the shipp is to sayle vpon: As to saye the
place we must now goe to lyes East: we then
direct our Course East Alter the Course:
That is saile vpon an other poynt of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Compas
Mistake the Course: That is not to knowe how
the land lyes <expan type="wch">which</expan> waye to goe. Alsoe Mayne
Course, and fore Course, and Missen Course
Are the sayles wthout the Bonnett<expan type="es">es</expan> Note
All shipps of great Burthen have double 
Courses to hold more wynd and give the 
shipp more waye in a fresh gale but in an
Easie gale they hinder as doe all thing<expan type="es">es</expan> that 
are waightie over head;/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="crab(n)">a Crabb</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is an engine of wood <expan type="wth">with</expan> 3 Clawes placed
on the ground iust in the Nature of a Cap&shy;
ptayne, beinge placed and most C<expan type="o+_m+_o+_">ommo</expan>nly 
vsed where they buyld shipps: for <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Launching
out or heaveinge in of a shipp in to the decke
or off the Keg;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cradle(n)">a Cradell</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a frame of tymber brought a longst <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
outside of the shipp by the Bildg wherein
they doe launch Shipps for the greater
safetie; In Spaine and other places 
they vse to tryme all their great shipps
in them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="craft(n)">Craft</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is anie kinde of Nett<expan type="es">es</expan> or lynes or hookes to Catch
Fish: For at Sea they will saye when they have lost
their lynes or Nett<expan type="es">es</expan>: That they have lost their
Craft wee alsoe call smale vessells : As Ketches
Hoyes, Craes, and such like, smale Craft &amp;
he that sailes in them we saye he vses smale
Craft;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="51">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="crank(a)">Cranck</form>
<xpln lang="en">We saye a shipp is Crank sided when she will 
beare but smale ayle, and will lye downe very 
much <expan type="wth">with</expan> side wynd<expan type="es">es</expan>. The cause whereof is that
her breath beinge layd to lowe she hath noe thinge
to beare her vpp, when once shee begins to heele
Wee alsoe saye she is Crancke by the ground, when
she cannot be brought a ground but in danger to 
over throwe: The reason whereof is she hath noe
Bilge to beare her her flores beinge layd to 
narrowe; / 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cringle(n)">Creengles</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are little Ropes spliced into the Boltropes
of all sayles belonginge to the Maine and 
foremast vnto <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Bowleinge bridles are 
made fast And they are alsoe to hold by when 
wee shake off a Bonnett;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="crossbar(n)">Crossbarr;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a round shott <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Barre of Iron as it were
putt through the middle Comynge out at both
end<expan type="es">es</expan> some 6 or 8 inches more or lesse This will
not flie soe farr as a round shott but farther 
then a Langerell or a Chayne shott: It is very
good to vse in feight for the Cuttinge and 
spoyleinge of Ropes, sayes, yard<expan type="es">es</expan> &amp; Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, as
alsoe to doe Execu<expan type="">cion</expan> a mongst men where 
they stand plyinge their smale shott But
it is not vsed vnder water for that it will 
hardly goe through a good shipps sides vnlesse
it be vsed out of very great Ordinance;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cross jack(n)">Cross-iack</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a yard at the vpper-End of the Missen

haveinge noe Hilliard<expan type="es">es</expan> nor tyes belongeinge to
it, The vse whereof is to spread and hale on
the Missen top sayle sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cross-tree(n)">Cross=trees</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those Crosse Peeces of tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> are sett
on the head of the Mast beinge bolted and lett 
into an other, very stronge, In a generall appella<expan type="">cion</expan>
all those fore peeces beinge soe made &amp; putt to&shy;
geather are called the Crosse trees, But in
truth and more strictly all those Two peeces 
wch goe Thwart shipps are called the Crossetrees
and the other <expan type="wch">which</expan> goe longst shipps are called Tress&shy;
trees: The vse whereof is to beare and keepe 
vpp the Topp Mast for the foote of the Topp&shy;
Mast is fastened in them, soe that they beare all
The stresse, These alsoe doe beare vpon the Topp
And doe necessarielye belonge to all Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
Carry anie other, Top or Flagstaffe at <expan type="y+e">the</expan> head;/
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="crowfoot(n)">Crowe feete</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those small lynes or Ropes wch
stand in Sixe, Eight, Tenn or more <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan>
beinge soe divided and putt through holes of 
a deadman-eye, They are of noe necessitie But
only sett vpp by the Boate-swaynes to make 
the shipp to showe full of smale Rigging<expan type="es">es</expan> and
are placed to the Bottome of the Back&shy;
stayes of the Fore Topmast, Sprit-sayle
Topmast Missen, Topmast and the Topgallant 
Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="culvertail(n)">Culvertaile</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a manner of lettinge one Tymber into an

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cunting(n)">Cuntinge.</form>
<xpln lang="en">The hollowe Concauitye betwixt the
Strands of the Roapes is called the Cun&shy;
ting, it is Chieflie perceiued in three
Strond Roapes, for Roapes made of 4
Strandes lye smoother togeather, &amp; Closer
so that tis little perceiued. <expan type="y+e">the</expan> are al&shy;
waies made of 4 Strand Roapes, because they
are smoother to run in <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Hownd, then three
Strandes Roapes: Worme <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Cable in the
Cunting. <term lang="la">vide</term> Worming.
</xpln>
<lemenote>
Word-entry absent from British Library Sloane MS 207 but found in British Library Additional MS 21571, fol. 49r.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cut(v)">Cutt</form>
<xpln lang="en">This word is vsed in this sense, Cutt the
saile; That is when men are a loft vpon the
yard The Mayne saile or Foresaile beinge
farthelled vpp they must lett it fall downe
When a saile is well fashioned, they saye it is well Cutt; Cutt the Cabell in the Hawse -
That is most co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed when we ride in some
storme, and desire to sett saile but Cannot stay
the weyinge of the Anchor for feare of driveinge
tomuch to Leeward or the like, generally vpon
anie occation when we cannot staye the weying
of the Anchor, Then wee cutt the Cabell in 
the Hawse, to save soe much as wee can of it
In extraordinarie occasion either att an Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>
or att Sea, wee some tymes cutt the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> att
the Bord The cause att an Anchor is when
the storme increaseth soe that the power wch
the wynd hath vpon the Rigginge &amp; the Mast
doth force her Anchor to come home or else
endanger the breakinge of the Cabell Then
they Cast downe the Mast, But if there be
only a greate Seagale and little or noe wynd
There it is to noe purpose to Cutt the Mast
for they doe little or noe hurt; This happens
in many places where the wynd doth not blowe
home, as att S<expan type="+t">aint</expan>Se Cruse in Barbarie where
such have rid such a Roade that the Sea hath
broke over their fore Topp and yett not a breth 
of wynd: Att sea they Cutt the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> on these
occasions: When an extraordinarie Gust
or Storme, hath soe layd the shipp on saile <expan type="y+t">that</expan>
there is noe hope, that she can right a gaine
</page>

<page no="54">
and soe would quicklie be over sett or fild <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
water: Then in cuttinge the Mast, first Cutt the
Lee shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>, for else when the Mast is over bord
it wilbe hard Cuttinge them and th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of the
Mast may chaunce beate out the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>,
next Cutt a little into the weather side of the 
Mast, and then Cuttinge the weather shroud<expan type="es">es</expan>
The Mast will instantly and wthout anie 
Dangerfulle over Bord; Likewise at Sea 
in a greate Storem where the shipp rowles
much: If the <expan type="p-_">par</expan>tners give waye the Mast
will roule out the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>: In this case alsoe
if they cannot be mended, The Mast must
be Cutt by the Bord;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cutwater(n)">Cuttwater</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Cutt water is the sharpenesse of 
the shipp before <expan type="wch">which</expan> doth as it were Cutt the 
water and divide it before it comes to the 
Bowe, soe that it maye come by degrees and
not to sodenly to the breadth of the shipp 
otherwise the shipp would beate soe full ag<expan type="+t">ainst</expan>
the water, that she would make but little
waye; And therefore many tymes when 
a shipp is to Bluffe we putt to a false St<expan type="e+_m+_e+_">emme</expan>
and as it were lengthen her forward on, and
this we call a Cutt water, <expan type="wch">which</expan> will not only 
make her sayle better but alsoe make her 
much a gainst a headsea:
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="55">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="D"><alpha>D</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="davit(n)">the Dauitt</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a peece of tymber haveinge a Notch
at one end whereon they hange a Blocke
by a Strape and this is only vsed for to 
hange the Blocke on <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Called the the fishe
Blocke, by <expan type="wch">which</expan> they hale vp the Flooke of 
the Ancho<expan type="+r">or</expan> to the bowe or Loofe; It is shifted
to either side as they have occasion and 
is not made fast to the shipp butt layd by
till it be vsed It is putt out betwixt the Catt
and the loofe: Launch out or Launch in the
Davitt: That is putt it out or in alsoe the 
Boate hath a Davitt <expan type="wch">which</expan> is sett out over <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
head of the Boate <expan type="wth">with</expan> a sheever into <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
they bringe the Boye Rope to waye the 
Ancho<expan type="+r">or</expan>, and it stand<expan type="es">es</expan> in the Carling<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are
in the Boat<expan type="es">es</expan> bowe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="deadman's-eye(n)">Deadmen-eyes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are a kinde of Blockes wherein there are
many holes, but noe sheevers, wherein the 
Lannyers goe, <expan type="y+t">that</expan> make fast the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> to the
Chaines; The Mayne staies in some shipps
are sett taught by Laniers in deadmen-Eyes
but most greate shipps vse double Blockes
the Crowe feete doe reeve through dead&shy;
men-Eyes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="deadwater(n)">Deadwater</form>
<xpln lang="en">The water <expan type="wch">which</expan> is the Eddy water at the
sterne of the shipp is Called deadwater
and therefore wee saye a shipp hold<expan type="es">es</expan> much 
dead water; That is <expan type="wch">which</expan> hath a greate Eddy
followes her att the Sterne or Rudder and 
this may be called dead because it doth not
passe a waye <expan type="wth">with</expan> that life and quicknesse 
as the other doth;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="deck(n)">Deck</form>
<xpln lang="en">The decke is <expan type="y+t">that</expan> floare or Plancke wherein wee
place <emend err="or">our</emend> Ordinance: It lyes vpon the Beames
they are called by the names of First second
</page>

<page no="56">
and Third decke beginninge att the lowest Alsoe 
there is the halfe decke That is the decke <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
from the Maine Maste to the Steeridge, and 
quarter decke <expan type="wch">which</expan> is from the Steridge aloft to
the M<expan type="+rs">Masters</expan> Cabbine: There is alsoe a spare decke
wch is the vppermost betwixt the two Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
and is made very sleight <expan type="wth">with</expan> a nettinge or sleight
bord<expan type="es">es</expan> to ward<expan type="es">es</expan> the sid<expan type="es">es</expan> of the shipp &amp; agrateinge
in the middest Alsoe these deckes are called by the
names of Orlopes As they vse to saye the
first and Second Orlopp: A Flush decke
or as they vse to saye a deckeflush foreaft 
That is when from Steme to Sterne it lyes 
vpon a right lyne wthout anie fall; Note that
the best contriveinge of a man of warr is to 
have the decke flush and to have all the Port<expan type="es">es</expan>
in that decke of an equall height soe as anie
Peece maye serve anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te, The reasons are
for that the deckes beinge flushe men maye
passe fore and aft <expan type="wth">with</expan> much more Ease for
the deliu<expan type="+r">er</expan>inge Powder and shott or releiveinge
one an other But cheifely for <expan type="y+t">that</expan> if a Peece
or two be dismounted by shott, in anie place
where there is a fall, an other cannot be 
brought to supplie it<expan type="es">es</expan> place, besid<expan type="es">es</expan> the discom&shy;
moditie by disioyninge the equall <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
shipp; The shipp is much weakened and alsoe
it looseth much stowage in the Sterne Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>
yett there maye be some vse of these falls to
a Marchant Man for his defence whoe maye
fitt a Close feight out of every fall, and 
thought he loose one <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the decke yet he may
still keepe more to be gayned from him; The
decke cambers, That is when it doth not lye
flatt, but Compassinge To sincke a decke or
to lett sinke a decke is to remove it and 
place it lower; To rayse a decke is to put
it higher a bove water The makeinge
of a decke is termed the layinge of a 
Deck
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="57"> 
<wordentry type="h"> 
<form lexeme="deep sea-lead(n)">Deepe sea-lead</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the Lead <expan type="wch">which</expan> is hunge att <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sea&shy;
lyne to sincke it downe: The weight 
where of is Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly 24 <expan type="lb'">lb</expan> This hath some
white hard tallowe layd vpon the lower
End <expan type="wch">which</expan> bring<expan type="es">es</expan> vpp the ground, and soe
by the differences of the ground wee 
knowe where and vpon what Coast<expan type="es">es</expan> wee
are: But in Ooze ground wee vse a white
woollen cloth vpon the <correction type="d" deleted="C"></correction>lead <expan type="wth">with</expan> a little
Tallowe <expan type="wth">with</expan> out <expan type="wch">which</expan> Cloth the Ooze would 
Not sticke vonto the Tallowe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="deep sea line(n)">Deepe sea Line</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale lyne <expan type="wth">with</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee sound in deepe
waters to finde ground and soe accordinge
to the depth and ground in many knowne 
places, as in the Comeinge into our Channell
and many other places when wee cann see
noe land yet wee knowe <emend err="were">where</emend> we are;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="disembogue(v)">Disimbogue</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is as much as to saye to come out of the mouth
of anie Gulfe <expan type="wch">which</expan> beinge large wthin may 
have straight and narrowe comeinge out, beinge
vsed thus; when they come out of the west&shy;
Indies betweixt Cuba, and Cape Florida, 
wch is the straight whereout the Current
doth sett. They saye they disimbogud out of
the Bulke, But it is not vsed for the goeinge 
out of a Harbor or the like;/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="dispert(v)">Dispertinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">To dispert is to fynd out the difference of 
The diameters of Mettalls betwixt the Breech
and the mouth of anie Peece of Ordinance
By <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee knowe, what allowance to give to the
mouth of the peece beinge ever lesse then the
peeces Breech That thereby we may make a
</page>

<page no="58">
iust shott There are divers wayes but the playnest
is the surest and best <expan type="wch">which</expan> is by puttinge in
of a strawe or smale sticke at the touch hole to 
the lower side of the Concave or Sillender of the
peece And then applie it in the same manner
to the mouth And it will exactly shew the 
difference of the thicknesse of the Mettaile att
the Breech and mo<correction type="i">u</correction>th of the peece;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="dock(n)">Dock;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are Two kind<expan type="es">es</expan> of dockes a drye docke
wch is made of Fludgates to keepe out the Tyde,
in <expan type="wch">which</expan> we buyld our shipps and repayre them
in <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee sitt wthout danger &amp; harme, The 
other is a Wett decke <expan type="wch">which</expan> is anie Creeke or
place where wee may hale in a shipp out of
the tyd<expan type="es">es</expan> waye in the Ooze And there when 
a shipp hath made her selfe as it were a place
to lye in wee say the shipp hath decks her selfe,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="drabbler(n)">a Drabler,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Vide <correction type="d" deleted="Bell"></correction> <xref>Bonnet</xref> for this is in all respect<expan type="es">es</expan>
the same to the Bonnett, that the Bonnett is
to the Course; This is only vsed when the Course
and Bonnett are to Showle for to Cloath the 
Mast: Some smale shipps <expan type="wch">which</expan> are Coasters
and therefore are for most Conveniece to 
have short Courses, doe vse Two drablers, 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="drag(n)">Draggs</form>
<xpln lang="en">Any thinges that hange over the shipp att
sea as shirt<expan type="es">es</expan>, gownes and the like, as alsoe the
Boate in that respect, All <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe hinder 
the shipps waye vnder sayle, are Called dragg<expan type="es">es</expan>,
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="draught(n)">Draught,</form>
<xpln lang="en">By draught in water is ment soe many 
Foote, as the shipp goes in water, A shipp 
drawes much water That is goes deepe in
</page>

<page no="59">
water: <emend err="Ashipp"></emend> a shipp of smale draught, That
is drawes but little water Note that shipps
of greate draught are Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly wholesome
shipps in the sea; And shipps of little draught
Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly goe best but Rowle most: The First is 
Best for a longe voyage the last for a discovery,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="dredge(n)">a Dredge.</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is an Engine where<expan type="w+th">with</expan> they take vp
Oisters out of the Sea being made
of a frame of Iron sharpe at the bottom
and a verie strong Nett of a small com&shy;
passe fastend to it, to Contayne <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Oysters
or anie thing else <expan type="w+ch">which</expan> the Iron rakes of
the Grownd.
</xpln><lemenote>
Word-entry absent from British Library Sloane MS 207 but found in British Library Additional MS 21571, fol. 54v-55r.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>


<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="dredge(v)">to Dredge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Or dredginge is to take a little Grapnell wch
beinge hunge over the Boat<expan type="es">es</expan> sterne, we lett downe
to drage vpon the grownd to finde a Cabell wch
hath bin lett slipp vnto whose Anchor there was
noe Buoy for this passinge a longe the ground
as the Boate doth rowe will Catch hold of it, if it
meete <expan type="wth">with</expan> it;/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="drift sail(n)">a Drift saile</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is saile vsed vnder water beinge veered out
right a head haveinge sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> to it, the vse whereof
is to keepe a shipps head right vpon the Sea
in a Storme, alsoe it is good where a shipp 
drives in fast <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Current, to hinder her driveinge
in soe fast But it is most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed by 
Fishermen in the North seas;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="drive(v)">Driue</form>
<xpln lang="en">We saye a shipp drives when we lett fall the 
Anchor and it will not hold the shipp fast, but
that she falls a waye <expan type="wth">with</expan> the tyde or wynde; 
for <expan type="wch">which</expan> we have noe helpe, but to veere more
Cabell; For you must note, that the more Cabell
Is out The faster and surer a shipp will ride; 
Or else to lett fall more Anchor, alsoe when 
a shipp is a Hull or a Trey, we saye she drives to
Leeward or drives in <expan type="wth">with</expan> the shore, And the
like accordinge to the waye she meetes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="duck up(v)">Duck vp</form>
<xpln lang="en">This terme is vsed <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Clewe Garnett
and Clewe lynes, of the maine saile for sailes
and spritt sailes when as the Maine saile or fore
</page>

<page no="60">
saile doth hinder the feight foreward that Steeres,
or anie the like occasions: And to the spritt saile
most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly when we make a shott <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Chase
peece for the Clewe of the spritt saile, will 
hinder the feight, and beinge not duckt vpp wilbe
shott awaye, soe then wee saye ducke vp the 
Clewe lynes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="61">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="E"><alpha>E</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="earing(n)">Earinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Bolt Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> at all the Fower
Corners of the saile is left open as it were a Ring
The two vppermost are putt over th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd<expan type="es">es</expan> of the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> or yard armes, and soe the saile is att
those two end<expan type="es">es</expan> made fast to the yard into the 
lowermost; The Tackes and Sheaths are seased
or (as the more proper terme is) They are
bent vnto the Clewe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ease(v)">To Ease</form>
<xpln lang="en">This word is vsed in the same sense at Sea,
as otherwise wee vse the word slacke; For 
generally when we would have anie Rope
slacker and not soe hard strayned, we saye
Ease the Bowling<expan type="es">es</expan> Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>: Only when the
Tacke should be slackened; The proper Terme
is lett rise the Tacke, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is a very fitt Terme
in respect That the Tacke beinge loosed it rises
vpp from the Chestrees vnto <expan type="wch">which</expan> it was haled
Close;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="eddy(n)">an Eddie</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the runninge backe of the Water in some 
place contrary to the Tyde and soe fallinge
into the tyde againe <expan type="wch">which</expan> happens by reason 
of some head land or greate poynt in a River
Comeinge out soddenly, and soe hinderinge 
the Free passage of the Water <expan type="wch">which</expan> it had
in the Channell before it come to this poynt,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="eddy water(n)">an Eddie water</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the wynd <expan type="wch">which</expan> recoyles or returnes backe from
anie saile, Hawse or the like goeinge Contrary
to that wynd whence it proceeded, But is never
soe stronge as the other;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="end for end(adv)">End for End</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is a terme vsed when anie Rope doth r<expan type="">unn</expan>e
all out of the Blocke; That it is unreeved or
</page>

<page no="62">
as when a Cable or Hawser doth runne all out
att the Hawse, <expan type="wch">which</expan> may happen either of purpose
to save the Cabell, or by Chance when Comeinge
to an anchor, if they should Mislayinge of the 
Stoppers, or that the Stoppers should breake
Then they saye: The Cabell is att Hawse 
runn out End for End 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="enter(v)">Enter</form>
<xpln lang="en">To enter is to come into a shipp But in feight
they must be carefull to Cleere the deckes wth
fire Pott<expan type="es">es</expan> or the like if it be possible from the
Traynes of Powder before men doe enter For
it happens many tymes, That there are more
men left in a minuit By enteringe then in 
longe feight Bord &amp; Borde and therefore
beinge soe dangerous it is fitt that men should
bee well advised first: though many tymes, if
a shipp bee not well provided of Close fieght<expan type="es">es</expan>
it is the spediest &amp; safest waye of takeinge 
them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="entering ladder(n)">Entring Ladder</form>
<xpln lang="en">Of theses there are two, the one <expan type="wch">which</expan> is vsed
by the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> in harbo<expan type="+r">r</expan>, and faire weather
wth entringe Ropes to it: This is all made of
wood, the other is made of Ropes <expan type="wth">with</expan> smale
staves for steps, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is hung over the Gallery
for enteringe out of the Boate in fowle 
weather, when by reason of the shipps heaveinge
and settinge, They dare not bringe the Boate
to the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> for feare of Staveinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="entering rope(n)">Entring Rope;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> <emend err="handes">hangs</emend> by the side of the shipp
in the wast where men doe vsually come 
a Board the shipp out of the Boate But it
is taken generally for anie Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is given
a man to Enter by:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="63">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="eye(n)">Eies</form>
<xpln lang="en">The hole wherein the Ringe of the Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>
is putt Called the Eye of the Anchor: Alsoe
the Compasse or Ringe: <expan type="wch">which</expan> is left of the 
Strapp wherevnto a Blocke is seased is -- 
called the Eye of the Strapp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="eyelet hole(n)">Eylett-holes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those round holes a longst the Bottome
of the sailes vnto <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe belonge Bonnett<expan type="es">es</expan>
and the Bonnett<expan type="es">es</expan> have the same for the 
drablers: They have a little lyne sowne a boute
them, to make them stronge, and are for noe
other vse, but to receive into them the Latchett<expan type="es">es</expan>
of the Bonnett or drablers <expan type="wth">with</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> The Bonnett
is laced to the Course and the drabler to
the Bonnett:
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="64">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="F"><alpha>F</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fathom(n)">Faddom</form>
<xpln lang="en">A Faddome is 6 foote, <expan type="wch">which</expan> though every one knowe
I sett downe to give notice, that wee measure the
length of all our Ropes by Faddomes and not by 
anie other measure as we doe the Compasse
of the Ropes by inches, For wee waye a Cabell
or a Hawser of soe many Faddome longe 
and soe many inches about; Alsoe wee 
re<expan type="">ckon</expan> in soundinge by faddomes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fake(n)">a Fake</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is one Cirkle of anie Rope or Cabbell that is
Quoyled vp round; And soe when wee Veere
out a Cabell, They many tymes aske to knowe
how much is left behind wthin Bord; How many
Fakes are left;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fall off(v)">Fall off</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp vnder saile doth not keepe soe
neere the wynd as wee apoynt, wee saye that
the shipp Falls off: This happens many tymes
by the Steeres-mans negligence But sometymes
the fault is in the shipp, <expan type="wch">which</expan> happens either
because shee may be to light a head, or that her
Mast may be stayed too forward for these two
thing<expan type="es">es</expan> doe make a sheepes head fall from the
wynd;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fall(n)">Falls</form>
<xpln lang="en">When wee men<expan type="">cion</expan> the falls of a shipp, as
to saye a shipp hath a Fall or many Falls it is
ment by the raiseinge or layinge some <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
of the decke higher or lower then the other
Alsoe the smale Rope, <expan type="wch">which</expan> we hale by in all
Tackles, is called the Falle of the Tackle 
as to saye over hale the Fall of the greate
Tackle only the wyndinge Tackle hath noe
Fall;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="65">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="fashion piece(n)"><doubtful type="u">the</doubtful> Fashion 
Peeces;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the two tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe discribe the breadth
of the shipp a Sterne, and are the outwardmost
Tymbers of the Shipps sterne on either side 
<emend err="expectinge">excepting</emend> a loft where the Cowneere is Counted;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fender bolt(n)">Fender Bolt --</form>
<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Bolts</xref>
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fender(n)">Fenders</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are anie peeces of old Cabells, or Ropes or 
Billett<expan type="es">es</expan> of wood <expan type="wch">which</expan> are hunge over the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan>
to keepe an other shipp or Boate from rubbinge
on the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>; That they maye not breake her
<correction type="d" deleted="their"></correction> Bend<expan type="es">es</expan>, or rubb of the stuff, when she is new
trymed: Boats have the same to save them from
much beatinge against the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan>: In the Boate
the men have little short Staves <expan type="wch">which</expan> they call
fenders: Hence we saye fend the Boate, that
is save her from beatinge against the ships
sid<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fid(n)">Fidd:</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is as it were an Iron pinn made Taperinge
and (as it were) sharpe att the lower end, wch
is for to open the Strand<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Ropes when
wee Splyce two Ropes to geather; But when
wee splice Cabells wee vse Fidd<expan type="es">es</expan> of wood in
the same Forme and nature but much bigger
wch if they were made of Iron would be too 
heavie to worke wthall The pinne of the heele
of the Top mast <expan type="wch">which</expan> beares it vpp on the 
Chestrees is a Fidd;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fidhammer(n)">Fiddha<expan type="m+_">mm</expan>er</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Fidd made sharpe at one end to splice a
Rope <expan type="wth">with</expan> a hammer at <expan type="y+e">the</expan> other end <expan type="wth">with</expan> a head
And a Claw to drive or drawe anaile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fight(n)">Feights</form>
<xpln lang="en">The mast Clothes <expan type="wch">which</expan> hange round about the shipp
to hinder men from beinge seene in feight
</page>

<page no="66">
are called the Feight<expan type="es">es</expan> Alsoe anie Bulkhead afore 
or abast out of <expan type="wch">which</expan> they may vse murderers
or smale shott, or generally anie place wherein
men may Cover them selves, and yet vse their
Armes are called Close feight<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fire work(n)">Fire-workes;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are anie kind of Artificiall receipt<expan type="es">es</expan> applied
to anie kinde of Gudgine, weapon or instrument
Whereby we vse to sett on fire, the Hulls, sailes
and Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> of a shipp in Feight, whereof there are
many sort<expan type="es">es</expan>, but the most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed att sea 
are these, Fire Pott<expan type="es">es</expan>, Fire-bales, Fire Peekes,
Trunckes, Brasse balls, Arrowes <expan type="wth">with</expan> fireworkes
and the like; To saye all that might concerninge
these will require too longe a discouse for this
that I heare p<expan type="+r">re</expan>tend;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fish(n)">a Fish</form>
<xpln>Is anie peece of tymber or Plancke <expan type="wch">which</expan> we
make fast either to Mast or yard to souccoure &amp;
strengthen it when it is in danger to breake
Then wee C<expan type="">omma</expan>nd the Carpenter; to fish
the Mast or yard, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is done First hollowinge it
fitt for the place, and then nayleinge it
wth speekes, and wouldinge it a bout <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
ropes: This Fish is very drye meate;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fish(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Fish</form> 
<xpln lang="en">Is a Tackle hunge at th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of the davitt by
the Strape of the Blocke, in the Blocke there
is a Runner <expan type="wth">with</expan> a hooke att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd <expan type="wch">which</expan> doth hitch
the Flooke <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Anchor and soe they hale by 
the fall that belong<expan type="es">es</expan> to it and soe raise the Flooke
to the Bowe, or Chaine wale of the shipp:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fish block(n)">Fish-blocke</form>
<xpln lang="en">It is the Blocke that belong<expan type="es">es</expan> to the fish and
is called the Fish-blocke;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="67">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="fish hook(n)">Fish hooke,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is likewise the hooke belonginge to the Fish 
And is called the Fishhooke;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="flag(n)">Flaggs;</form>
<xpln lang="en">There is only vsed att sea for distin<expan type="">cion</expan> of 
Nations, or officers of Fleet<expan type="es">es</expan>; As that the
Admirall should have his in the mayne topp
the vice admirall in the fore And the 
Reare Admirall in the Missen Topp; But alsoe
for distin<expan type="">cion</expan>, and signes what shipps must doe
accordinge as they have direc<expan type="">cion</expan>s from the
Cheife Co<expan type="">maunn</expan>der, as to Chase or to give
over, to come to Councell or the like Att sea 
to Lower or Strike one flagg in feightinge is
taken for yeildinge: But otherwise of greate 
obedience and respect: And to be made to 
take it in <expan type="p-_">per</expan> force, the greatest disgrace that
cann be; when they would have the Flagg out
they saye heave out the Flagg, and take in the
Flagge or Farthell the Flagg, That is wrapp
it Close about the Staffe: To strike the Flagg
is to pull it downe vpon the Capp and soe 
lett it hange over loose;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="flare(v)">Flaire</form>
<xpln lang="en">When ashipp is alittle howsed in neere the 
Water, and a bove that the worke doth hange
over a gaine, and is layd out broader aloft, 
They saye that the worke doth flaire over 
this makes ashipp more roome wthin board
for a Man of Warr, But it is not slightly nor
by the most <correction type="i">Co<expan type="">mmon</expan></correction> oppinion held to be holson for ashipp
Yett I have scene th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>xperience and anie of
oppinion, That is cann wronge a shipp butt
little; If her beareinge be layd high enough;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="flone(a)">Floane</form>
<xpln lang="en">When anie of the sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> are not haled home to the
Blockes: Then they saye the Sheate is Floane: 
But when they say lett the sheate That
</page>

<page no="68">
is to lett it goe a maine or as farr as it will This 
is most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed in greate gust<expan type="es">es</expan> for feare
of Spreadinge the Top Mast, or over settinge the
shipp, for the Seat<expan type="es">es</expan> beinge flowne the saile
doth hold noe wynd: I have seene in an extraordi&shy;
narie wynd, that when a shipp hath layne downe
on the quick side in the water, we have to make
her right a gaine, lett flye the sheate, but the 
Gust hath Flitted all the sayle to peeces 
leavinge not anie iott or but some ragge in 
the Bolt ropes;/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="flood(n)">Flood</form>
<xpln lang="en">It is Flood when the water begins to rise younge
flood: quarter flood; half Flood are all the
Termes Comonly knowne;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fluke(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Flooke</form>
<xpln lang="en">This is the Brod <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Anchor <expan type="wch">which</expan> takes
Hold in the ground: As alsoe those in the 
Grapnells, <expan type="wch">which</expan> have Fower Flookes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="float(v)">Floate</form>
<xpln lang="en">We saye anie thinge doth Floate <expan type="y+t">that</expan> swymes
above water, not touchinge ground; As the
shipp is afloate, That is when it is borne vpp
Cleere from the gournd, by the riseinge of 
the water: A floatie shipp, is a shipp wch
draws but little water;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="flow(v)">Flowe</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the water doth rise or heighten we say
it doth flowe: But note that it doth ever 
in all places seas or Rivers where it doth flowe,
Flowe before the shore, before it flowes by 
the offinge or Middle of the Streame and 
soe it doth Ebb by the shore before it doth in
the streame The reason is for that the water
is of most force and weight where it is deepest
and soe is hardlyer reformed beinge once bent 
</page>

<page no="69">
anie waye when wee saye it flowes at London
Bridge Southwest, or attaine other place
South or West or as it happens: By this
is ment That when the Moone is att the
Full, or else new Moone, Then vpon that
daye the Sunne beinge in the South west
poynt <expan type="wch">which</expan> is 3 a Clocke in the afternoone
it is height water at London Bridge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="floor(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Floare</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Floare of the shipp is soe much of the
Bottome of her as she doth rest vpon when
she is a ground And therefore those wch
have longe and broad floares lye best and
safest <expan type="wth">with</expan> the ground and the others are
Crancke and dangerous both the wringe 
them selves, and to overthrowe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="flush(adv)">Flush;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a decke is layd from stem to stem 
wthout anie falls or rysing<expan type="es">es</expan>; we saye her decke
lyes flush, fore and aft, and this word is not 
vsed in anie other sense;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fly(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Flye,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that part of the Compasse, whereon the 32
poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> of the wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> are discribed to <expan type="wch">which</expan> vnder&shy;
neath is the Needle made fast;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="free(v)">to Free</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp hath much water wee saye she 
want<expan type="es">es</expan> Pumps to free her: Or when wee bale
out the water, That is called freeinge the shipps
Alsoe when the Boate hath water in her wee
Co<expan type="">mma</expan>nd them to free the Boate: Soe that
this word Free is not vsed in anie other 
respect a boute a shipp, but to gett out the 
water, nor there is not anie other word vsed
soe <expan type="p-_">pro</expan><expan type="p-_">per</expan>ly for <expan type="y+e">the</expan> gettinge out the water in a 
shipp or Boate, as this;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="free-shot(n)">Free-shott,</form>
<xpln lang="en">When anie extraordinarie land waters come
downe a ryver sodainly, or else when anie greate
</page> 

<page no="70">
ryver comes into a Sea, soe as that the water
is Fresh a Myle or two, as in many places it
is we say it is a greate Free shott;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="forefoot(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Forefoote</form>
<xpln lang="en">There is noe such <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of a shipp <expan type="wch">which</expan> is termed
herefore foote: But it is aword vsed in this
kinde when two shipps saile, soe that one doth
lye <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Steme soe much a weather the
other, That keepinge their Courses, That shipp
wch doth soe lye will goe out a head of the other,
Then we saye, that she doth lye <expan type="wth">with</expan> the forefoote
of the other as she stand<expan type="es">es</expan> or comes <expan type="wth">with</expan> her fore&shy;
foote: But beinge once soe past out before her 
ahead, and by her wee doe not saye, she is past
by her forefoote, But thus she is gone out 
a Head: Soe that this word fore foote implies
a Crosse, an other shipps waye;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="forelock(n)">Forelockes,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are little flatt peeces of Irone made like
wedges, <expan type="wch">which</expan> are putt into the holes at th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd<expan type="es">es</expan>
of Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> to keepe the Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> from draweinge
out or slypinge backe, Alsoe these keepe 
downe and fast the Capsquares of the 
Carriages;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">

<xpln lang="en">Vide<xref>Mast</xref> 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="fore-reach(v)">Fore-reach</form>
<xpln lang="en">When Two shipps sayle togeather, or after
one an other, she <expan type="wch">which</expan> sailes best, that is fastest
doth forereach vpon the other: If Two shipps
saile both on waye by a wynd one may keepe
the better wynd, <emend err="Theother">The other</emend> maye forereach, Then
he that doth fore reach, if he would speke wth
the other (as suppose he be amann of warr
the other a Marchant he must cast a boute 
</page>

<page no="71">
he is soe farre reached of her that he may
lye <expan type="wth">with</expan> her forefotte;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="foul(a)">Fowle</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp hath binn longe vntrymed soe
that grasse or anie filth be gott aboute her 
she is fowle, Alsoe when anie Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> we 
should hale is hindred by anie other or tangled
in it selfe as Topsaile; Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan>, Tuckle 
Falls or the like may be, or anie thinge else 
soe that it cannot runne, we saye the Rope
is fowle, as the sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> are fowle of the 
Ordinance, the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan>, Clewe lynes 
or the like, are fowle, and soe must be Cleered
Before they can be made to Runne;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="foul water(a)">Foulewater</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp vnder saile comes into shallowe
water, soe as she rayses the Mudd, or sand
in her waye, <expan type="wch">which</expan> she maye though she doe not
touch the ground, but come verry neere it
wee saye she makes fowle water, when she
sayles <expan type="wth">with</expan> her keele neere the ground, can&shy;
not feele her helme, soe well as in deepe 
water. The reason is for that neere the ground
The water hath not that weight &amp; force as
it hath where it is deepe, And alsoe by 
reason of an Eddie, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made betwixt the
ground and the Bottome of the shipp, being
soe neere to geather, The water cannot come
soe swift to the Rudder as it doth in deeper 
waters Note that the swifter the water comes
</page>

<page no="72">
to the Rudder the better a shipp doth steere or
feele her helme:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="founder(v)">Founder</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp by an extraordinarie leake or
else by anie greate Sea: that broke into
her is halfe full or full of water, soe that we
cannot free the water forth we saye she
is Fowndered, The word is significant , for
iust as a Fowndred Horse cannot goe soe 
a shipp is full or neere full of water will 
not feele her helme; That is will neither
Veere nor steere bu drive a waye <expan type="wth">with</expan> the
sea iust like a logg of wood:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="fur(v)" lexeme="furring(n)">Furr or
Furd;</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are two kind<expan type="es">es</expan> of Furringe the one
Is after a shipp is built, to laye on, an other
Plancke vpon Plancke, The other <expan type="wch">which</expan> is 
more Eminent &amp; more properly furringe
is to Ripe vpp the First Planckes and
putt other tymbers vpon the First, and
soe to putt on the Planckes vpon these 
tymbers: The occations of it is to make a 
shipp beare a better saile for when a shipp
is too narrowe, and her beareinge not layd
out enough or too lowe, Then they must make 
her broader, and laye her beareinge heigher
They Comonly Furr some 2 or 3 streakes 
vnderwater And as much above accordinge
as the shipp requires more or lesse I thinke
in all the world there are not soe many ships
furd as are in England, And it is pittie 
there is noe order taken either for the 
punishinge of those whoe buyld such shipps
or the preventinge of it for it is an infinit
losse to the owners, and an vtter spoyleinge
and disgrace to all shipps that are soe
handled;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="73">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="foot hook(n)" lexeme="futt hook(n)">Futt hookes</form>
<xpln lang="en">This word is Com<expan type="">mon</expan>ly soe pronounced, But
I thinke more properly it should be called 
Foote hookes, For the Futt hookes are the
Compassinge tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> give the breadth
and beareinge to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> ship, <expan type="wch">which</expan> are scarfed to 
the ground Tymbers: And because noe tymbers
that Compasse cann be found longe enough to 
goe vpp through all the side of the shipp
These Compassinge tymbers are scarfed one
into an other, And these next the keele are called
the lower or ground Flotthookes, The vpper
other are called the vpper Futthookes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="furthell(v)">Furthell;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Or Farthellinge a saile, is when wee wrapp
vpp a saile Close togeather, and soe bynd it
wth the Caskett<expan type="es">es</expan> to the yard, but toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the 
yard armes we vse Rope yarnes, for the saile
is not very weightie, This manner wee vse
only to the mayne saile, Fore saile, and 
spritt saile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="furtheling line(n)">Furthellinge
lynes,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale lynes, <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made fast to all the
Topsailes, Topgallant-sayles, and alsoe, the 
Missen yard<expan type="es">es</expan> armes, The Missen, hath but one, The
other on either side, By these we furthell those
sailes, But the top sailes have not the Buntt bound
vpp to the yard, as the Maine and fore sailes
have but is layd in the topp, and soe bound fast
to the head of the Mast this wee call Stowinge
the Topsaile:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="former(n)">A former</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a peece of wood tunred <doubtful type="u">ro</doubtful>und somewhat
lesse then the bore of the Peece for <expan type="wch">which</expan> it is 
made, as a Saker foremer, a Minion former &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>.
The vse whereof is to make it vpon a paper 
Carthrages or lynneon Carthrages;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="74">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="G"><alpha>G</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gage(v)">Gage</form>
<xpln lang="en">We vse to Gage our Caske that we may see how 
greate it is, or how much it is leaked out, <expan type="wch">which</expan> we
doe by puttinge downe a sticke at the Bunge, and
that by the wetnesse will shewe how much liquo<expan type="+r">r</expan> is in
it: Alsoe when wee would knowe how much water
a shipp drawes when she is a floate we strike 
a Naile into a pike or Pole and soe putt it downe
by the Rudder; Till this naile catch hold vnder the
Rudder and this we call Gageinge the shipp Note
that we cannot exactly by this tell how much water
she drawes for we must allowe for the Racke of
the shipp aftward on, for the Pole doth not goe 
downe in it Perpendiculerly, And soe many foote
as shee drawes is called the shipps gage, when 
one shipp is to weather of an other; she hath as
they terme it the weather gage But they neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> 
vse to saye the Leegage;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gale(n)">Gale</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the wynd doth not blowe to hard, but rea&shy;
sonable soe that ashipp <correction type="d" deleted="doth"></correction> <correction type="i">may</correction> beare her topsailes
a Tryppe, we call it accordinge to the strength
of it, either an easie loome gale <expan type="wch">which</expan> is when it is
much wynd, somtymes at Sea Two shipps 
beinge not farr a sunder If it be fine smooth 
gentle weather, and but little wynd, one shipp
will have more wynd then an other, and somtymes
the one be flatt becalmed the other have alittle
Breadth of wynd, Then they saye, The shipp wch
hath the wynd doth gale a waye from the other,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

on the outside of the shipp next the Keele
</xpln>

the most dangerous place in all the shipp
to springe a Leeke for it is allmost impossible
to come to it wthin bord;/
</xpln>
