<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="garnet(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Garnett</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Tackle where <expan type="wth">with</expan> we hoise in all Cables
and good<expan type="es">es</expan> if they be not to heavie, as greate 
Ordinance &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> It hath a Pendant comes from
the head of the Maine Mast, <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Blocke 
wch is strongly seased in the Maine staye
iust over the Hutch waye, where wee vse to take
in our good<expan type="es">es</expan> to hold; In the Blocke they doe 
Reeve the Runner <expan type="wch">which</expan> hath a Hooke att one 
end, <expan type="wth">with</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee hitch the Sling<expan type="es">es</expan>, att the 
other a double Blocke in <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee Reeve the 
<emend err="the"></emend> Fall of the Runner, and soe by that we 
hale and hoise in the good<expan type="es">es</expan> when it is not vsed 
it is made fast alonge by the staye, at the 
Bottome of the staye:
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gird(a)">Girt</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the Cabell is soe taught that vpon the
turninge of the Fidd the shipp cannot goe over
it, Then she will lye a Crosse the tyde &amp; then we
saye she is gyrt, <expan type="wch">which</expan> will instantly be vndone 
if the Cablell be Veered out slack;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="goring(a)">Goareinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">A saile is Cutt Goareinge when it comes sloopeinge 
by degrees, and is broader att the Clewe then at
the Eareinge. All Topsailes and Top gallant
</page>

<page no="76">
sailes are soe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="goose wing(n)">Goose wyng;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When wee are goinge before a wynd or quarter
wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> a faire fresh gale, wee many tymes
to make more hast, vnparcell the Missenyard
and soe Launch out the yard and saile over the
quarter on the Leeside: and so fittinge Guyes
att the Further end, to keepe the yoord Steddy
wth a Boome wee Boome out the sheate of 
the Missen saile, This doth helpe to give the
shipp some waye <expan type="wch">which</expan> otherwise the Missen 
saile will not especially before a wynd: This
saile soe fitted is called a Goose winge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="grapnel(n)">Grapnells;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are in nature of Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>s beinge vsed for 
Gullyes or Boat<expan type="es">es</expan> to ride by, only they differ in
forme for a Grapnell hath Fower floakes, and
never a stocke for it need<expan type="es">es</expan> none, beinge the wch
waye soeu<expan type="+r">er</expan> it fall two of the flookes do ever hold
by the gournd, In men of warr, we vse them
that are height to slinge into a shipp to catch
hold of some of her grating<expan type="es">es</expan>, Railes, Gunn walls
or the like And soe haveinge a Chayne made 
fast vnto it, we lash fast the shipps togeather
There are alsoe smale Grapnells <expan type="wth">with</expan> Three
hookes, but not broad like flookes <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee
vse to sweepe for hawseres, or smale Cabells;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="grating(n)">Gratings;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale ledges layd on a Crosse an other
like a percullis or a Prison gate; Those wch
are called the Grating<expan type="es">es</expan> are betwixt the 
Maine and Fore Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe serve for a close
feight and alsoe for the Soucco<expan type="+r">r</expan> of men either
in too hott, or too fowle weather, <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Tarr&shy;
pawlinge vpon them. There are alsoe in 
many places of the shipp Grating<expan type="es">es</expan> made
</page>

<page no="77">
for Ayre and light but Cheifely over Ordi&shy;
nance for the vent of the smoke of the Powder
wch comes out of the Touch hole, in feight;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="grave(v)">to Graue</form>
<xpln lang="en">Graveinge a shipp is bringinge her to lye
drye a ground, and then to burne off the old
filth and Stuffe <expan type="wth">with</expan> Reede, Broome, and the
like and soe to laye on newe: Some wee only
Tallowe, but that will quickly growe fowle 
Others tallowe &amp; sope <expan type="wch">which</expan> will alsoe <correction type="i">quickly</correction> growe
fowle The most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> and best is <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
trayne oyle, Roson and Brimstone boyled to&shy;
geather For this will last longest, Cleane,
The layinge on of this Stuffe is called 
payinge The shipp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gripe(n)">a Gripe</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Gripe of the shipp is the Compasse and
sharpeness of the Stem vnder water: especially
toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the lower <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te the vse whereof is, to make
ashipp keepe a good wynd; And therefore some tymes
when a shipp will not keepe a good wynd They 
putt to another falce st<expan type="e+_m+_">emm</expan>e to the true St<expan type="e+_m+_e+_">emme</expan>
to make her Gripe more;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gripe(v)">to Gripe,</form>
<xpln lang="en">We saye a shipp doth gripe, when she is apt
contrary to the Helme, to Runne her head or 
Nose into the wynd more then she should There
are Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly Two causes of this: The one when
a shipp may be too deepe a head; That her head
be not apt by reason of the weight <expan type="wch">which</expan> preses
her downe, to fall a waye from the wynd: The
other may be the stayinge of her Mast: For 
if she be ashort shipp and drawe much water;
If her Mass be stayed to much aftward on;
it will cause her head still to Runne into the
wynd The Fle<expan type="m+_i+_">mmi</expan>ngs beinge generally long
</page>

<page no="78">
floatie shipps, doe staye all their Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>aftward 
on very much else their shipps would never keepe
a wynde: For it is apparent to sense, that all 
sailes, from the Maine Mast, Aftward on; the 
farther Aft they stand, the more they keepe the
shipp to the wynd, as the head sayles the more
forward on they stand; The more they have power
to flatt the shipp about from the wynd;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gromet(n)">Grometts,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are little Ring<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made to the vpp
side of the yard, <expan type="wth">with</expan> staples <expan type="wch">which</expan> are dryven in&shy;
to the yard <expan type="wch">which</expan> have noe other vse but to tye &amp;
make fast the Caskett<expan type="es">es</expan> vnto them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="ground(v)" lexeme="grounding(n)">Ground &amp;
Groundinge,</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="aground(adv)">When a shipp is brought of purpose to be trymed
on the ground or otherwise: That is called 
groundinge of the shipp: There are Three
manner of layinge a shipp a ground; That is
either layinge her head vpward<expan type="es">es</expan> toward<expan type="es">es</expan> 
the Bancke, and her Sterne, toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the 
Offward; And is termed layinge her Pitch&shy;
Longtoo, This is vsed to shipps that are Cranke
wth the ground, for this waye they take the 
best advantage for a shipp to beare her selfe
The second is to laye her all allongst the 
shore, and to Heele her to the shore-ward; This
is vsed to shipps <expan type="wch">which</expan> have reasonable <correction type="i">good</correction> floares
and will beare them selves sufficiently well 
The third is layinge her a longst the shore,
And Heeleinge her to the Offward; This we
vse to shipps, <expan type="wch">which</expan> have greate <correction type="i">broad</correction> and longe 
flores, as Fleming<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> have standinge Streakes
The reason is for that otherwise wee should 
hardly come to her Keele: Some Sea-fareing
men, are very su<expan type="p-_">per</expan>sticious of goeinge to Sea
at Certayne dayes &amp; C<expan type="">ommo</expan>nly they hold it good
to begine the voyage of Sundaye, And there&shy;
fore <emend err="("></emend>to seeme to have begunn the voyage 
</page>

<page no="79">
<emend err="thoug">though</emend> they be not readie to goe, That daye 
they will way or (as the Terme is) trypp <expan type="y+e">the</expan> 
Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>, and goe a little waye and soe goe to 
Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan> againe; this they call breakeinge
ground;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ground timber(n)">Ground tymbers</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> are first layd vpon 
the keels, and soe bollted through the 
keelson into the keele, and are those <expan type="wch">which</expan> make
the floare of the shipp, and are therefore 
called ground tymbers, because the shipp - 
doth rest vpon these, when she lye a ground;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gudgeon(n)">Gudgins --</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those Irons <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made fast to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sterne
Post, and <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Pintills of the Rudders are
hanged to;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gull(v)">To Gull</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the Pinn of a Blocke doth eate or weare
into the Sheever, it is Called Gullinge, Alsoe
when a gard doth rubb a gainst a Mast, wee 
saye it will Gull the Mast, And therefore
to avoyd that we putt a platt made of Synnett
to the Middle of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> yard to keepe it from 
Gullinge the Mast;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="gunwale(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Gun-wale</form>
<xpln lang="en">That peece of tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> reacheth on either 
side the shipp, from the halfe decke to the 
Fore Castle beinge the vppermost Bend (as it
were) <expan type="wch">which</expan> finisheth the vpper workes of the Hull
there, and wherein they putt the Stannchions 
wch support the Wast-trees is Called <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Gunnwale,
whether there be anie gunns there or not: Alsoe
the lower <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of anie Porte where anie Ordi&shy;
nance doth lye is Called the Gunnwale;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="guy(n)">a Guy</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is anie rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is vsed to keepe a peece of 
Ordinance or anie thinge else, the Boate or
the like, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is hoised into the shipp from 
swinginge into the shipp to fast when it is over
</page>

<page no="80">
the Gunnwale to be hoysed in by them by this
Rope wee doe ease it in gentlie, and it is -- 
Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly made fast to the Stantions of the
Wast-trees: And that is called a Guy, wch
word I thinke comes from <etym lang="en">Guyde</etym> for this 
doth guyd it in: Alsoe there is a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is 
fastened to the fore Mast att one end and is
to the Pendant of the wyndinge Tackle; 
and soe reeved a gayne through an other 
wch is seased to the Fore Mast somewhat lower
then the first <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te: And this is to hale forward
the Pendant of the wyndinge Tackle, and 
this Rope is called a Guye 
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="81">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="H"><alpha>H</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="hail(v)" lexeme="hailing(n)"">To hale or Haleinge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Haleinge of a shipp is callinge to her to knowe
whence she is or whether she is bound or anie other
occasion, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is done Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly in these word<expan type="es">es</expan> &Ocirc; the 
shipp; or att sea noe more but, oh: and the other
then answers haie: These word<expan type="es">es</expan> are Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> to
Christianseamen, to hale either other in Alsoe
sometymes wee seeme to call to them or salute 
them <expan type="wth">with</expan> Whistle or trumpett<expan type="es">es</expan>, and this is 
Called halinge <expan type="wth">with</expan> trumpett<expan type="es">es</expan> or whistles;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="halliard(n)">Hale-yards</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are <expan type="y+e">the</expan> ropes by <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee hoise all the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> only <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
Crosse iacke, nor the Spritt saile yard have
None because they are ever slunge, yet in smale
Craft they have halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> spritt sayle yard<expan type="es">es</expan>,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="hand(v)">to Hand or Handinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">When they would deliu<expan type="+r">er</expan> awaye anie thinge to be
past to an other, or have it brought to them 
they saye, hand this a Waye, or hand me that,
or hand it a longe, Soe when they want men to
hoyse or doe anie labou<expan type="+r">r</expan>: They vse to Call for
more hand<expan type="es">es</expan>, not more men;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hand-speak(n)">a Hand-speeke</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is but a woodden heaver, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is vsed in steed of
a Crowe of Iron to Travers the Ordinance
but most especially, to the Wyndlas in the Boate
or shipp, <expan type="wch">which</expan> have wyndlases to heave vpp the
Anchor by;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="harping(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Harpings</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Harpinge of a shpp is the Breadth of a shipp
att the Bowe: Alsoe some call the End<expan type="es">es</expan> of 
the Bend<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are fastened to the stemme
the Harping<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hatch(n)">Hatches</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those loose <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> and as it were dorres of the
decke, <expan type="wch">which</expan> in the Midd shipp before the
Mayne Mast, that wee open to lett downe 
thing<expan type="es">es</expan> into the hold haveinge att each corner
a shackle of Iron to lift them by;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="82">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="hatchway(n)">Hatch-waye</form>
<xpln lang="en">By the Hatchwaye is ment <expan type="y+e">the</expan> place <expan type="p-_">per</expan>pendiculer
Over the Hatches: when they saye laye a thinke on
The Hatch-waye, that is on the Hatches;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="haul(v)" lexeme="overhaul(v)">to Haule or 
ouer Haule,</form>
<xpln lang="en">That <expan type="wch">which</expan> others Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly call pullinge a Rope 
the Sea faringe call over hauleinge or haule-
taught the Bowling<expan type="es">es</expan>: or haule in a Rope <expan type="y+t">that</expan>
hang<expan type="es">es</expan> wthout bord or the like in anie kinde
To over haule is when a Rope is over halled too
stiffe or taught: Then to haule the Contrary
waye that I was, hawled before to make it 
slacker;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hawse(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Hawses;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those greate round holes before vnder the
Head out of <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Cabells doe come when the
shipp is at an Anchor: A bould Hawse is 
when they lye high from the water: And this
is best when they lye lowe: If there be greate
Sea the Hawse will stilbe in the water, and
take in much water into the shipp Fresh the 
Hawse: That is when wee suspect that the 
Cabell is Fretted or Chaffed or is like (as 
many tymes it will) to burne it in the Hawse
(for the Cabell endures the greatest stresse)
Then wee Peere out a little, to lett an other <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t
of the Cabell endure the stresse Alsoe when
wee laye newe Platt<expan type="es">es</expan> vpon the Cabell in the
Hawse it is called Freshinge the Hawse, Cleere
the Hawse: That is when two Cabells, <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
come out att two Hawses, and by the wynding 
of the shipp haveinge some tornes one about 
an other Then the vndoeinge these turnes it
Cleeveinge the Hawse <expan type="wch">which</expan> is necessarie to be
done, or else the Cabells will Gall one an
other very much: Anie shipp or thinge <expan type="y+t">that</expan>
is Crosse afore the Hawse or layd a Crosse
the Hawse or when one shipp rid<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> her
sterne iust afore the others Hawse They
saye she rid<expan type="es">es</expan> vpon the Hawse;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="83">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hawser(n)">Hawser</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a three stand Rope and may be called a little
Cabell for that <expan type="wch">which</expan> is on shipps Hawser wilbe
an other shipps Cabell: Those doe serve for 
many vses, as to warpe the shipp over a Barr
The maine and fore shroud<expan type="es">es</expan>, are made of Hawsers
&amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> Only note the differrence of the makeinge
or sayinge is the Cause of the difference 
of the names: <expan type="wch">which</expan> to knowe, Vide <xref>Boyes</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="head(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Head</form>
<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Beake Head </xref> yett sometymes it is not 
exactly taken only for the Beakehead for 
sometymes they saye a head that is a bout 
the Foremast takeinge (as it were) all the fore
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipp for the head;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="headline(n)">Head lynes,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the Ropes of all sailes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are vpper 
most next the yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, by <expan type="wch">which</expan> the saile is made
fast vnto the yard<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="head-sail(n)" lexeme="overhaul(v)">Head sailes;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are all sailes belonginge to the Fore Mast
Spritt saile, and Sprittsaile Topp mast
for these are the sailes <expan type="wch">which</expan> govern the 
head of the shipp, to make it fall off, and
to keepe it out of the wynd; These Head
sailes in Quarter wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> are the Cheife 
draweinge sailes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="head sea(n)" lexeme="overhaul(v)"">Head sea:</form>
<xpln lang="en">When it hath binn agreat storme The 
wynd it may be will sodaynly alter Sixe 
poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> or more But the sea will goe 
the same waye it did for some houres 
Then if our Course lye to goe right against
the sea; as we maye the wynd beinge altered
we shall meete the Sea right a head, and 
soe call it a head Sea: Somtymes alsoe,
</page>

<page no="84">
when it hath binn but a little wynd there wilbe
a sea <expan type="wch">which</expan> will come contrary so the wynd; But
then not long after the wind will come that
waye, and doth shewe that on that poynt of the
Compasse when as that the Sea comes there
hath benn much wynd Note that generally 
before anie greate storme, the Sea will come
that waye before annie wynd, <expan type="wch">which</expan> shewes that
the sea out runns the wynd: The reason is
(I take it) for that the Sea begine a Continuat 
body one <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te begine moved The wynd doth
quickly infuse motion to the rest as wee see 
by the Circles <expan type="wch">which</expan> astone doth make when it
is throwne into the water Note in Head Seas
all short shipps are badd saylers for they beate
much against the sea but Longe shipps doe
goe more easilier for they will ride vpon 
Two waves at once, and falll more gently into 
the Sea:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="heave(v)">to Heaue,</form>
<xpln lang="en">As we Comonly vse the word flinge awaye
soe Sea men vse the word <emend err="flinge">heave</emend> a waye; for
if it be butt a Rope yarne or Chipp: They will
saye heave it a waye: Heave over bord, that
Rope or the like Alsoe the turninge about of 
the Capstaine is Called Heaveinge att the
Capstayne: Alsoe when a shipp att Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan> 
doth ride and fall <expan type="wth">with</expan> the waves they saye 
she heaves and sett<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="heel(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Heele;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">The heele of the Maine Mast, fore Mast &amp;
Missen is nothinge else but that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
Parde a waye a little slanntinge on the Aft
ward side of the Foote of the Mast like 
a heele to give the Mast leave to be stayed
aftward on; as the Fleming<expan type="es">es</expan> doe especially
</page>

<page no="85">
But the Heele of the Topp Mast are square
and in that they putt the Fidd of the Top Mast;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="heel(v)">to Heele</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is for the shipp to lye downe on a side; whether
she be a floate or around: And soe shee 
heeles much or little: She heeles to Starbord
or to Porte: Some su<expan type="p-_">per</expan>ticious Seamen 
when they take in good<expan type="es">es</expan> or victualls for a voyages
If by Chance in Stowinge the provision she
Heele to Starbord, will saye it is a signe of
a longe and badd voyage For then they 
will saye she heeles from Handward because
they take in all there good<expan type="es">es</expan> on the Larbord sid<expan type="es">es</expan>
But if she Heeles to Larbord It is signe of 
a good voyage, and some good<expan type="es">es</expan> to come in, when
she is a ground we saye she heels, to the Shore
ward or to the offward accordinge as it is;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="helm(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Helme,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that Peece of tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Helmes
man doth hold in his hand to Steere and
governe the Rudder by, and one end is 
made fast be the Head of the Rudder, But soe
as that it may be taken off, though the Rudder
be the Cause of the shipps workinge yet the
Helme is the instrument <expan type="wch">which</expan> governes the 
Rudder and therefore, we impute it all to
the Helme as when we saye the shipp 
feeles the Helme, or doth not feele the Helme
That is will worke and be governed by 
the Helme or not, For if a shipp be very fowle
or out of her trym, or too deepe, or to light, 
many tymes she will not feele the Helme, 
but saile as if she had none: Part the Helme,
Starbord the Helme amid ship <correction type="d" deleted="beinge altered"></correction>
or right the Helme Termes of Condinge to 
</page>

<page no="86">
Direct <expan type="wch">which</expan> way the Steeres man should putt
the Helme: In smaller shipps vnder the Rate
of Five Hundred Tunnes, They vse to putt
a ship to the other end of the Helme, and soe
Steere &amp; governe the Helme by that;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hitch(v)">to Hitch;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to Catch hold of anie thinge by a Rope to 
hold it fast, or <expan type="wch">which</expan> a hooke, As we saye Hitch 
the Fish hooke to the Flooke of the Anchor 
when we hoyse in the Boate, Hitch the Tackles
in the Ringe of the Boat<expan type="es">es</expan> or the Garnett<expan type="es">es</expan> in the
Sling<expan type="es">es</expan> That is Catchinge hold of it by the hookes,
to hoyse in the good<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hold off(v)">to Holde off</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when wee have the Cabell att the Capstaine if
the Abell be very stiffe and greate or else have
layne in slymie Oozie ground it surges and slipps
backe vnlesse that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te <expan type="wch">which</expan> is heaved in, be still 
halled a waye hard from the Capstaine, to keepe
the Capstayne Close and hard to the Cabell 
whelpes If it be a smale Abell men may doe it
in their hand<expan type="es">es</expan>, But if greate, then eyther they hold
off <expan type="wth">with</expan> Nippers or else (as in all greate shipps 
they doe) bringe it to the Geere Capstayne, and
this is called Holdinge off,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="honeycombed(a)">Ho<expan type="n+_">nn</expan>y Combd;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a Peece of Iron Ordinance either 
<emend err="either"></emend> by beinge ill Cast or <expan type="wth">with</expan> over much 
weareinge is Rugges and hath little holes in 
the Concave of the Peece, she is said to be 
hony Combd; This is very dangerous for
a Crosse barr shott to catch in or anie Rugged
shott, as alsoe some Ragg of the Cartridge or
peece of the wadd may sticke in it and soe
fire the Powder, that shall instantly be putt
in, And therefore wee refuse these peeces
as much as wee maye: To trye whether 
</page>

<page no="87">
a peece be Ho<expan type="n+_">nn</expan>y Combd wee putt in a Naile
or Crooked peece of wyer att the End of
a Staffe, and soe where that Catches, wee
knowe, she is Honny Combd, or light a Candell
att the end of a staffe, and that will shewe all
the imperfecc<expan type="">ions</expan>of the Peece;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hook(n)"><expan type="y+e">The</expan> Hookes,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Hookes of the shipp, are all these forked
Tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> are place vpright on the 
Keeles, both in the Racke and Runne of the 
shipp: These doe give the Narrowinge and
breadthinge of the shipp in those <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> accordinge
as they are framed, and they are Bolted into
the Keele: Those Compassinge tymbers wch
are before, and doe helpe to strengthen the 
ste<expan type="m+_">mm</expan>e and fore <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipp are called 
Brest hookes
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="horse(n)">a Horse,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast to one of the ForeMast
shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> a dead man-eye att the end of it
Through <expan type="wch">which</expan> is reeved the Pendant of the 
Spritt saile sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>, and is for noe other vse butt
to keepe the Spritt saile-sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> Cleere of the
Flookes of the Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>: Alsoe when a Man 
heaves the leade out of the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> There is 
a Rope made fast to the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> for him to leane
against for fallinge into the Sea: Alsoe they 
vse a Rope to sett taught the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> wall knot<expan type="es">es</expan>
one end made fast to the Shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> to the other 
the Lannyeres are bourght, and soe <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Land 
Speeke turninge it they sett, Taught the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan>
This is Called a Horse: Alsoe those little short 
ropes <expan type="w+ch">which</expan> are seised to the Middle of the Top mast
and Top gallant staye <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Blocke wherein
Bowling<expan type="es">es</expan>, are Called Horses;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hold(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Holde</form>
<xpln lang="en">All the Roome betwixt the Keelson and the First &amp;
lower deckes is called the Howld And it is where
all our victualls good<expan type="es">es</expan> and stores doe lye: yett
it is divided into seu<expan type="+r">er</expan>all Roomes <expan type="wth">with</expan> Bulkhead<expan type="es">es</expan>:
As the Steward<expan type="es">es</expan> romme, the Powder room The
Boatewaynes store, and the like Rumidge the 
Store, Stowe the hold, Cleere the Holde: Vide
<xref>The Proper Names</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hound(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Hounds</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the holes in the Cheekes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are fastened
to the head of the Maste wherein the Tyes doe
Runne to hoyse the yard; The Top mast<expan type="es">es</expan> have 
But one hole, a loft in the head of the Mast be&shy;
cawse they have butt single tyes: And this is alsoe
Called the Hound<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wholesome(a)">Holesom,</form>
<xpln lang="en">We saye a shipp is <correction type="i">a</correction> holsome shipp in the sea
when she will hull-trye and ride well att an 
Anchor wthout Rowelinge or Tumblinge and 
labouringe much in the Sea: Alonge shipp
wch drawes much water will Hull well, trey-well
and Ride well: If she should drawe much water
and be shorte, she may hull well, but neither 
trey-well, nor Ride at Anchor: If she drawe 
little water, and be longe, she may ride well, and
trey well, but not Hull well: If she be shorte &amp;
drawe little water, she will neither Hull, trey
nor Ride well: And therefore those are the
most vnholsome shipps: Note alsoe, that the
Rouseinge in or layinge off, of the vpperworkes
of a shipp doe much ease, or wronge her in
all these manner of workeinges: But however,
the over Cavveringe of her is bad for all, and
makes her more labour somethen other&shy;
wise she would bee;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="89">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="house in(v)">Howsinge in,</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="housing in(n)">When a shipp after she is past the breadth
of her beareinge, is brought in narrowe and
their vpper workes they saye that she is housed
in: Most are of oppinion that the houseinge
in of a shipp makes her the more holsome in
the Sea: Because the waight of her Ordinance
and her vpper workes doe not over hange the
Nailes <expan type="wch">which</expan> (as they suppose) would make the
shipp rowle the more But I am sure it 
takes a waye agreate deale of Roome: for
a Mann of warr: And the Tacke will neuer
come soe well a board, as when she is layd out
a lofte: I have soe much experience of both 
sort<expan type="es">es</expan>: That I am of oppinion if two shipps 
begiven (Ceteris paribus) A shipp <expan type="wch">which</expan> is layd
out a loft not Flayreinge off: But propor&shy;
tionable finished vpp to her other workes
shalbe the holsomer shipp, for that the Counter
poyse on either side, (The whole weight not soe
much overhanginge the <expan type="p-_">per</expan>pendicular of the
keele) shall keepe her more Steddy and make
her be<correction type="i"><doubtful type="u">come</doubtful></correction> longer in fetchinge over a seele: The
reason is the same, and will hold propor<expan type="">cion</expan> 
in a shipp to the walkinge of a Sunambulus
whoe <expan type="wth">with</expan> equall weight will goe much more 
sure: If his weight wherewth he doth steddy 
himselfe be att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of a longe staffe, wch
by reason of the greatenesse of the Circle, must
have alonger tyme to come over his Par&shy;
pendiculer, then if the same were in a short
staff or in a Lumpe togeather in his hand 
wch once inclyninge either waye, he hath 
nothinge where <expan type="wth">with</expan> to Souccour, and 
Counter poyse the weight;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="hoise(v)">to Hoise,</form>
<xpln lang="en">When they would hale vp anie thinge into <expan type="y+e">the</expan> ship wth
a Tackle or a dead Rope, or gett vp a yard they call
it Hoiseinge, or Hoise <expan type="y+e">the</expan> water in or Hoise vp <expan type="y+e">the</expan> yard<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="90">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="hull(n)">The Hull;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the very body or Bulke of ashipp <expan type="wth">with</expan> out 
Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, Ropes or saile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hull(v)">Hullinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when ashipp is att Sea, and hath taken in 
all her sailes: In a Calme weather, it is done
to save her sayles from beatinge out against
the Mast: But in fowle weather when they
<correction type="d" deleted="at"></correction> are able to beare noe saile: The manner
is noe more but takeinge in all the sailes
and tyinge downe the Helme to the Lee&shy;
side of the shipp, and soe if she be a good 
Condic<expan type="o+_n+_e+_">ione</expan>d shipp she will easily lye vnder the
sea: And thus she makes her waye one poynt
a fore the Beame: That is if the wynd be at
west and the shipp looke South, she will make
her waye East and by South, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is one 
poynt a fore the Beame, The Beame will
beare East and West It is not yett agreed
on by Seamen whether it be better for ashipp
to Hull <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Top Mast vpp or downe: The
most received oppinion is to have it downe, in respect
that generally they <correction type="d" deleted="suspect"></correction> suppose that the weight
a loft will make it seele the more dangerously in 
a storme But besid<expan type="es">es</expan> the experience, <expan type="wch">which</expan> I have 
seene to the Contrary I can give this Reason, whye
it is best in a dangerous and desperat storme to 
Hull <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Top mast vp, and all Seamen will 
Confesse, that the weather Seele, is the most 
dangerous seele, and therefore must graunt <expan type="y+t">that</expan>,
<emend err="that"></emend> it is most safest Hullinge <expan type="wch">which</expan> doth not p<expan type="+r">re</expan>vent
that danger of the Seele: If her Top mast<expan type="es">es</expan> be 
downe when she seele to Leeward, the lesse 
weight over Head she hath to hinder her from 
Co<expan type="m+_e+_">mme</expan>inge and Rowleinge backe over againe to
wynd ward The faster shee will Seele over
and the shorter, Soe that in meetinge the wynd&shy;
ward Sea soe short and soddainely it may 
indanger to breake in and founder her But
</page>

<page no="91">
if the Top Mast be vp she must need<expan type="es">es</expan> be the
longer a Comminge vp to wyndward and soe
neete the Sea <expan type="wth">with</expan> more Ease; That is <correction type="d" deleted="more"></correction>
have more leisure to breake a waye vnder
her: yett it is true shee will make the 
greater Lee saile But in that there is
noe danger though to an vnexperienced
man there may seeme to be;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="hullock(n)">Hullock;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of a saile <expan type="wch">which</expan> is loosed and left
open in a greate storme, when wee dare not have
anie more out And is only vsed to the Missen saile
when wee would keepe the shipps head to her
Sea, <expan type="wth">with</expan> a little saile makeinge all vpp exceptinge
a little at the Missen yard arme: Or else when
a shipp will not weather Coyle to laye her head
the other waye, wee loose (for that is the terme)
a Hullocke, of our fore saile, and soe Changinge
the Terme to the weather side, the shipp will
fall off and laye her head where her Sterne
was before
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="jeer(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Ieere</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a peece of a Hawser <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast to
the Maine yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, and fore yard<expan type="es">es</expan> Close to
the Ties of Greate shipps (for smale shipps
doe not vse it) and soe is Cleeved through a 
blocke <expan type="wch">which</expan> is seased Close to the Topp and
soe comes downe and is Reeved through an
other blocke att the bottome of the Mats close
by the decke; Greate shipps have one on on
side, an other on the other side of the ties:
The vse of this Rope is to helpe to hoise vp <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
yard But the Cheifest vse is to succo<expan type="+r">r</expan> the
Ties and to hold the yard from Fallinge 
downe, if the Ties should breake/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="jeer capstan(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Ieere 
Capstaine</form>
<xpln lang="en">This hath it<expan type="es">es</expan> name from the Ieere <expan type="wch">which</expan> is 
ever brought from the Capstaine to be 
heaved att by: It stand<expan type="es">es</expan> in the wast by the 
Hutch waye and serves for many other vses
As to Heave vpon the voyall, Or hold off
The Cabell from the Maine Capstaine;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="iron sick(a)">Iron sick</form>
<xpln lang="en">A shipp or boate, is said to be Iron sicke 
when the Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> or Speekes or Nailes 
are soe eaten awaye <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Rust of the 
Saltwater that they stand hollowe in the Planck<expan type="es">es</expan>
and soe the shipp doth receive in water by 
them And this is the Reason whye they putt 
Leade over all the Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> head<expan type="es">es</expan> vnder water;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="junk(n)">Iunke</form>
<xpln lang="en">Anie peece of Cabell, That is cutt off most
C<expan type="">ommo</expan>ly anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of an olde Cabell is 
called a Iuncke: such as these they hange
for fenders by the shipp sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, or else vntwist
it and make platt<expan type="es">es</expan> for Cabells, Rope yarne
and Synnett, If it be not to olde and Rotten
If it be olde then they make Ockham of it;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="93">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="jury mast(a)">Iury mast</form>
<xpln lang="en">When by occasion of storme or feight wee 
have spent or lost either the Fore Mast or
Mayne Mast, wee doe reserve (if it be possible)
the Mayne or fore yard <expan type="wch">which</expan> are putt downe
into the step of the Mast and soe fasten
it in the Parteners, and soe take the 
Myssen yard or if wee have anie other wch
serves for a wynd yard: <expan type="wch">which</expan> fittinge wth
sailes and Ropes in the forme of the other
wee make a shift <expan type="wth">with</expan> to steere and governe
the shipp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="keckle(v)" lexeme="keckling(n)">To keckle or Kecklinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">Wee vse this terme only to the Abell and the 
Boltrope, when we feare the Callinge of the Cabell
in the Hawse, on the Boltrope against the quarter
of the quarter shipp: wee turne a smale Rope 
Round about it: But in manner it differs not
from serveinge other Ropes: Though to these 
this servinge is Called Kecklinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Anchor</xref>;

</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="kedge(v)" lexeme="kedging(n)">To Kedge or Kedginge;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When in a Narrow River we would bringe
vpp or downe a ship the wynde beinge con&shy;
trary to the Tyde: Then they vse to <correction type="d" deleted="saye"></correction> sett
the Foresaile, or Fore Tope saile, and the 
Missen, and soe lett her drive <expan type="wth">with</expan> the tyde. The 
reason of vseinge these sayles is to fetch her
aboute if she come to neere the shore: Alsoe
they vse a smale Anchor in the head of the 
Boate, <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Hawser, and comes from the ship
wch Anchor they lett fall in the midle of the
streame if the shipp come to neere the 
shore, and soe wynd her head about by that;
and soe lift vpp the Anchor againe, when she
is about From this vse the Anchor is Called
a Kedger or Kedge Anchor;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="keel(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Keele</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the First Tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> is layd of <correction type="d" deleted="the"></correction> a shipp
and is the Bases whereon all the rest are fastened
and soe much is to be accompted the Keele as
doth lye in a straight lyne, att the one end whereof
is scarfed in the St<expan type="">emm</expan>e and att the other is lett
in the stem Post: To this are all the ground
tymbers and hookes for and Aft bolted and 
on them are all the vpper worke raysed, A
rancke Keele, is when a sheepe hath a deepe
</page>

<page no="95">
Keele And this is good to keepe a shipp from 
Rowlinge: For if aFloatie shipp rowle to much,
that hath but a Showle keele, wee putt to an
other Keele vnder the First to make it deeper
for that will make it take some more holde in
the water and this wee call a false Keele;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="keel rope(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> keele Rope</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> runnes a longst the shipp vpon
the Keele, wthin the tymbers of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> ground tymb<expan type="+r">er</expan>s
one end comeinge oute before the other Abast
Some will have this of Base Rope: But the 
best is a haire Rope for lastige: The vse of it
is to Cleere the lymber holes, when they are
Stoaked <expan type="wth">with</expan> Ballast or anie thinge else soe as 
the water <expan type="wch">which</expan> lyes betwixt the tymbers cannot
come to the well of the Pump;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="keelson(n)">Keelson</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the lowest peece of tymber wthin the ships
hold, <expan type="wch">which</expan> lyes all allonge vpon the ground tym&shy;
bers, right over the Keele, Through <expan type="wch">which</expan> are 
driven, the Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe fasten the Keelson
Groundtymbers and the Keele togeather;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ketch(n)">Ketch</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale Boate such as vse to come to Billing<expan type="es">es</expan>&shy;
Gate <expan type="wth">with</expan> Mackrell Oysters &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>;/
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="kennel(n)">Kenells;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale peeces of tymbers nailed to the

</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="kenk(n)">Keenke,</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> should runne smoothe in the Blocke
weare; this wee call a Keenke: Alsoe the same
is in a Cable, if it R<expan type="u+_n+_">unn</expan>e out doubleing in that 
</page>

<page no="96">
manner, <expan type="wch">which</expan> happens either by ill quoylinge of the
Cabell, or by lettinge it runne out too fast: But if
it be perceived it is remedied by over settinge the
Cabell, else the Cabell will Gall very much in that
place;/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="knave line(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Knaue Lyne</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope the one end fastned to the Crosse trees
vnder the Maine or fore top, and soe comes downe
by the tyes to the Ramhead, vnto <expan type="wch">which</expan> there is seased
a smale peece of Billet some two foote longe 
wth a hole in thend of it, in <expan type="wch">which</expan> hole this lyne is 
Reeved and soe brought to the shipps sides &amp; hauled
vpp taught to the railes, The vse whereof is to 
keepe the Tyes and Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> from turninge 
about on an other, <expan type="wch">which</expan> beinge neere they would doe
were it not for this line But after the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan>
and tyes are stretched a while, it is taken a waye
and noe more vsed but on like occasion;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="knee(n)">Knees</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those Crooked tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> are soe called
In respect they represent a mans knee bowinge
These doe bynde the Beames and the Futtockes
togeather beinge bolted into both of them: Some
doe stand a longst shipps, and some right vp
and downe, you may easily know them in Part
where they are vsed by the forme of them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="knittle(n)">Knittles</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are Two Rope yarnes twisted togeather
wth a Knott att each end, to sease a Rope or
Blocke or the like;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="knight(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Knights,</form>
<xpln lang="en">There is a Maine Knight and a fore Knight
One standinge Aft the Maine, the other Abast
The fore Mast vpon the Second decke being
</page>

<page no="97">
fastbolted to the Beames: A Knight is 
a peece of tymber, wherein are 4 Sheevers
Three for the Halliard, and one for the Topp&shy;
rope to runne in, when they are hoised: They
are Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly Carved <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Picture of 
some head vpon them, by <expan type="wch">which</expan> they are easily 
knowne;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="knot(n)">Knotts,</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are tow sort<expan type="es">es</expan> of Knott<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are vsed
att Sea, The one is a Bowlinge Knotte <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
soe made that it will not slipp nor slide, <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
the Knott The Bowlinge bridles are made fast
to the Greengles, but it is alsoe vsed many other
wayes, The other is a wale Knott <expan type="wch">which</expan> is a Round 
Knott or Knobb made <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Three stond<expan type="es">es</expan> of
a Rope soe that it Cannot slipp the Tacke
Topp saile sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> and Stoppers have the wale
Knott, and many other Ropes
</xpln>
