<page no="98">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="L"><alpha>L</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="labour(v)">to Labour,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Wee saye a shipp labours in the Sea when 
she rowles and tumbles very much either a Hull
vnder saile or att an Anchor, A shipp rowles
most a Hull when it hath benn a growne storme 
and soddenly the wynd ceases, but the Seas
continue greate still, Then she will rowle for
want of wynd vnder saile a shipp Rowles most
right before a wynd, but beat<expan type="es">es</expan> most vpon a head
Sea: soe that some shipps are most dangerous
to putt afore the sea in a greate storme, and 
weake shipps dangerous to beate against the head
sea; Att an Anchor shipps rowle and Labour 
most when they lye betwixt wynd and tyde, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
vpon the Turninge of the tyde, when they wynd
and the tyde are Contrary, and neither hath
gott power to make her straine her Cabells
to ride <expan type="wth">with</expan> her head either to wynde or tyde;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ladder(n)">Ladders</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are Three vsuall ladders belonginge to
a shipp: The Enteringe Ladder in the wast
A ladder of Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> hang<expan type="es">es</expan> out of the Gallery
for fowle weather, and att sea, to come out of
the Boate or goe into it, And one at the 
Beake-head <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast over the Boltspritt
to gett vp vpon the Boltspritt by, The Venetians
and most Levant shipps, and alsoe Spanish 
Gallions have Ladders <expan type="wch">which</expan> goe into the Top 
and come downe abast the tyes, for they seldome
goe vp by the Shroud<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="lade(v)">To Lade</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to fill the shipp <expan type="wth">with</expan> good<expan type="es">es</expan>, or provision
for when the hold is full they saye shee hath
her ladeinge: Alsoe to Charge a Peece 
of Ordinance is to Lade the Ordinance
</page>

<page no="99">
Alsoe some saye lade the water out of the Boate/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ladle(n)">a Ladell,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that where <expan type="wth">with</expan> wee putt the powder into a Peece
of Ordinance when wee take the powder out 
of a Gudg Barrell we neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> vse these in feight
vnles wee have spent all our Cartrages, for
they are both troublesome and dangerous for 
scatteringe of Powder, and not soe speedie,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="landfall(n)">Landfall:</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is as much as the fallinge <expan type="wth">with</expan> the land as thus:
If wee saye wee shall see land such a day, and
that it fall out soe iust accordinge to our 
recconyng, we saye we have made agood 
land fall, or if wee mistaken, Then we made a 
badd Land fall;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="landlocked(a)">Land locked,</form>
<xpln lang="en">When we are in anie Roade or Harbor, soe
that the land lye round a bout vs, and the
sea not anie poynt open vpon vs, wee saye we
ride Land locked: These are ever good Road<expan type="es">es</expan>
and Harbo<expan type="+r">or</expan>s for noe sea cann come in to wronge
the shipp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="land to(n)">Land too,</form>
<xpln lang="en">By this is ment iust soe farr off a sea, as
wee cann see the Land: As when wee direct
on to lye off att Sea in the height of the Cap&shy;
land too, That is soe neere, and soe farr off
as he may even iust see, and discover the land
and noe neerer;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="land turn(n)">A Land-turne</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the same of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> same of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> land as a Breize off <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sea
Only differinge <expan type="y+t">that</expan> the land turne Comes by night
&amp; <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sea turne or Breize by daye: Vide <xref>Breeze</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="100">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="langrel(n)">a Langrell,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a loose shott, <expan type="wch">which</expan> goes in <expan type="wth">with</expan> a shackell to bee
shortned when it is putt into the Peece, and
to Flye out att length when it is discharged wth
a halfe Bullett either of leade or Iron at eigher
end: This is good shott neere hand to vse out
of our Ordinance to Cutt downe Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, yard<expan type="es">es</expan>
Ropes and sayles, alsoe it will doe much, e<expan type="">xecuteon</expan>
A mongst the men a loft: But it is not to be
vsed twixt wynd and water, for it will not
peerce a good shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="laniard(n)">Lanniers</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the smale Ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> arer Reeved in the
dead men-eyes of all the shrouds &amp; Chaines and
the vse of them is either to slacke, or to sett
taught the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan>: Alsoe all the stayes 
belonginge to anie Mast whether they have 
Blockes, or deadmen-eyes belonginge to them
are sett taught by Lanniers Alsoe the smale
Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> makes fast the Topper of the 
Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> to the Halliard is called a Lannyar;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="large(a)">Large,</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp goes neither by a wynd nor 
before a wynd but as it were twixt both 
<expan type="y+t">that</expan> is quarteringe, and such a wynde that
carries her soe, we call a large wynde;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="lace(v)" lexeme="lacing(n)">to Lase or Laseinge</form>,
<xpln lang="en">Is the pro<expan type="p-_">per</expan> terme for puttinge to the Bonnett
to the Course, or <correction type="d" deleted="to"></correction> the drabler to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Bonnett
As lase on the Bonnett: Alsoe wee saye lase
the Nettinge to the Roofe trees, or wast trees;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="lash(n)" lexeme="lasher(n)">a Lash or Lashers;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When wee bynd vp anie thinge to the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>
or Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, as Pikes, Muskett<expan type="es">es</expan>, or Butt to the
Mast or the like, as, fishes and spare topp mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
wthout bord, wee call it Lashinge too But the
</page>

<page no="100">
Lashers cheifely are those Ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> bynde fast
togeather the Tackles &amp; Breeching<expan type="es">es</expan> of the
greate Ordinance when they are halled 
wthin bord The reason is because the 
Breeching<expan type="es">es</expan> cannott be halled vpp taught
by hand: Therefore the Rope is brought 
a bout the Breeching and Tackles alittle
before the Carriage right vnder the Peece
and soe Lashes them fast together;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="lashing(adv)">Lashinge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Note that wee saye a shipp goes Lashing
Veereinge, wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> large &amp; Roomier it
is in a manner all one, for then she neither 
goes by a wynd nor before;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="latchet(n)">Latchetts,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale lynes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are sowen into the 
Bonnett<expan type="es">es</expan>, and drabler like Loopes where<expan type="w+th">with</expan>
They lase the Bonnett to the Course or the
drabler to the Bonnett puttinge them into
the eilettholes and soe laseinge them one over
an other;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="launch(v)">Launch;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">This word is vsed in steed of putt out: As we
saye Launch a shipp out of a docke or out
of the Key Launch the Boate: Launch out
or Launch in the davett, Launch out the 
Capstaine Barrs Alsoe in an other sense
when they have hoised vp a yard high enough, or
the Topmast: They crye Launch hoe, That is
to hoise noe more, Alsoe in Stoweinge the holde
They will say Launch forward
when they would have a Butt or the like brought
forward or Aftward on; Alsoe when they are:
Alsoe in Pumpinge if the Pumpe suckes: 
Then they Cry Launch hoe; That is Pump noe
more;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="102">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="lay(v)">layd</form>
<xpln lang="en">When wee are sayled out of sight of a land, soe that
wee cannot see it, we saye wee have layd the
land But if it be soe that some other poynt of
land doe hinder vs from seeinge it, Then wee say
That we have shutt it in, or shutt it into the other
Poynt;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="leak(n)">a Leake,</form>
<xpln lang="en">There is noe shipp soe right but that <expan type="wth">with</expan> the
laboureing in the Sea, nay though she ride in
harbour will not make some water; But we say
a shipp is Leake when she makes more water
then is ordinarie <expan type="wch">which</expan> is some hundred swakes in
24<expan type="+tie">tie</expan> or 48 hours: The causes of Leakes are either
the startinge of some tree nailes, the openinge
of the Seames, the Eateinge of the wormes,
or else by receiveinge some shott vnderwater
The wayes of stoppinge are but two, either wthin
bord <expan type="wch">which</expan> Cann hardly bee if the Leake be lowe
amongst the ground tymbers or the Hookes 
But then the best reamedy is to drive downe
tallowe and Coales mingled together, Rawe
beefe, Otemeale bagg<expan type="es">es</expan> or the like if it cann
be Come att, then it is easily stopped <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
lead: If it be <expan type="wth">with</expan> a shott, Then they drive in
a Plugg <expan type="wth">with</expan> some Canvas about it, The
other is wthout bord, where it is easilie 
stoped, if it be not to lowe by heeleinge the 
shipp over on the other side and soe nayling
lead to it: But if it be lowe then to stitch 
a Bonnett, or a nettinge <expan type="wch">which</expan> is better <emend err=")"></emend> <expan type="wth">with</expan> long
rope yarnes opened and soe suckinge it vnder
the Keele to bringe it a gainst the Leake The
midraught of the water will suck in the 
Ockham and soe stopp her self: But this
will not Continue longe: When a shipp is
Leake the Terme is she hath sprunge a Leake
or she makes much water;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="103">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="ledge(n)">Ledges,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the smale peeces of tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> come 
thwart shipps from the wastrees, to the Ruff 
trees, to beare vpp the Netting<expan type="es">es</expan>, and soe if
there be a gratinge over the halfe decke,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="lee(n)">Lee,</form>
<xpln lang="en">This word is many wayes vsed: But generally
the Lee is vnderstood for that <expan type="wch">which</expan> is oposite to
the wynd: The Lee shore, That is the shore
against <expan type="wch">which</expan> the wynd beares, yet to be vnder
the Lee of the shore is to be Close vnder the
weather shore, That is whence the wynd
doth come, A Lee the Helme, That is put
the Helme to the lee side of the shipp: In 
Condinge they vse to call to him att the helme
have a Care of the Lee latch, That is to
looke that the shipp goe not to the Lee ward
of her Course A Leeward shipp is one that is 
not fast by a wynd, and doth not make her 
waye soe good as she might, To come by the Lee
or to laye a shipp by the Lee, is to bringe her 
soe that all her sailes may be a gainst the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
and shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> flatt and the wynd to come right 
on her broad side, soe that the shipp will lye as
it were starke still, Or if she make anie waye
it wilbe <expan type="wth">with</expan> her brod side right <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Beame
The manner of Bringinge a shipp by the Lee
if she have all her sailes a broad is to beare vp
the helme hand to windward. let rise the fore
tacke, and veere out the Maine sheate and 
take in the Missen or Peele it vpp <expan type="wch">which</expan> is 
called Spellinge the Missen;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="lee fang(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Lee fangs</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Reeved into the Greeyles of
the Courses when wee would hale in the Bottom
of the saile to Lase on the Bonnett, In a stronge
gale, they serve alsoe to helpe to take in the 
saile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="104">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="leach(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Leetch,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Letch of a saile is the outward side or skirte
of the saile form the earinge to the Clewe
The midle betwixt <expan type="wch">which</expan> is especially to be 
accompted the Leetch;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="leach line(n)">Leetch-lynes,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale lynes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are fastened to the Leetch
of the Topp sailes (for they belonge to noe
other sailes, and are Reeved into a Blocke
at the yard Close by the Topp saile tyes: The
vse whereof, is when the take in their topsaile
to hale in the letch of the saile, and Note they
ever hale the Lee Leetch lyne first, for then 
the rest will come in <expan type="wth">with</expan> more Ease;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="leg(n)">Leggs,</form>
<xpln lang="en">They are called the Legg<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Martnett<expan type="es">es</expan>
and are smale Ropes putt through the Bolt<expan type="es">es</expan>
of the Martnett<expan type="es">es</expan> Ropes, of the maine and 
fore saile, in the Leetch of the saile <emend err="meere">neere</emend>
a foote of length, and soe att either end, beinge
spliced into them selves; They have alittle
Eye whereinto the Martnett<expan type="es">es</expan> are made wth
Two hitches, and th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd seised to the standing
parte of the Martnett<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="let fall(v)">Lett fall,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Phrase generally vsed for the putting
out of anie sailes, when the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> are aloft
But not if the Maine yard<expan type="es">es</expan> or fore yard<expan type="es">es</expan> be
stricken downe, soe as that the sailes may
be loosed, before the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> be hoysed But 
most properly It is vsed to the Maine 
saile Fore saile and spritt saile for Top&shy;
sailes, the more proper terme is heave
out the Top sailes, because they doe lye in the 
</page>

<page no="104">
<emend err="">Topp &amp;</emend> to the Missen saile, we saye Sett the
Missen and not lett it fall;/
</xpln>
<lemenote>
The catchword on the previous page, "Topp &amp;", is not carried over.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="lift(n)">Lifts,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> belonge to the yard Armes
of all yard<expan type="es">es</expan> and doe only serve to top the 
yard Armes That is to make th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> hange higher, or lower, or even as we 
lift But the top saile lift<expan type="es">es</expan> doe serve for sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>
to the Top gallant yard<expan type="es">es</expan> aswell as for lift<expan type="es">es</expan>
in the Top saile yard<expan type="es">es</expan>: The haleinge of 
them is Called Toppinge the lift<expan type="es">es</expan>, as Topp
a Starbord or Top a Porte, That is hale
vpon the Starbord or Larbord lift<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="limber(n)" lexeme="limber hole">Lymber or
Lymber holes</form> <xpln lang="en">Are little square holes Cutt into the bottom
of all the ground tymbers and hookes, next
to the Keele, and right over the Keele about
3 or 4 inches square: The vse whereof is to
lett the water passe to the well of the Pump
wch else would lye betwixt the tymbers, 
vnto these is putt the Keele Rope,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="linch pin">Lins Pinns,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are only vsed a boute the Truckes of the Carri&shy;
ages, to keepe on the Trucke vpon the Axeltree
beinge little Iron pinnes, iust the same that
keepe on Coach wheeles;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="locker(n)">Lockers;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Anie little boxes or as it were Cubbord<expan type="es">es</expan> wch
are made by the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> to putt in shott
by the Peeces or in anie other places, are
by a C<expan type="o+_m+_o+_n+_">ommon</expan> name Called Lockers, we have
them to every peece, to have shott lye ready
if on the sodaine wee should have occation;
</page>

<page no="106">
But in feight the shott lyes not there, but in a 
Rope made like a Ringe <expan type="wch">which</expan> lyes flatt vpon the
decke, soe that the shott cannot doe soe much
hurt if an other shott should light amongst it;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="log line(n)">a Log lyne,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Some call this a Minut lyne: It is a smale lyne
wth a little peece of a board at th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd <expan type="wth">with</expan> a little
lead to keepe it Edglonge in the water: The
vse of it is That by iudginge how many faddome
this Runnes out in a Minut to give a Iudgment,
how many Leagues the shipp will runne in 
a watch for if in a Minut there runn out 14 faddom
of lyne, Then they conclude that the shipp doth
runn a Myle in an houre 60 the nomber of 
Minut<expan type="es">es</expan> in an houre beinge Multiplyed by 14:
The nomber of Faddomes make iust soe many
paces as are in a Mile, soe acco<correction type="i">r</correction>dingly as in
a minut, there runnes <correction type="i">out</correction> more or lesse, they doe 
by Iudgment, allowe for the shipps waye: But
this is a waye of noe certainetie vnlesse the
wynde and Seas, and the Course would con&shy;
tinue, all one, besid<expan type="es">es</expan> the Erro<expan type="+r">or</expan> of turninge the
glasse, and stoppinge the lyne both att an
instant, soe that it is rather to be esteemed 
as a trycke for a Conclusion, then anie solid 
waye to ground vpon: The manner of doeing
it is one stand<expan type="es">es</expan> by <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Minut glasse, whilst an
other out of the Gallery lett<expan type="es">es</expan> fall the Logg 
iust as the logg falls into the water the 
other turnes the glasse, and iust when the
glasse is even out he Cryes stopp Then 
he stopps and reccons how many faddome
are runne out, and soe gives his Iudg<expan type="m+_t+_">ment</expan>,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="107">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="loof(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan>Loofe,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">The loofe of the shipp is Counted that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
a loft of the shipp <expan type="wch">which</expan> ties iust before the
Chestrees, as farr as the Bulke head of the
fore Castle, And therefore wee call these
peeces of Ordinance <expan type="wch">which</expan> lye there the 
loof peeces: Loofe vp, a Terme in Conding
the shipp to have him keepe her neerer the
wynd, Loose into a Harbour That is to keepe
Close to a wynd, and soe goe into it, Keepe
your Loofe, That is to keepe Close by a wynd,
To springe ons loofe, That is when a shipp is
Goeinge large, to Clap close by a wynd;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="loof hook(n)">a loofe hooke</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a tackle <expan type="wth">with</expan> two hookes, one to hitch into 
A Creengle of the Maine and fore saile, wch
Creengle is in the Boltrope of the Leetch of
the saile yet farr a bove the Clewe, and the
other to hitch into astrapp <expan type="wch">which</expan> is spliced into
the Chestrees, and soe to Bowse downe the 
sailes: The vse whereof is to soccor the Tacke
in a greate gale, that all the force and stresse
may not beare vpon the Tacke, And alsoe
it is vsed when wee would sease the Tacke 
surer or the like;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="loom(v)">to Loome</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Loomeinge of a shipp is as you would saye 
the very prospective of a shipp for the word
is vsed in this sense: A shipp loomes a greate
saile: That is shee seemes to be agreate shipp:
shee Loomes but smale That is shewes or 
seemes to be but a little shipp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="loom gale(n)">a Loome Gale</form>
<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Gale</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="lust(n)">a Lust,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp heeles alittle to Starbord or Port
We <emend err="saue">saye</emend> she hath a Lust that waye: Though this
</page>

<page no="108">
happen by stowinge her hould vnequally: But
most properly a shipp is said to have a lust to
one side or other, when out of her owne mould &amp;
makeinge she hath a kinde of inclina<expan type="">cion</expan> to one
side, more then to the other, <expan type="wch">which</expan> happens by the 
vnequall carryinge of the workes, or it may be
the vnequall <correction type="i">carryinge</correction> waight of tymbers 
for it is a great matter to carry a shipps 
workes soe even, but that there shalbe some smale
differrence I have seene the Experience in
many shipps that beinge equally Ballasted, 
They would Carry a greater saile, slope lesse,
and go better vpon Tacke then vpon the 
other;/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="lie under the sea(v)">Lye vnder <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
sea:/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When in a storme wee are a Hull and 
make fast the Helme a Lee: soe as the sea 
doth breake vpon the Bowe and Broad side 
of the shipp, we saye she lyes or is <correction type="i">layd</correction> vnder the 
Sea
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="109">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="M"><alpha>M</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="man(n)">A Mann,</form>
<xpln lang="en">We saye a shipp is well manned when she hath
men enough to vse the Ordinance Tryme the
sailes, and plye a Convenient nomber of smale
shott besid<expan type="es">es</expan> Cururgions, Carpenters, and 
some to hand a Longe Power, and other men
that are necessarie, but not <emend err="not"></emend> feighters: I 
meane soe <correction type="i">as</correction> that men beinge appoynted to 
their Charge shall only intend that: Though it
be true that a Man may stepp from 
a gunne to a Rope, or from a Rope to vse a 
smale shott and the like, and therefore it may be
thought there should not neede soe many: yet
I would have those thing<expan type="es">es</expan> done as workes of
Superarroga<expan type="">cion</expan>, not as beinge forst vnto
them, for if necessitie then require whilst the 
sayles are a Tryminge the Ordinance and 
smale shott must lie still: what inconvenience
the want of sufficient mannage is in a Mann of
warr they can best tell whoe have bene experienced
in that laboursome feight att Sea; <expan type="wch">which</expan> many 
tymes doth not only last a daye, but two or three
For myne owne <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te though I might well be 
ashamed not to knowe and dare to doe asmuch wth
a fewe men as anie other, yet to speke my Con&shy;
science, and tell my minde Cleerely: were
I worthy to Co<expan type="m+_a+_n+_">mman</expan>nd the King<expan type="es">es</expan> shipps in anie
service, I would rather have 20 men to many
then Tenn to Fewe. A Marchant Man is 
Counted well Manned, when he hath double soe
many men as would else barely saile his shipp
yett Comonly they loose their shipps rather
for want of Menn then desire to saue them 
selves For though for a while he may defend 
himselfe yett the Mann of warr wilbe sure of 
him if he can have Sea Roome and tyme 
enough, when they would have Men to goe heave
at the Capstayne: They saye Man <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Capstaine
Alsoe when shipps meete and desire to hewe
All their men They are Commannded to
</page>

<page no="110">
come all vp a loft, and this they call Maninges
the shipp: Soe when men are Commannded to 
goe into the Top well That is a sufficient nomber
of Men to goe into the Top to take in the saile
Alsoe Man the Boate;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="man-of-war(n)">A Man of Warr</form>
<xpln lang="en">I doe not meane to discribe what a Captaine
or Man is, whoe is a Man of warr: But a 
shipp of warr amongst Seamen makeinge
vse of the figure Metonemia Continens pro 
contento. These qualities Comodities and 
Condic<expan type="">ions</expan> I require in a shipp <expan type="wch">which</expan> I would should
be a right Brave Man of warr, she must
saile well <expan type="2+ly">secondly</expan> be Roomy betwixt the deckes <expan type="3+ly">thirdly</expan> 
Flush <expan type="wth">with</expan> out anie Falls for hingeringe Men 
to passe too and Froo at Ease, She must beare
out her lower Tyre all reasonable fittinge 
weather <expan type="wch">which</expan> if she doe the lower she carryes
them the better: Her Chase and Bowe, must
be well contrived to shoote as many peeces
right forward, and bowinge as may bee, 
for those <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> come to be most vsed in feight; 
Her Ordinance not to lye right over <correction type="i">on</correction> an other,
but soe as that vpon the least yawe of the 
Helme, one Peece or other may ever come 
to beare, and Lastly she must beare a stoute
saile: Such a shipp well manned <expan type="wth">with</expan> men 
Convenient to plie their Ordinance handle 
the saile and vse some smale shott, were
worthy to be called a Man of warr: That
shipp <expan type="w+ch">which</expan> want<expan type="es">es</expan> anie of these is like a soldier
whoe should want either a hand or a legg or 
an arme;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="manger(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Manger;</form>
<xpln lang="en">A place made <expan type="wth">with</expan> Planckes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are 
fastened vpon the decke right vnder the 
Hawse, some foote and a halfe high and 
som tymes it is made like a Tryangle 
meetinge att the outward Angle Aftward
</page>

<page no="111">
either Abast or before the Mast, The vse whereof
is only to receive the water <expan type="wch">which</expan> Comes in 
att the Hawses when the shipp rid<expan type="es">es</expan> att Anchor
in greate stresse, the water should not runne
aft in the deckes and soe in hold as it may 
Some ships whose Hawses lie high and that
doe ride Easily in the Sea neede them not, but
other have much vse of them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="marling(n)">Marling</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale lyne made of vntwisted hemp to be more
gentle and plyant then other lynes, and it is
alsoe tarrd: The vse whereof is to sease th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd<expan type="es">es</expan>
of Ropes for fasninge out: Alsoe they vse to 
sease the sid<expan type="es">es</expan> of the straps, att the Arse of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> 
Block<expan type="es">es</expan> together <expan type="wth">with</expan> this; Alsoe if a saile be
ript out of the Boltrope, Then if they have
haft or it be cold weather soe as they cannot 
sewe it in, They take Marlinge, and <expan type="wth">with</expan> that
putt through the Eylett holes they make fast <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
saile to the Boltrope: This is called Marlinge
the saile;/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="marling spike(n)">a Marlinge Speeke;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale speeke of Iron made of purpose 
for the splyceinge together of smale ropes 
and alsoe to open the Bolt rope when they 
sewe in the saile,
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="martnet(n)">Martnetts,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale lynes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are fastened to the Legge
on the Leetch of the saile, and seeme like Crowe
feete, the fall beinge Reeved through a Blocke,
and the Topmast head, and soe come downe
by the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> to the decke; The Martnett<expan type="es">es</expan> of <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
Top sailes are in the same manner, to the head
of the Top gallant Mast, but their fall Comes
noe lower then the Top where it is halde, when
they are to hale the Martnett<expan type="es">es</expan>: The Terme is
Top the Martnett<expan type="es">es</expan>: The vse of them are to 
</page>

<page no="112">
bringe that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Leetch of the saile <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
next to the yard Arme vp Close to the yard; when
wee farthell the saile: These most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly 
belonge to the gwo Courses yet many greate 
shipps, have them to the Top sailes &amp; Spritt sailes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="mast(n)">Masts,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Mastinge of a ship is of much importance to the
sayleinge and Condic<expan type="">ions</expan> of a shipp, For if she be over
Masted either in Length or bignesse, it will overcharge
the shipp and make her lye downe to much by a wynd
and labour too much a Hull, if she be vndermasted: That 
is to smale or too short, Then she looses the benefitt
and advantage of spreadinge soe much <correction type="i">more</correction> saile to give
her waye. There are some differences in the pro&shy;
por<expan type="">cion</expan>inge of Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> accoringe to the vse of the
shipp, for those <expan type="wch">which</expan> are to goe longe Voyages are
not to be Masted accordinge to true propor<expan type="">cion</expan> 
but ot be made shorter and bigger then ordinary
for feare of spendinge them in longe iourneyes 
where they cannot be repaired: But the rule and
waye whereby wee give the true propor<expan type="">cion</expan> 
but to be made shorte and bigger then ordinary 
for the length of anie Mast is to take 4/5 of 
the breadth of the shipp, and that multiplied 
by 3 shall give the true number of feete that the
Maine Mast shalbe in length. The bignesse to be
one Inch to a yard in length, if it be a Maine 
Mast: For Example take a shipp whose breadth
is 30 foote, fewer Fifths of 30 foote, are 24 foote,
soe as I saie that the shipps Mast Must be 24
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> longe for every yard is 3 foote and 24
inches through alloweinge one inch to every 
yard: The fore Mast is in Length to be 4/5 of
the Maine Mast <expan type="wch">which</expan> wilbe Twentie yard<expan type="es">es</expan> lacking
one 4/5 part of a yard and 20 inches through 
The Bolt spritt is ever the same In length 
and thicke <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Fore-Mast the Missen
Mast to be halfe the length of the Mayne Mast
</page>

<page no="113">
wch wilbe xij yard<expan type="es">es</expan> longe and Twelve inches 
through. And soe this is the true propor<expan type="">cion</expan>
of the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> of a shipp <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Thirtie foote
broade at the Beame: For as wee take the
propor<expan type="">cion</expan> of the length of our yard from the 
Keele soe doe we take the propor<expan type="">cion</expan> of the 
length of her Maste form the beame or breadth;
of a shipp; A longe Maste is termed a Tawne
Mast, a shott Mast is termed a lowe Mast;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="mat(n)">Matts</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are Broad clowt<expan type="es">es</expan> weaved of Synnett and
Thrums together, The vse whereof is to save
thing<expan type="es">es</expan> from Gallinge and are vsed in these
places to the Maine and Fore yard<expan type="es">es</expan>: Att the
Ties to keepe the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> from gallinge against
the Mast vpon the Gunnwall of the Loose
to keepe the Clewe of the saile from 
Gallinge there vpon the Bolt spritt, and 
Boate head, to saue the Clewe of the fore saile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="metal(n)">Mettle</form>
<xpln lang="en">By speakinge of the Mettle of a Peece of
Ordinance is C<expan type="">ommon</expan>ly ment, not the qualitie
butt the quantitie of that Mettle whereof it is 
made: As to disspert the Mettle Vide <xref>dispert</xref>:
when they saye the Peece is layd vnder Mettle
That is <expan type="wth">with</expan> her mouth lower then her breech: And
contrary she lyes over Mettle if her Mouth lye
higher then her Breech: and if she lye Playne
Poynt Blancke, then they sayes she lyes right
wth her Mettle, Soe that it seeme because
the Breech hath most Mettle, they do more 
singulerly attribute the word Mettle, they doe more 
then anie other, If a Peece have much Mettle
in anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te: They saye she is well fortified there
and soe Contrary &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="mizen(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Mysen,</form>
<xpln lang="en">when wee say the Missen it is ment that wee 
speeke of the saile, not of the Mast, As wett the
Missen, that is fitt the Missen saile, Charge the
Missen; That is bringe the yarde to the other side
of the Mast, and soe the Tacke to the other Board
And soe Peeke the Missen: That is putt the yard
right vpp and downe by the Mast: Speele the Missen
That is lett goe the sheate, and Peeke it vpp; The
vse of the Missen is to keepe the shipp Close 
to a wynd Note if a shipp grype to much then 
we vse noe Missen, for then she will neuer keepe 
out of the wynd: Somtymes alsoe wee vse the - 
Myssen when we are att an Anchor to backe 
the shipp a steerne to keepe her from fowlinge
her Anchor vpon the turninge of the tyde Some
tymes alsoe wee trey <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Missen: Some 
greate longe shipps require two Missons: Then
they call that next the Maine Mast, the Mayne
Missen; that next the Poope the Bonauenture
Missen;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="moor(v)">To Moare or 
Moaringe</form>
<xpln lang="en">To moare a shipp is to laye out the Anchore 
as is most fitt for thte shipp to by in that place
where she is: For there are these kind<expan type="es">es</expan> of 
Moaring<expan type="es">es</expan>: First to Moare a Crosse or Thwart
wch is to laye on Anchor on on side a River and
the other on the other right against it, soe as
both Cabells either for Ebb or Flood may 
</page>

<page no="114">
<emend err="">beare</emend> togeather Next to Moare a longst That is to
laye an Anchor right in the Midle of the 
Streame on a head, and the other a Stearne
and this is where they feare dryveinge a shore
For then both the Cabells, will beare togeather
if she falle in vpon either shore The third is
Moareinge a water shott: That is as <expan type="y+u">you</expan> would
saye quarteringe betwixt both: For this is
neither a Crosse the tyde nor a longst the
tyde when they come into anie place, they
<expan type="p-_">per</expan>ceive where, <expan type="wch">which</expan> waye and vpon what poynt
of the Compasse the wynde &amp; sea is like to 
endanger them most, And soe iust there 
they laye out an Anchore, And this is called
Moareinge, for west, North west, or as the 
poynt is: Note that a shipp is not said to be
<emend err="Moare">Moared</emend> <expan type="wth">with</expan> lesse then Two Anchors aground;
yet is she have but one aground and a Hawser
a shore, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Called a Provisoe we saye 
she is Moared <expan type="wth">with</expan> her head to the shore;/
</xpln>
<lemenote>
The password on the previous page, "beare togeather", not completely carried over.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="mount(v)">Mounting:</form>
<xpln lang="en">Mountinge a Peece of Ordinance is taken
in Two sort<expan type="es">es</expan>: That is either to putt them vpon
and into the Carriages: As wee saye the 
shippsOrdinance are not Mounted, That is
not on their Carriages, or else when they are
on the Carriages, and the Mouth of her lye 
to lowe for the Marke: Mount (we saye) 
The Peece higher: But if she lye <expan type="wth">with</expan> her
mouth to high for the Marke wee say lett 
fall the Peece a little, and not dismounte the
Peece: For to dismount the Peece is to take
her out of the Carriage, As in feight when 
a shott hath taken or broken a Carriage we
saie, The Peece is dismounted;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="monk's seam(n)">Munck seames,</form>
<xpln lang="en">This is a kinde of sewinge the Canvases of the
sailes together, when the edge of the one is 
</page>

<page no="116">
sewen over the edge of the other and soe it is sewen
to both sid<expan type="es">es</expan> this is the strongest waye of sowinge
the sailes;/ 
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="murderer(n)">Murderers;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale Iron or Brasse peeces <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
Chambers in Marchant men, they are most vsed
at the Bulkhead of the fore Castle, Halfe decke
or Steere reach, and they have a Pintell 
wch is putt into a Stocke, and soe they stand &amp;
are Traversed, out of <expan type="wch">which</expan> they vse Murderinge
shott to skower the deckes, when men enter 
But Iron Murderrers are dandgerouse for
them <expan type="wch">which</expan> discharge them, for they will skallde 
extreamely and endanger their eyes much wth
them I have knowen divers hurt <expan type="wth">with</expan> shootinge
them off
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<form lexeme="kneel to(v)">Neale too</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is when it is deepe water close to the Shore

and downe wthout anie Showleinge;/
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="neap(n)" lexeme="neap-tide(n)">Neapes or 
Neape-types;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the Moone is in the Middest am of the 
Second and last quarters, then wee have 
Neape tyd<expan type="es">es</expan> the Etimoligie of the word, I 
knowe not, but the meaninge of it is this: The
Neape is opposite to the springe, and there
are as many dayes allowed for the Neape 
or fallinge of the tydes as are for springe 
or Ryseinge of the tyd<expan type="es">es</expan>: These do cause that
where it doth not ever flowe, high enough, we are
forced to staye for the Launchinge and Rouinge
and alsoe for the goeinge over some Barr vntill
the springe Note in Neape tydes the water
is neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> soe high, nor soe lowe as in the springe
tyd<expan type="es">es</expan>: Alsoe the tyde never Runnes soe swift
in Neapes as it doth in spring<expan type="es">es</expan>: Note That as
the highest of the springe is Three dayes after
the Full or Change of the Moone, and soe the
lowest of the Neape is Fower dayes before the
full or Change, and then wee saye it is dead 
Neape; when ashipp lackes water soe that
it doth <emend err="it doth"></emend> not flowe high enough to bringe
her off the ground or out of a docke, we saye
she is be neaped: Soe if a Shipp be wthin a 
Barrd Harbour that there lacke water to 
Carry her over till the springe, wee say she
is Be neaped;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"> 
<form lexeme="needle(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Needle</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that Iron wyre <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast to the fish of
the Compasse and is that <expan type="wch">which</expan> gives the <correction type="d" deleted="mon"></correction>
mo<expan type="">cion</expan> to it beinge touched <expan type="wth">with</expan> a loadstone
The best for to receive and retayne <expan type="y+e">the</expan> vertue
of the stone are made of Steele: And <expan type="y+e">the</expan> best forme
is to make them round <expan type="wth">with</expan> two smale poynt<expan type="es">es</expan>
directinge to the North, and South for in this
poynt they doe most equally poyse the Flies;
</page>

<page no="118">
whoe would vnderstand more of this, let him
reade <expan type="D+r">Doctor</expan> Barlowes booke of the Loade stone 
where all thing<expan type="es">es</expan> belonginge to the Needle are 
most exactly and Compendiously sett downe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="netting(n)">Nettings</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those smale Ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are seased together
wth Rope yarnes in the forme of a Nett <expan type="wth">with</expan> Mashes
and are for the most <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te vsed in the wast: yet
I have seene flemying<expan type="es">es</expan> have netting<expan type="es">es</expan> over all
from the Topp of the Fore Castle over the
Poope, and are stretched from the ledges wch
Are placed from the wastrees, to the Roofetrees
In Marchant men, it is Cheifely vsed haveinge
a saile laid over it for to shaddowe their 
men, and for a Close feight But I thinke 
they are in an Error, for it is most dangerous
for fireinge Of smale defence, if men enter 
beinge quickly cutt downe, And beinge once
Torne downe, as it may easily <expan type="wth">with</expan> smale 
Grapnells, Cloye all the wast; In a Man
of warr it is good to have them for the pleasure
and Ease of the Company, but not to vse them
in feight;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="nipper(n)">Nippers;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale Ropes a boute a faddom &amp; ahalfe or
two faddome longe, <expan type="wth">with</expan> a little truck, at one
end, or some have only a wale knott; The vse
whereof is to hold off the Cabell from the
Maine Capstaine, or the Ieere Capstaine
when the Cabell is either soe slymie or soe 
greate, that <emend err="that"></emend> they cannot strayne it
to holde it off <expan type="wth">with</expan> their hand<expan type="es">es</expan> only;/

<form lexeme="observe(v)">to Obserue</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to take the hight of Sunn or Starr wth
anie instrument, whereby wee knowe in what
degree of Latitude the shipp is I neede not
saye much of this, for it would require many 
lynes, and it is taught in every booke of Naviga<expan type="">cion</expan>;/
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ockham(n)">Ockham</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is noe thinge but old Ropes, or others, vntwisted
and soe puld out, as it were into Loose hempe
againe; Alsoe towe and flaxe beinge soe 
imployed about a shipp is called white Ockham
The vse where of is to drive into the Seames, in
all <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> alsoe where they suspect water maye
come in: As the head<expan type="es">es</expan> of Tree nailes. white 
Ockham is best to drive first into the Seame
But Tard-Ockham is best for <correction type="d" deleted="drive first"></correction> the
outside of the seame next the water,when it
is Rowled vp soe, as when the Caller drives
it in it is called a Thred of Ockham;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="offing(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Offinge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">By this is ment as much as to saie out in the
open sea, from the Shoreward; as if I be in a 
shipp, the ship on one side me, and on the other 
side to Sea-bord an other Shipp. she is in the 
Offinge, soe if a shipp be saileing into the
Seaward froward<expan type="es">es</expan> the shore, wee saye she 
stand<expan type="es">es</expan> for the Offinge: Or when a shipp (as in 
our Channell, keepes in the Middle of the 
Channell, and comes not neere the shore
Wee saye she keepes in the Offinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="offward(n)">Off:ward</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a terme vsed when a shipp is a shore and 
keeles to the water ward fromward<expan type="es">es</expan> the shore,
she keeles then (they saye) to the Offward: Or
if her Sterne lie toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the Sea, we saye
her Sterne lyes to the Offward and her 
head to the Shoreward;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="120">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="orlop(n)">Orlopp</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Orlopp is noe more but the decke, as we
saye the lower decke, or the Second decke
soe you may aswell say the lower Orlopp
or the Second Orlopp, and indeed it is 
C<expan type="o+_m+_o+_">ommo</expan>nly held the proper speech to Call them
the first Orlopp and the Second Orlopp, for
the word Orlopp <emend err="seeme">seemes</emend> to be appropriated
only to these Two deckes: For if a shipp have /
Three deckes, they neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> call the vppermost wch
is the Third by the Name of Orlopp. but by 
the Name of decke: As to speake of them they
will say she hath a Tyre of Ordinance in the First
and Second Orlopps; and alsoe vpon the vp<expan type="p-_">per</expan>
decke, for if there be a third decke it is neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> 
called by the name of Orlopp, but by the<correction type="d" deleted="rd"></correction> vpper
or third decke;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="overset(a)">Ouer sett,</form>
<xpln lang="en">When ashipp att sea, <expan type="wth">with</expan> beareinge to much
saile, is borne over on a side, and soe founder in
the sea, wee saye she was over-sett; Some
tymes <expan type="wth">with</expan> an extraordinarie winde the shipp
may be oversett, <expan type="wth">with</expan> noethinge but the power
wch the wynd may have over the Hull especially
if the wynde and Current goe contrary I have
heard some say: that disimboguinge out of the
Indies, the Cap Florida where the Current
ever sett<expan type="es">es</expan> very stronge to the Northward
That if they have mett <expan type="wth">with</expan> a gust att the
North: The wynde haveinge power over
the Hull of the shipp aloft, and the 
Current settinge to wyndward haveinge
power of the Hull alone: They have
been in greate danger of Founderinge
alsoe the turninge over of anie greate 
Cable or smale Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is quoyled vpp
Is called oversettinge, as over-sett the Cabell
&amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="121">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="overthrow(a)">Ouer throwe,</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp is brought to be trymed a ground
doth fall over on a side, we call it over throwne
and not over-sett, the reason whereof is her
want of Floure to beare her vpon and some
tymes it may happen by the indiscretion of
those whoe bringe the shipp a gorund Keeleinge
to the Offward if the Ground be too steepie
when as they should keele her to shore-ward:
To p<expan type="+r">re</expan>vent <expan type="wch">which</expan> we waye butt to
Shore her vpp <expan type="wth">with</expan> her Top Mast, and yard;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="out licker(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Out Licker</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale peece of tymber, some two or 
three yard<expan type="es">es</expan> longe, as they have occasion to vse
it, and it is made fast to the Topp of the Rope,
and soe stand<expan type="es">es</expan> right out a sterne: Att the
owtwardmost end there is a whole into it wth
the standinge <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the sheate is made fast
and soe beinge Cleeved throught the Blocke of
the sheate is reeved a gaine through an 
other Blocke, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is seased to this peece of
tymber neere th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd, and soe the vse of this
is to hale downe the Missen-sheate to it; This
is seldome vsed in greate shipps; But the
caue whye in anie shipp it is vsed, is because
the Missen Mast is placed soe farr Aft: 
That there is not roome enough wthin bord
to hale downe the sheete flatt, and soe are
forced to vse this wthout board: The smale
French Burtons and Allowmers doe vse 
this most of anie shipps <expan type="wch">which</expan> I have seene 
And generally all New Castle Carvells
have them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="ooze(n)" lexeme="oozy(a)">Ooze or Oozie,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a soft slymy muddie ground, This is noe good
ground to ride att Anchor in for <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>s
will not hold here in greate stresses; But
the best waye to make them hold is to skewe
them, and in some places that will not serve
</page>

<page no="122">
neither, besid<expan type="es">es</expan> this is very bad ground for the
Rottinge of Cabells, If a Man would have 
a ship lye a gorund it is best layinge her
in Ooze, for there she will lye very soft &amp;
easie, for she will quickly docke her self there
It is very badd for Rottinge the Plancke and
the Ockham <expan type="wch">which</expan> is in the seames;
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="123">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="P"><alpha>P</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="paunch(n)">a Pantch</form>
<xpln lang="en">Those Matt<expan type="es">es</expan> made of Synnett <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made fast
to the Maine and fore yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, to saue them from
gallinge against the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> are called the 
Pauntches by a proper name;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="parbunkle(n)">Par buncle</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope vsed in the nature of a paire of 
sling<expan type="es">es</expan>: It is a Rope seased togeather att both
end<expan type="es">es</expan>, and soe putt double a bout a Caske to hoise
it in by, and the hooke of the Runner is Hat&shy;
ched into it to hoise it in: This is the quickest
waye of slinginge the Caske, but not soe sure
a way for slyppinge as the sling<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="parcel(v)" lexeme="parcelling(n)">Parcell or 
Parcellinge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to take a little Canvas a bout the breadth of
a hand, and soe laye it over, a seame, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
first Caulked, and it is most Co<expan type="m+_o+_">mmo</expan>ly vsed
alongst the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> over the Cabbin, on
the Quarter decke, or over the Masters 
Cabbin, then heate a little Pitch and Tarr
very hott and power vpon the Canvas, And
all this togeather is called Purcellinge a 
seame;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="parcel(n)">Parcells</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those thing<expan type="es">es</expan> made of Truckes and Ribbs 
and roopes <expan type="wch">which</expan> goe a bout the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> and are
att both end<expan type="es">es</expan> mad fast to the yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, and are soe
made <expan type="wth">with</expan> Truckes and Ribbs; That the yard
may slide vpp easily: These alsoe <expan type="wth">with</expan> the 
Brest ropes doe hold the yard close to the 
Mast;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="partner(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Partners,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> are bolted to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Beames
and doe compas and shoote in the Mast at
the decke, and are the Strength that doe
keepe vp the Mast steedie in the shipp, and
</page>

<page no="124">
alsoe that it should not rowle out the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan>
There are <expan type="p-_">par</expan>tners alsoe at the second decke
in the <emend err="name">same</emend> nature, but the Missen hath but
one paire of Partners: The Mast doth vse 
to be wedged fast in these from stirringe or
wagginge: yett I have seene some shipps that
would not saile well vnlesse the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> were
loose and as they terme it had leave to playe
in the Partners: But in a storme it is 
dangerous to lett the Mast have anie playe
for feare of wronginge the Partners, wch
if they should give waye to, there is noe 
reamedy butt to Cutt the Mast by the 
Bord;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="passarado(n)">a Passarado</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is anie Rope wherewth wee hale downe <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sheate
Blocke of the Maine and fore sailes when
they are haled Aft the Clewe of the Maine
saile to the Cubbridge head of the Maine Mast
and the Clewe of the fore saile to the Catt&shy;
head, and this is done when the shipp goes
Large
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="paul(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Paule</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a little peece of Iron bolted to one of
the Beames on the decke close to the 
Capstaine, but soe easilie that it hath 
leave to turne a boute; This is it against
wch the whelpes of the Capstaine doe
beare when they would have the Cap&shy;
staine kept fast from turninge back 
a gaine, and therefore they say have 
a Paule; Tha tis have a little more, that
the Paule may katch hold of the whelpes
This is very necessarie some tymes 
when they heave vpp the Anchor in a 
greate Sea gate, to holde fast least the
force of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sea, yerkinge vp <expan type="y+e">the</expan> shipp soddainly
should flinge men from the Capstaine This
is called Paulinge the Cap staine &amp; when they
would have <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Capstaine goe backe they say
vnpaule the Capstaine;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="125">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="pay(v)">to Paye</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the same that Parcellinge is, only wantinge
the Canvas for wee call it payinge a seame
when after it is Caulked wee heate Pitch to
laye vpon the seame to keepe it from being
peerced <expan type="wth">with</expan> the water: wee alsoe vse noe 
Tarr to this: Alsoe when wee grave a shipp, we
call the layinge one of the stuffe what err it be
(Rosen, Bruisson, Oyle or the like) Payinge her
For they say paye her vp to the Bend<expan type="es">es</expan>:
Paye this her or the like: Alsoe when a shipp
is to Tacke, and that all the sailes are a Back&shy;
staues, That is flatt a gainst the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> and
Mast soe that we are sure she will not fall
back a gaine we say the ship is Payd: And
then wee lett rise the Tacke and Hale the sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>
and soe come to lay her head the other waye;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="peak(n)">a Peeke</form>
<xpln lang="en">To heave a Peeke is to heave the Hawse of <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
shipp right over the Anchor, soe that the Cabell
is then aright perpendiculer betwixt them 
To ride a Peeke is to heave the Maine yard 
and fore yard hoysed and soe one end brought
downe Crosse to the shroud<expan type="es">es</expan> the other beinge 
raised vpp and soe they are donne to contrary
sid<expan type="es">es</expan>: The Starbord, yard arme of the Maine
yard Comeinge downe to the Starbord side, 
and the Larr bord of the Fore yard: Soe the 
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> seeme to Crosse one another like <expan type="St">Saint</expan> 
Andrewes Crosse: The manner of doeing
it is, for the Maine yard, lettinge goe Starbord
Top saile sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>, and Toppinge vp the Larbord
Lift<expan type="es">es</expan>, and soe Contrary wise for the fore yard
to Ride a Bonnett, Peeke is to ride in the 
same manner but the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> must not be but 
halfe Mast high; They neu<expan type="">er</expan> likely Ride in this
manner <expan type="wth">with</expan> their yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, but in a River:
The reason, least rideinge <expan type="wth">with</expan> their yard<expan type="es">es</expan>
a Crosse some ships might by Chance Come
fowle off them, and Breake their yard<expan type="es">es</expan>
</page>

<page no="126">
Alsoe the Roome, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is in a shipps holde from <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
Bitt<expan type="es">es</expan> forward on to the Stem is called the Peeke
or Fore Peeke of the shipp: In the King<expan type="es">es</expan> shipps
the Powder is placed there: Marchant men 
place their Victualls there outward bound;
But other men of warr <expan type="wch">which</expan> are full of men 
Will vse it to lodge some of their Companie;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="pendant(n)">Pendants,</form>
<xpln lang="en">A pendant is a short Rope made fast att one
end, either to the head of the Mast, or to a yard
or to the Clewe of the saile, and are in bignesse
accordinge to the place where they are vsed, 
haveinge att the other End a Blocke <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Sheever
to Reeve som runninge rope into it, As the 
Pendant<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Tacke <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made fast to 
the head of the ast; And soe the Pendant<expan type="es">es</expan>
of the Back-staies <expan type="wch">which</expan> are there made fast, and
hange a little waye downe, on the Inn side of the
shroud<expan type="es">es</expan>: Generally all the yard Armes, excepting
the Missen have Pendant<expan type="es">es</expan>, into <expan type="wch">which</expan> Braces are
Reeved, and by them are easilie knowne Alsoe they
call those Callers <expan type="wch">which</expan> are hunge out in the yard
Arme or from the head of the Mast for a shewe
To beautifie the shipp Pendant<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="pillow(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Pillowe</form>
<xpln lang="en">That tymber whereon the Boltspritt doth 
beare and rest on at the Cominge out of the 
hull of the shipp abast <correction type="d" deleted="from"></correction> by the St<expan type="">emm</expan> is 
called the Pillowe of the Boltspritt;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="pintle(n)">a Pintell;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale Iron Pinn <expan type="wch">which</expan> is fastened to 
Murderers as alsoe to harquabushes - a crooke
wch is putt into a sockett or anie hole to keepe
the Peece from Recoylinge: Alsoe those 
Iron Pinns <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made fast to the Rudder
And doe hange the Rudder, to the Sternepost
</page>

<page no="127">
putt into Iron sockett<expan type="es">es</expan> are called Pintells of the
Rudder;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="pitch(v)" lexeme="pitching(n)">Pitchinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is not only layinge Pitch vpon anie place, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
yet more properly called payinge; But it is
taken alsoe for the placeinge of the stepe of the
Mast, as they saye the Maine Mast is Pitched
a little to farr aft: That is stand<expan type="es">es</expan> and is placed
to farr toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the sterne of the shipp but it
is not meant by the head hanginge too farr 
aft: Soe the fore Mast is Pitched to farr 
forth: That is the shipp stand<expan type="es">es</expan> too farr forward
on: Alsoe if a shipp fall much into a sea, they say 
she Pitches much into the Sea, or if she beate
much against the sea, soe as to endanger her 
Top Mast <expan type="wth">with</expan> the stroakes, They saye she will
Pitch her Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> by the Bord;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="plat(n)">Plats</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are flatt Ropes made of Rope yarne woven
one over an other, and are for to save the 
Cabells in the Hawse from Gallinge; Alsoe
wee vse them on the Flookes of the Anchor
to save the Pendant of the Fore sheate from
gallinge against the Anchor
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="plot(n)">Plott</form>
<xpln lang="en">And Sea-card is all one Vide <xref>Card</xref>: To pricke
a Card is to note downe the Travers of the shipps
waye, and soe compareinge it to yo<expan type="+r">ur</expan> observa<expan type="">cion</expan>
findinge it where the shipp is to make a smale
Pricke in the Plott in that Latitude and 
longitude where you suppose the shipp to be,
and soe still keepeinge accompt of the dayes 
you shall still see how neere or farr off you
are to the place <expan type="wch">which</expan> you saile to;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="128">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="point(n)">a Poynt,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The sharpness of anie head land is called the Poynt
of the land, when they saye that two poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> are one
in an other: That is they are soe iust in a right
lyne from vs and betwixt the other that wee cannot
see the one from the other: Alsoe the Compasse is
divided into 32 poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> representinge 32 wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> Soe
that wee call sayleinge by the Compas, sayleinge
vpon a poynt: They vse alsoe to vnder the strond 
at the End of a Cabell some two foote longe, and 
soe make Synnetf of the <correction type="i">Rope</correction> yarne, and laye them 
one over an other againe, and make it lesse toward<expan type="es">es</expan>
the End, and soe att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd make them all fast <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
a peece of Marlinge or the like, This is called poynt-
inge the Cabell: The vse whereof if to keepe the
Cabell from fasinge but cheifely, to see that none
of the End be stollen of and Cutt a waye;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="poop(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Poope,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The <correction type="d" deleted="Rope"></correction> Poope of the shipp is the vpper 
most <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te a sterne of the shipp hull, and is the
decke over that <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Co<expan type="m+_o+_">mmo</expan>nly the Masters 
Cabbin/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="port(n)">a Port,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that place out of <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Ordinance are putt
through the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, and these are to be made
soe large that the Ordinance may have leave
to Travers as much bowinge, and quarteringe
as may be: About 30 inches is the ordinarie rate
for a demy Calveringe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="port(v)">to Port;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a word vsed in Condinge the shipp when she is
right before a wynd or if the weather sheate
be iust as farr as the Bulke head <expan type="wch">which</expan> is 
more then quarter wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> but not right afore
They will vse the word steedy a Porte or
Steedy a Star bord the shipp heeles to Port
</page>

<page no="129">
Bringe thing<expan type="es">es</expan> neere to Porte or the like termes
easily vnderstood;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="pouch(n)">Powches,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale Bulkehead<expan type="es">es</expan> made in holde either
Thwart ships or longst ships, Those whoe carry
Corne or anie such good<expan type="es">es</expan> that will shoute over 
from one side to the other will make many <correction type="i">Powder</correction> Bulk&shy;
head<expan type="es">es</expan> or seu<expan type="+r">er</expan>all Roomes, as it were to keepe it vpp
and these are called Powches: Alsoe when we
Carreene shipps, There are smale Bulkhead<expan type="es">es</expan>
made of some distance from the Keelson on
either side the holde <expan type="wch">which</expan> serve to keep vpp
the Ballast when wee shift it either for the 
rightinge or bringinge downe of the shipp
when she is vpon the Careene and those are
called Powches,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="powder(n)">Powder,</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are two sort<expan type="es">es</expan> of Powder The one
Serpentine powder <expan type="wch">which</expan> Powder is dust (as
it were) wthout Corninge, and this wee neuer 
vse att sea in Ordinance or smale shott: Both
because it is of smale force: And alsoe for that
it will <expan type="wth">with</expan> the ayre of the Sea quickly dye and
loose his force, The other is Corne Powder 
whereof there is two sort<expan type="es">es</expan>, Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> Powder wch
is a greate Corne, and not very stronge, The
other Muskett powder, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is the Finest, strongest
and best we cann gett: The ingredient<expan type="es">es</expan> wch
make the Powder are, First Salt Peter wherein
the force of the Powder Consist<expan type="es">es</expan> next Brimstone,
wch is apt to Flame and once flamed causeth
the Salt Peter to flame, last Cole <expan type="wch">which</expan> is apt
wth anie sparke to kindle, but not to flame yet
doth maynteyne the Flame of the other two 
The best salt Peter is that <expan type="wch">which</expan> hath noe fatt
The best Brimstone wthout drosse, and the best
</page>

<page no="130">
Coale that is made of the highest wood I only 
touch some cheife thing<expan type="es">es</expan> of this because there
are divers books concerninge many Conclusi&shy;
ons touchinge the effect<expan type="es">es</expan> of Powders in all 
kind<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="powder room(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Powder roome,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that Roome in holde where wee laye the Powder
The greatest care in placeinge the Roome must
be to have it farthest from the vse of anie fires
and first from the danger of shott,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="predy(adv)">Preddy</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a word vsed a mongst them in steede of 
readye: As when wee come to feight the Comm&shy;
annders them make the shipp redye, make the
Ordinance preddy; That is make them ready to
vse in feight: A Preddy shipe: That is when the
deckes are Cleere, and the Ordinance and all 
thing<expan type="es">es</expan> fitted for feight: Alsoe make the holde
Preddy, is to laye thing<expan type="es">es</expan> out of the waye soe as 
They may stowe the good<expan type="es">es</expan> in Commodiously 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="prow(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Prowe</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the fore parte of the shipp considered aloft
and not alowe betweene the deckes or in hold
to Eare in the Prowe is to enter in the fore castle
The Prowe peeces are those <expan type="wch">which</expan> the aloft before
Goe forward into the Prowe, that is goe into 
the fore Castle, before the fore Mast And it is
most truly vnderstood for that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te <expan type="wch">which</expan> is betwixt
</page>

<page no="131">
the Chase and loofe;/

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="prime(v)" lexeme="priming(n)">Pryminge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is soe fillinge, and fittinge the Touch holes, <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
fine drye powder &amp; puttinge in a Pryminge
Iron to peirce the Carthage, soe that, that
Powder beinge fired the Powder in the Car&shy;
thrurd may likewise fire too, For if the 
Powder in the Touchhole be fired and the rest
wth in the Peece goe not off, we saye she was
not Prymed, or not well Prymed: For this 
there is Powder made very smale and 
extraordinarie drie; And the Gunner hath it
in a greate horne at his girdle in feight; <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
the First ground or Colons <expan type="wch">which</expan> is layd on in
Paintinge the shipp for other Colours to be
laid vpon is called Pryminge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="pudding(n)">Puddings,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are ropes nayled round to the yard Armes of
the Maine and fore yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, Close to th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd, and soe
in 3 or 4 or more a distance, one from an other 
vpon each arme,: The vse of them is to save the
Robbins from gallinge a sunder vpon the yard
when wee hale home the Topsaile, sheates: 
Alsoe the servinge of the ringe of the Anchor
wth ropes to <correction type="d" deleted="serve"></correction> save the Clinch of the 
Cabell, from gallinge against the Iron is called
the Puddinge of the Anchor;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="pulley(n)">Pullies,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale Blockes <expan type="wth">with</expan> one or two sheevers 
in them, and may either be Called soe or by the
name of smale blockes: for greate Blockes are
not vsually called by the name of Pullies, as the
Pullies of the Topsailes, Brases, Clewe lynes
Mast=Nett<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<page no="132">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="pump(n)">Pumps,</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are Three sort<expan type="es">es</expan> of Pumpes vsed in shipps 
The First and most C<expan type="o+_m+_o+_n+_">ommon</expan>, are ordinarie Pumpes
such as are vsed a shore, and those doe stand by <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
Maine Mast The next is a Burr Pumpe <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
not vsed in English ships But Flemming<expan type="es">es</expan> have 
them in the sid<expan type="es">es</expan> of their ships, and are called by 
the name of Bildg Pumps, because they have
broad longe floares <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe hold much Bildg&shy;
water; The manner of these is to have a staffe
some 6 or 7 Foote longe, at th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd whereof is a 
Burr of wood where to the leather is nayled 
and this doth serve insteed of the Boxe: And
soe two men standinge right over the 
Pumpe doe thrust downe the staffe, to the
middest, whereof is seased a Rope longe 
enough for 6: 8. or. 10. or more to hale by
and soe they Pull it vpp and drawe water
This Pumpe doth deliu<expan type="+r">er</expan> more water then
the Former and is not soe laborious to 
Pump att: The Third and best are chayne
Pumps, These deliu<expan type="+r">er</expan> most water, and <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
most ease for the Companie, and are 
soonest mended, if anie thinge faile having
spare Esses, if anie chance to give waye; 
These have a Chaine full of Burrs, and 
a wheele <expan type="wch">which</expan> makes it deliu<expan type="+r">uer</expan> soe much and
goe soe Easilie; the terme is for Pumpinge
to Pumpe a spell, and at ordinarie and 
Burr Pumps they Recckon a stroake, as to
saye a spell of Two hundred stroakes: But 
at Chaine Pumps the Spells goe by 
glasses: The Pumpe suckes, That is 
drawes wynd and hath noe water that 
comes to it: There are alsoe Pumps made
of a Cane or else of Lattine <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee putt downe into the Caske to Pump vpp the 
drinke, for at Sea in holde wee vse noe
Sickett<expan type="es">es</expan>
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="purchase(v)">to Purchase,</form>
<xpln lang="en">We Call the cayninge of Cuminge a Rope
by our haleinge of it in <expan type="wth">with</expan> ou<expan type="+r">r</expan> hand<expan type="es">es</expan>, or 
heaveinge it in att the Capstaine or other-
wise purchasine as the Capstaine doth 
purchase a pace, That is drawes in the Cabell
a pace, or the Tackels doe purchase, and 
the Contrary when we cannot gett in anie
or hale it a waye, we saye we cannot 
purchase <expan type="wth">with</expan> that Rope, Tackle or the 
like: Note that the more <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> that anie 
Tackle, Halliard, or the like doe goe in The
more easilie A man may purchase vpon
them, as it is easier to purchse <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Blocke
wch hath Three sheevers, then a blocke wch
hath but Two But then this is longer 
adoeinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="puttock(n)">Puttocks;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the smale shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> goe from the
shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Maine, for and Missen 
Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, as alsoe to the Topp Mast shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>,
if the Top Mast have a Topp gallant-Topp,
The vse whereof is to goe off the Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> into
the Topp: For when the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> come 
neere vpp to the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> the fall in soe much
that otherwise they could not gett into the 
Topp from them: The Puttocks are att
</page>

<page no="134">
the Bottome seased to a staffe, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made
fast there to the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>, or some Rope 
wch is seased, there, and a bove to a Plate
of Iron, or to a deadman-eye. To <expan type="wch">which</expan> the
Lannyeres of the Top mast-shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> doe
Runne
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">

come to the Masteres Cabbin;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="quartering(n)">Quarteringe,</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="quarter(n)">Is when a Peece of Ordinance lies soe, and
may be soe traversed, That it will shoote in
the same lyne or in the same poynt of 
the Compas as the quarter beares: Alsoe
when a shipp sailes <expan type="wth">with</expan> quarter wynd<expan type="es">es</expan>: wee
saie she goe quarteringe: Then wee lett rise
the weather Cocke, and hale Aft the sheate
a little: This waye the shipp goes fastest 
for now all sailes drawe to gether;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="quarter wind(n)">Quarter wynds</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are when the wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> come in Abast the 
Maine Mast shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> iust <expan type="wth">with</expan> the quarter
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="coil(n)">a Quoyle,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Or a Quoyle of Ropes, is a Rope layd vp
round one fake over an other, as a Quoyle
of Cabell, That is a Cabell Quoyled vpp
But some tymes the word Quoyle is taken
for a whole Rope Quoyled soe that if halfe
that Rope be Cutt a waye they saye there
is but halfe a Quoyle of that Rope;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="coil(v)">to Quoyle,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to laye the Fakes of a Rope round over an
other soe that when occasion is they may 
runne out smoothe wthout anie kynke and 
alsoe to lye Handsomly in the shipp, And 
many of the smalle runninge Ropes as
the Breises Topsaile Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> or the
</page>

<page no="136">
like wee hange vp att the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> when they are 
soe Quoyled: It is a manner att sea every night
when they sett the watch, to Quoyle vp all the
Ropes in Order, that soe they may have them all
leere to come by in the night if they have 
occasion to vse <correction type="d" deleted="them"></correction> anie of them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="quoin(n)">Quoynes</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are Three sort<expan type="es">es</expan> of Quoynes vsed in
a shipp: That is the Quoynes <expan type="wch">which</expan> <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Gunners
vse vnder the Ordinance for to mount them
higher or lower, As they are made broad, but
thynner att one end then att the other <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
a handle att the broad end to drawe it out, <correction type="d" deletion="and"></correction>
or putt it far there in, as you have occasion
to Mounte the peece, Pullinge out the
Quoynes is Termed to drawe the Quoynes
An other sort are Called Cantick Quoynes
These are short, the length of a hand and are
made <expan type="wth">with</expan> Three edges: The vse whereof is
to putt betwixt the Caske, att the Bildg&shy;
hoopes of the Caske to keepe the Caske steeddy
from rowleinge, and labouringe one a gainst
an other, The Third sorte are standinge 
Quoynes, and they are made of Barrell 
bord<expan type="es">es</expan> some Fower fingers broad of a fitt length
to bew driven a Crosse betwixt the Butt<expan type="es">es</expan> on
on end, Two or Three hoopes from the Chine 
hoopes of one Butt and the other in the same
manner to an other to keepe the Chine of the 
Butt<expan type="es">es</expan> steddie from Iogginge
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>
