<wordgroup1 type="a" object="R"><alpha>R</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rabbit of the keel(n)" lexeme="rabbitting(n)">Rabbetinge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the lettinge in of the Planckes to the Keele
wch is a little hollowed a waye that the Plancke
may ioyne in the Better, and clooser to the
Hookes and the Keele, and this is only vsed in
the Rake and Runn of the shipp, and not in
the flatt floare, And the hollowinge a waye
is Called the Rabbett of the Keele;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rake(n)">Rake</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Rake of a shipp is soe much of her Hull
as doth over hange both end<expan type="es">es</expan> of her Keele Soe
that lett fall a perpendiculer vpon th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of
the Keele, att the settinge on of the Stemme
Soe much as is wthout that Forward on, is her
Rake Forward on; And soe in the like manner
att her settinge in of the Sterne post: And that
is the Rake Aftward on: Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly the Rake
Forward-on is more then a Third, but lesse
then halfe of the length of the Keele; There 
is not anie one Rule observed a mongst all 
nations; For some give longe greate Rakes
as generally all French buylt: The Flemming<expan type="es">es</expan>
not soe much: And for the Rake Aftward-on
it beinge of noe vse for the shipp, but only for
to make her shipp shapen as they call it, they
give as little as may be <expan type="wch">which</expan> is a bout 4 or 5 
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of her Keele forward on A greate Rake 
forward on gives a shipp agood waye, and 
makes her keepe a good wynd; but if the shipp
have not a good full bowe, it will make her
Pitch mightylie into the Head sea, besid<expan type="es">es</expan> it
doth mightiely charge the shipp, because it
doth over hange the saile And if a shipp 
have but asmale Rake, she will C<expan type="o+_m+_o+_">ommo</expan>nly 
be too Bluffe, and soe meete the sea too 
soddenly vpon her Bowes <expan type="wch">which</expan> will hinder her 
goeinge through much: The longer a ships
Rake is: The fuller must be her Bowe, The
</page>

<page no="138">
best Condic<expan type="o+_n+_">ion</expan>ed ships have rather too much 
then too little;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ramhead(n)">Ram-head,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Ram head is a greate Blocke <expan type="wth">with</expan> Three
sheevers in it, into <expan type="wch">which</expan> are the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan>, and
att the head of it into a hole are reeved, the 
Tyes This Blocke doth only belonge to the 
Maine and fore Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan>:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rammer(n)">a Rammer,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a staffe <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Round peece of wood att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd
of it, The outward most beinge flatt some what
lesse then the Bore of the Ordinance, To 
wch it doth belonge, and this is to drive home
the Powder close to the breech of the peece
and soe the shot to the Powder and the wodd
to the shott, and that is called Ramminge home
the Powder or Shott;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="range(n)">Ranges,</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are Two, the one a loft vpon the fore
Castle alittle abast the Fore Mast, The other
in the Beake head before the woulding<expan type="es">es</expan> of
the Bolt spritt, that is in the fore Castle, is
a smale peece of tymber, <expan type="wch">which</expan> goes over from
one side, to ther other, and there is fastened in
two tymbers; and in the Middle on either side
the fore mast two Knees <expan type="wch">which</expan> are fastened in 
the decke; And this tymber in <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Top&shy;
saile sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> in a Sheever, and hath diu<expan type="+r">er</expan>s woodden
Drivers through it to belaye the Ropes vnto 
as the Fore Tacke, Fore Topsaile sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>
or forebowling<expan type="es">es</expan>, the Fore-loose-hooke, And 
that in the Beake-head is in the same 
forme wherevnto is belayed, the Sprittsaile
lift<expan type="es">es</expan>, and Garnett of the Spritt saile and
other Ropes belonginge to the Sprittsaile
Top saile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="139">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="ratling(n)">Ratlinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a lyne where <expan type="wth">with</expan> they make the steps by wch
wee goe vp the shrode<expan type="es">es</expan> to the Puttockes and to
the Topp Mast shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> in greate shpps And these
stepps <expan type="wch">which</expan> make the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> looke like Ladders are
called the Ratling<expan type="es">es</expan> of the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="reach(n)">a Reach</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the distance of anie two poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> of land <expan type="wch">which</expan> beare
in a right lyne to an other <expan type="wch">which</expan> terme is most 
Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed in Rivers as Lyme house reach 
Greenewitch Reach, longe Reache and the like,
The reach beinge Counted as farr as you cann
See the Reach in a Straight lyne: Alsoe some 
call the distance and Crossinge over Betwixt Cap&shy;
Verd and the first Island<expan type="es">es</expan> enteringe into the 
west Indies Longe Reach;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="reeve(v)">to Reeue</form>
<xpln lang="en">This word is vsed iust in the same sence in 
respect of Ropes That puttinge in or puttinge
through or passinge Through would be But
they ever vse this word Reeve, as when wee would
expresse that the Tecke is putt through the 
Chestrees, we say it is Reeved through, or in steed
of puttinge a Rope through a Blocke we say reeve
it in that Blocke: As the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> are Reeved
in the Knight<expan type="es">es</expan>, and Ramm-head<expan type="es">es</expan> And it is gen<expan type="+r">er</expan>ally
to be vnderstood and applied to all Ropes that
passe through Blockes, dead men-eyes Chestrees
and the like And soe when wee would have 
that Rope pulled out of the Blocke &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>: wee say
Vnreeve that Rope or the Brases, lift<expan type="es">es</expan>, Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> 
&amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> are vnreeved;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rib(n)">Ribbs</form>
<xpln lang="en">By a Resemblance that the tymbers <emend err="thas">that</emend> the
Futtockes of the shipps have, when the planck<expan type="es">es</expan>
are off to the Ribbes of a dead Carcas wee doe
</page>

<page no="139">140
in that kind call all those tymbers by a generall
appella<expan type="">cion</expan> the Ribbs of ashipp though otherwise
they have perticuler names, as if two shipps in
a greate gate by aBoard one an other; and one breake
wth her weight some of the other Futtocke tymbers
They will saye she hath broke some of her <correction type="d" deletion="tymbers"></correction>
Ribbs: Alsoe those little longe wood peeces, wch
are made <expan type="wth">with</expan> holes like the Combe vnder the Beake
head and doe belonge to the Barrells of the yard
are Called the Ribbs of the Parrells;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="ride(v)">to Ride --</form>
<xpln lang="en">We saye ashipp rid<expan type="es">es</expan> when as her Ancho<expan type="">r</expan>s doe 
hold her fast; soe as that she doth not drive a waye
wth the tyde and wynd for though shee sheere from
one side to an other yet if her Anchors doe holde 
fast and Come not home wee saye she rid<expan type="es">es</expan>: To
Ride a greate Roade That is to ride where the sea
and wynd had much power over the shipp and 
Strayne the Cabbells very hard, Note a shipe 
rides Easiest and <expan type="wth">with</expan> more Securitie haveinge
butt two Cabells splyced togeather, <expan type="wch">which</expan> they call
a shott, then she will by Three single Cabells, for the
length of the shott will give her more scope to play
and Rise vpon the sea <expan type="wth">with</expan> Ease: for by reason alsoe
of the waight the shipp can hardly strayne it:
For when a greate sea comes to yerke vp the ship
the shott is longe before it comes to strayninge
that the force of the sea wilbe past before it 
can come vp to beare soe much stresse as 
a shorter Cabell would doe: The deeper the 
water is the worser it is to ride and requires
much more Cabell in propor<expan type="">cion</expan> then Showler
water: For though in the showler water the
sea will breake more yet it hath not the power
and weight <expan type="wch">which</expan> the deepe water hath: when 
we ride an extraordinarie Rode, we strike 
</page>

<page no="141">
downe our Top Mast and bringe our yard<expan type="es">es</expan> alongst
shipps in much wynd, especially to ride a Crosse;
is to ride, <expan type="wth">with</expan> our Mayne yard and for yard 
hoysed vp to the Hound<expan type="es">es</expan> and both yard Armes
topped alike: To ride a Peecke, is to ride <expan type="wth">with</expan> the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> Peeked a Peeke and alsoe when wee ride
wth her Hawse iust over the Anchor then wee ride
a Peeke, That is when wee ride ready to sett 
saile: when they would expresse that they have
rydd a greate Road and stresse They saye they 
rid Hawse fall That is that the water Brake 
into the Hawses: To ride Thwart is to ride <expan type="wth">with</expan> her
side to the tyde, then she never straynes her Cabells
To ride betwixt winde and tyde, is when the wynd
and tyde have equall power, The one, one waye,
the other, the other way, so that the shipp 
lyes Cowlinge <expan type="wth">with</expan> her Broad side in the trough
of the sea; and this she will Roule mightily, but 
not strayne her Cabells;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rider(n)">Riders</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are great tymbers in Holde or else aloft wch
are not properly belonginge to the buylt of
the ship, but only Bolted on vpon the other
Tymbers; to strengthen them where they find
the shipp to be weake: Marchant men doe
spare them as much as they Cann, because
they hinder stowage of Castle in Holde,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rigging(n)">Riggings</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Rigging<expan type="es">es</expan> of a shipp are all ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
belonge either to Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> or yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, and more 
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>ticulerly wee saye the Mast is rigged, The
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> are Rigged, That is when they have all
the Ropes that belonge vnto them, we say a 
</page>

<page no="142">
ship is well rigged, when the Ropes belonginge to 
her are of a fitt Cize, and not to bigge, nor to little
Alsoe when there are noe vnnecessarie Ropes putt
vpp, as too many shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>, Tackles for the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
Crowe feete or the like, when that wee saye a shipp
is over Rigged, it is meant the Ropes are to bigge
for her <expan type="wch">which</expan> is a greate wronge to the shipps sailing
for alittle weight aloft doth hinder more then a great
deale allowe, by makeinge the shipp apter to 
heele, and houldinge winde taught, for Note the
vprighter anie shipp goes the better shee doth 
saile for a Crancke sided shipp can neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> saile 
well by the wynd To tell the <expan type="p-_">par</expan>ticulers of rigginge
a shipp will require a smale discourse, by it 
selfe, and would be too longe for this and 
therefore I leave it to some other occation;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rising(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Risinges</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those thicke Planck<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> goe fore and 
Aft on both sid<expan type="es">es</expan> vnder the End<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Beames
and tymbers of the second decke, the Third 
decke, the halfe and quarter decke where on 
the Beames and tymbers of these deckes doe
beare at oth end<expan type="es">es</expan> by the shipps side: But
those, thicke Planckes <expan type="wch">which</expan> in the like sorte
doe beare vp the lower decke are called
Clamps;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rising timber(n)">Rising Timbers</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the hookes placed on the Keele wch
beare this name in respect that accordinge
to their riseinge by little and little; soe the
Rake and the Runn of the shipp doth rise by 
</page>

<page no="143">
little and little form the flatt floare;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="road(n)">a Roade,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is anie place where a shipp may ride neere the 
land and yet cannot ride land locked for all wynd<expan type="es">es</expan>
agood Road is where there is good ground 
for Anchor holde, Showle water, and soe as that
how erre the wynd Blowe, there can noe great
Seagate come in, beinge the land may be in the
winde on one side, and some sand<expan type="es">es</expan> Rockes, and the
like to breake off the Sea on the other: Alsoe wee say
if it be aplace, as in diu<expan type="+r">er</expan>s places of Barbarie, and
other where the Sea will give a mayne warninge
(That is the sea will come swelling in before the wynd
as att Saphee) of anie fowle weather, soe that
aman may have tyme to sett saile and goe to 
some other Roade on the other side of the Baye 
Head land or the like, and this wee call shiftinge
of Road<expan type="es">es</expan> A wild Roade where there is little land
on anie side but lyes all open to the sea, as to
ride vpon a head land, or alongst a shore where
there is noe Baye or anie thinge to breake
off the sea or wynd if it come of the sea A badd
Rode is the Contrary to the good &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="roader(n)">a Roader,</form>
<xpln lang="en">We call anie shipp that rid<expan type="es">es</expan> at an Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>
in a Roade a Roader;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rope(n)">Ropes,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Generally all the cordage belonginge to 
ashipp is called by the name of Rope: As
we say a Cabell is a good or a badd Rope 
accordinge as it is and soe a Hawser or the 
like, but more <expan type="p-_">ticulerly</expan>, Only some <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
</page>

<page no="144">
besid, their <expan type="p-_">par</expan>ticuler appella<expan type="">cion</expan>s have the gen<expan type="+r">er</expan>all 
word Rope added to them Those are an enteringe
Rope, a Buoy Rope, a Quest Rope, a Keele Rope
a Baskett Rope, a Rudder Rope, a Preventer
Rope, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is a little Rope seased, close over the
tyes, close att the Ram-head, That if one <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
of the tyes should breake, the other should not
runn through the Ram head, to endanger the
yard, A Brest Rope, is the Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> Lashes
the <expan type="p-_">par</expan>cell to the Mast;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rope yarn(n)">Rope-yarnes,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the yarnes of anie Ropes vntwisted, but
Most C<expan type="o+_m+_o+_n+_">ommon</expan>ly it is made of att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nds of the 
Cabells halfe woven or soe, They serve for 
many vses to save smale Ropes wth, or to marke
Synnett Matt<expan type="es">es</expan>, and the like, alsoe Kneetles, wch
is Two twisted together, and Caburnes They
serve alsoe to make vpp the yard armes of the
sailes, and therefore still when wee take our 
sailes, the boyes of the shipp are to attend the
sailors <expan type="wth">with</expan> these Ropes yarnes to furnish 
them as they have occasion to vse them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="robbin(n)">Robbins</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are little lynes Reeved into the Eyeltt holes 
of the saile vnder the Head Rope, and are to
make fast the saile vnto the yard, and the 
Terme is make fast the Robbins, and not 
tye them, and Note that Sea feareinge men
ever vse the word make fast in steed of 
tynge, as Landmen vse to saye, Tye a Rope
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="roof-tree(n)">Roofe-trees,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made of light 
wood as of Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> sawen, That goe from the
halfe decke to the Fore Castle and are to 
</page>

<page no="145">
beare vp the Grating<expan type="es">es</expan> and the ledges whereon
the Netting<expan type="es">es</expan> lye: These are suported vnder
wth Stannchions <expan type="wch">which</expan> rest vpon the decke:
Alsoe if they have occasion to vse anie such
Peeces over the halfe decke for Netting<expan type="es">es</expan> or
Sailes it is called a Roofetree;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rove and clinch(n)">Roue &amp; Clinch</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Rove is that little Iron plate vnto wch
the Clintch nailes are clyntched; The Planck<expan type="es">es</expan>
of Clintcher Boat<expan type="es">es</expan> are thus fastened together
Alsoe the Planck<expan type="es">es</expan> of the <correction type="d" deleted="par"></correction> Port<expan type="es">es</expan> are soe
Fastened, together, <expan type="wch">which</expan> kind of worke, is called
Rove &amp; Clintch;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="roundhouse(n)">a Round house,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the vppermost roome of the sterne of the shipp

</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="round ing in(n)">Rounding-in</form>
<xpln lang="en">This is a terme vsed to the Maine and fore&shy;
saile when the wynd larges vpon them, then they
lett rise the Maine Tacke and fore tacke, and
hale Aft the Fore sheate to the Catt-head; and the
Maine sheate, to the Cubbridg-head, this they 
call Roundinge aft or Roundinge in the <correction type="d"deleted="sheate"></correction>
saile The sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> beinge there, they hale them 
downe to keepe them steddy from flyinge vpp
wth Rope called a Passardo;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rowel(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Rowle;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that round peece of <correction type="d" deleted="tymber"></correction> wood or Iron 
wherein the whipp doth doe: and is made to
turne aboute that it may carry over the whip
from side to side <expan type="wth">with</expan> more ease;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<page no="146">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="rouse in(v)">Rowse in</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a word they vse perticulerly when as a Cabell
or Hawser doth lie slackein the water, and they
would have it lie Tough, as when a shipp 
rid<expan type="es">es</expan> but by one Anchor vpon the Turninge of 
the Tyde, The Cabell wilbe slacke, and soe
wilbe in danger to fowle a bove the Anchor
Then to keepe it stiffe, and Taught they will 
hale in soe much as lies slacke, and this they
call Rowseinge in the Cabell, or Rowse in
the Hawser, but it is not vsed to the halinge 
in of anie other Rope, as Boat, Rope or the
like:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rudder(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Rudder,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that peece of tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> hang<expan type="es">es</expan> att the sterne
post of the shipp having. 4.5.or 6. Irons <expan type="wch">which</expan> are
called Pintells, accordinge to the bignesse of the
shipp fastened to the, <expan type="wch">which</expan> Pintells are fitted
for the Gudgins at the sterne post, and soe
by these the Rudder is hanged to the Sterne 
post This is the Bridle <expan type="wch">which</expan> governes the 
shipp, the Narrower the Rudder is, The better
if the shipp doe feele it, for a Broad Rudder
doth hold much water; if the Helme be putt over
to anie side But if the ship have a flatt quarter
soe that the water cannot come thicke and 
stronge to the Rudder; then she will require
a Broad Rudder: The puttinge to of the
Rudder is termed hanginge the Rudder: The
parte or edge of the Rudder <expan type="wch">which</expan> is next the 
sterne post is Called the inside of the Rudder
<emend err="wch is next the Sterne post is Called the
inside of the Rudder"></emend> the Aftermost <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te is -- 
called the Backe of the Rudder;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rudder rope(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Rudder Rope,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope or strape <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Reeved into one
<emend err="one"></emend> hole of the Rudder neere the head, and
soe likewise through the Sterne post, and
then both end<expan type="es">es</expan> are spliced together This
</page>

<page no="147">
serves to save the Rudder if it Chance to be 
beaten off when the shipp stickes aground;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rudder iron(n)">Rudder-irons,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the Cheekes of that Iron whereof the 
Pintell is parte and these are fastened and
nayled round about the Rake of the Rudder;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rummage(v)">to Rumidge,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to remove anie good<expan type="es">es</expan> or Luggage out of a 
place betwixt the deckes, or aniewhere else
but most Comonly we vse this word to the 
removeinge and Cleereinge of thing<expan type="es">es</expan> in the 
shipps holde, soe that good<expan type="es">es</expan> or victualls may
be well stowed or placed Soe when they would
have this done they saie they will goe Rumidge
the holde;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="run(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Runne,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that parte of the ships Hull vnder water,
wch comes Thinner and Lancker a waye by 
degrees from the floare tymbers all a longe
to the sterne post This is alsoe called the 
ships waye Aftward on For as she hath 
either a good or a Badd Runne, soe the water
doth passe a way swiftly or slowly alongst her, 
and the shipp doth make more ways: wee say
a shipp hath a good Runne when it is longe and
comes off handsomly by degrees, and that her
Tacke doe not lye to lowe, <expan type="wch">which</expan> will hinder 
the water from Cominge strongly and swiftly
to the Rudder, and a badd Runne, when as it
is short, and that the shipp is to full belowe
soe that the water comes slowly and weakely
to the Rudder the fore of it begine broken off
by the bredth of the ship belowe, <expan type="wch">which</expan> will 
make (as it were) an Eddie water att the 
</page>

<page no="148">
Rudder, and that wee call a dead water: The
Runne is of Maine importance for the shipps
Saileinge, for if the water come not swiftly
to the Rudder, shee will neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> Steere well 
and it is agenerall observa<expan type="">cion</expan>: That, that
ship that doth not Steere well, cannot saile
well, and then she cannot keepe agood wynd
For is a shipp have not fresh wynd 
through the sea she must need<expan type="es">es</expan> fall to <correction type="d" deleted="thee"></correction>
Leeward <expan type="wth">with</expan> the sea; And therefore when 
shipps will not saile well they lengthen them 
Aftward on, or putt too a false steere Post
Marchant men doe not give soe much Runne
as a Man of war may doe, because the Nar&shy;
rowinge in of the shipp belowe doth loose 
much stowidge:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rung head(n)">Rung-head,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are th head<expan type="es">es</expan> or End<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Rung<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are
made a little Compassinge, and doe heade
and direct, as it were the Mould, and 
sweepe of the Futtockes, For in these 
Rung-head<expan type="es">es</expan>, the lynes <expan type="wch">which</expan> give the Compasse
and beareinge of the shipp doe begin Alsoe 
more generally the outward End<expan type="es">es</expan> of the 
Heeles <expan type="wch">which</expan> are in the same manner Com-
pasinge are Called Rung-head<expan type="es">es</expan>, for the 
Sleeper <expan type="wch">which</expan> is bolted into the other Rung-head<expan type="es">es</expan>
is alsoe Bolted into these and they say it is
bolted fore and Aft to the Rung-head<expan type="es">es</expan>:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rung(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Rungs,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the ground tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe give the 
<emend err="the"></emend> Floare of the shipp: And these are
Bolted to the Keele beinge straight saveing
att the end<expan type="es">es</expan> and there they beginn to 
Compasse a little;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="149">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="runner(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Runner</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> a parte doth belonge to the Garnett
and the Two bolt<expan type="es">es</expan> Tackels: That before wch
comes in the Aftermost shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Fore Mat
and that Tackle Abast <expan type="wch">which</expan> comes in the fore
Mast shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Mayne Mast It is reeved
in a single Blocke <expan type="wch">which</expan> is seased to th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of
a pendant, and hath att one end a hooke to 
Hitch into anie thinge, and att the other end
a double Blocke wherein is Reeved the Falle
of the Tackle, or the Garnett, <expan type="wch">which</expan> doe not
purchase more then the Tackle or <correction type="i"><expan type="y+e">the</expan></correction> Garnett
would doe wthout it, and therefore to heave 
thing<expan type="es">es</expan> they vse this; But for light ones 
They only vse the Tackle <expan type="wch">which</expan> hath a blocke wth
a Hooke, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is seased to the standinge part
of the Fall: Over hall the Runner, That is
to pull downe that end <expan type="wch">which</expan> hath the hooke in
it to Hitch it into the sling<expan type="es">es</expan> or the like
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sail(n)">Saile</form>
<xpln lang="en">To every yard in the ship there belonges a 
saile, and they are called after the name 
of the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> wherevnto they belonge All the
head sailes, that is those <expan type="wch">which</expan> belonge to the 
Fore Mast, and Bolt spritt, doe keepe the shipp
from the wynd and are vsed to flatt the ship
All after sailes (That is the Mayne Mast, and
Myssen sailes doe keepe her to the wynd, and
therefore fewe shipps are soe well Condi<expan type="">cion</expan> 
as to Steere quarter wynd<expan type="es">es</expan>, <expan type="wth">with</expan> one saile, but
most have one after saile and one other head
saile as it were to Countermannd <correction type="i">one</correction> an other 
yet some shipps will steere <expan type="wth">with</expan> their Maine
Top-saile only: Att sea they call a ship a saile
as when they see a shipp they saye a saile 
a saile The sailes are Cutt in propor<expan type="">cion</expan> as
the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> and yard<expan type="es">es</expan> are in length &amp; bredth
one to an other except the Myssen, and spritt
saile: The Missen saile is Cutt by the Leetch
twice as deepe as the Mast is longe from
the decke to the honnd<expan type="es">es</expan>, and the spritt saile 
is 3/4 a deepe as the fore saile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="serve(v)">to Sarue,</form>
<xpln lang="en">To serve anie Rope is to saie Synnett
Spinn yarne, Rope yarne, a peece of 
Canvas or anie the like vpon a Rope, and
soe Rowle it fast a bout to Keepe the Rope
from Callinge as wee serve the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>
att the head of the Mast The Boate 
Rope or anie the like <expan type="wch">which</expan> are in danger
of Frettinge against anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the ship,
Mast of yard<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="151">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="scarf(n)">a Scarfe</form> 
<xpln lang="en">Is when th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of one tymber is lett into the end
of an other very Close and even , or as they
terme it wood and wood: That is soe much
wood taken a waye of the one as is of the
other; In this manner of the St<expan type="e+_m+_">emm</expan> is fastened
to the Keele and that is called the Scarfe
of the Keele and yet when there is not a peece
of tymber longe enough to make a Keele, then
they make it of more <expan type="wch">which</expan> are Scarfed one 
into an other Soe when the St<expan type="e+_m+_">emme</expan> or anie, 
other tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> ought to be entire, and all 
one is too short, it is peeced in this manner
and That they call Scarffinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="scuttle(n)">a Scuttle,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a square hole soe much as Conveniently
a Man may go downe att cut through anie
Hutch or anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the decke to goe downe
by into anie Roome, most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly they are in
these places One Close before the Mayne Mast
att the Maine Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan>, before the Knight in
the fore Castle in the Hatch waye for the 
Steward<expan type="es">es</expan> roome, One in the Gunn Roome to 
goe downe into the Sterne sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>, One in 
the Masters Cabbin to goe downe into the 
Capstaines Cabbin if they be putt from the 
feight alloft and soe in anie place where
they desire to goe downe through one decke
into an other; Alsoe for vent for <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Ordinance
there are smale Scuttles <expan type="wth">with</expan> grating<expan type="es">es</expan>: They
have all Covers fitted for them least men in
the night should fall into them Alsoe all the
little windowes and holes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are Cutt out 
a loft in the Captaines, or Masters Cabbins
are Called Scuttles;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="152">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="seize(v)">to Sease or Seasinge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to make fast as you would saye to byd fast anie
Ropes togeather <expan type="wth">with</expan> some smale Rope yarne 
Malinge or anie lyne Alsoe the fasteneinge of <correction type="d" deleted="anie"></correction>
<correction type="d" deleted="Rope">Rope</correction> a Blocke att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of a Pendant, Tackle, fall
Garnett or the like is called Seasinge Soe if 
anie Rope be to longe as the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> are over,
generally the word seasinge implies as much as
byndinge anie thing togeather, soe as they 
cannott <correction type="i">slipp</correction><correction type="d" deleted="gett"></correction> out as Seasinge the Tackle into the
Clewe and the like, The bates Seasinge, is a Rope
made fast into a Little Chaine or a Ringe in a fore
shipp of the baote, and is the Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> in Harbo<expan type="+r">r</expan>s
they make fast the Boate by the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="seel(v)">a Seele</form>
<xpln lang="en">There is noe difference betwixt Seelinge and 
Heelinge: But that Heelinge is a Steedy lying
downe of the shipp on a side, either when she is
aground, as an Anchor, or vnder saile, and 
Seeleinge is a soddaine lyine downe or 
tumblinge to one side or the other when the 
wynd doth for saile her, That is when the wave
of the sea is past from vnder faster then she
can drive a waye <expan type="wth">with</expan> it, Then when a shipp lies
downe on a side after it wee saye shee Seeles 
The Leeseele is when she Roules to Leeward, 
There is noe greate danger in this Seele, 
Though it be in a greate storme, because the
sea will p<expan type="+r">re</expan>sently come vnder and right her
But then when she Rowles back to wyndward,
the danger is least she should come over too
short and soddainely and soe the wave breake
right into her and founder her or carry 
a wave some of her vpp or workes, as it 
hath fallen out <expan type="wth">with</expan> many shipps: Soe that
</page>

<page no="153">
Seeleinge is but a soddaine Heeleinge forsee
by the Motion or force of the Sea or wynd;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="send(v)">Send</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a ship falls whether vnder saile or
at Anchor, <expan type="wth">with</expan> her head or Sterne deepe 
into the Trough of the sea; we say she send<expan type="es">es</expan> 
much either a sterne or a head, The reason of 
sendinge <expan type="wth">with</expan> her head is If she have a little Bowe
not sufficient to beare her vpp, and a fat
quarter to pitch her foreward, and soe for her
sendinge a sterne it is Contrary, when she hath
too lancke a quarter, and to fat or full a Bowe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="set aland(v)">Sett a Land, sunn
<doubtful type="u">or</doubtful> shipp by the 
Compasse;</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is to observe by the Compasse how the
land beares vpon anie poynt of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Compasse
This they vse most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly to doe when they
are goe-inge off to Sea from anie land, to 
marke how it did beare off then, that thereby 
they may keepe the better Accompt, and 
drest their Course: Alsoe they vse to sett 
the Sunn by the Compasse That is to make
vpon what poynt it is, To knowe thereby the
hower of the daye: Soe when two ships saile 
in the sight, especially when a Man of warr 
chases a shipp, they call sett her by the Compasse
That is make vpon what poynt she beares: Then
if they stand both one waye,as Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly they
doe, If the Chase strive to goe a waye<emend err=")"></emend> by this
wee knowe whether wee reach forth vpon her
That is out saile her or noe For if wee bringe
her Aft wee do out saile her, If wee bringe her
forth she out sailes vs: If we alter not, Then 
wee goe both a like; As for example the wynd
</page>

<page no="154">
beinge at North we stand both a waye west and
the Chase beares North west; that wilbe on my
nether Bowe Then if in saileinge I bringe her
to beare Norwest, and I by North, I have brought
her a Poynt Aft, and if I bringe her North I 
have brought her iust <expan type="wth">with</expan> my Middshipp beame
And soe I see I fetch vpon her, and it is called
bringinge Aft because whereas before she bore
vpon my loose now she beares vpon my quarter
and soe the Contrary;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="settle a deck(v)">Settle a Deck</form>
<xpln lang="en">When wee have occation to laye a decke 
lower it is termed setlinge a decke, as if her
Ordinance lye to high and wee would have 
Them lye neere the water, or that the deckes
be too close, and wee desire rather to settle the
lower most, then to raise the vpper most,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="sewing(n)" lexeme="sew(v)">Sewinge or
to Sewe,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the water is gone from the shipp soe
that she lyes drye we saye the shipp is sewed
or if it be but gone from anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of her 
head we saye the ship is Sewed ahead If
it be a place where the water doth not ebb
soe much that the shipp may lye drieround
wee say she cannot Sewe there;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shackle(n)">Shackells;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are a kinde of ryng<expan type="es">es</expan> but not round made
some what longwise, larger att one end 
then att the other in the Middle of the Port<expan type="es">es</expan>
on the inside they are vsed to shutt downe 
fast the Port<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Bilbott, <expan type="wch">which</expan> they vse to 
barr downe the portes wth, and that is
</page>

<page no="155">
called the Barr of the Porte Alsoe the
fashion but smale ons are made fast in the 
Corners of the Hatches, to lift the Hatches
vpp by them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shank(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Shancke</form>
<xpln lang="en">The longest parte of the Anchor is called
the Shancke of the Anchor;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shank painter(n)">Shancke
Painter,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a short Chaine fastened vnder the Fore 
Mast sh<expan type="+r">r</expan>oud<expan type="es">es</expan>, <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Bolt to the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> 
and att the other end hath a Rope vpon the
Chaine doth rest, the whole weight of the 
After parte of the Anchor when it lyes by
the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan> And the rope by <expan type="wch">which</expan> it is 
Halled vpp is made fast a bout a tymber head
that is seldome or not att all vsed at sea but
in a Harbour or a Roade;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shear(v)" lexeme="shearing(n)">Sheareinge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when ashipp goes in and out vnder saile
and he at the Helme doth not Steere her 
Steedy: Alsoe where a tyde gate runnes very
Swift the shipp will Sheere in and out,
And so much in some places, That they 
are faine to have one stand att the Helme
and to Steere her vpon the tyde for feare
she should Sheere home her Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>s that
is drawe them home, or if it be neere the
shore, she may sheere a ground,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sheet(n)">Sheats,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> are putt to the Clewe lynes of
all sailes, in all sailes, that are lowe sailes
</page>

<page no="156">
serve to hale Afte or Round Afte, the Clewe of the
saile But in Topsailes they serve to hale home, That
is to Hale Close the Clewe of the saile to the yard
Armes, when they hale Aft the Sheate of the Maine
saile, It is to make the shipp keepe by the wynd 
when they hale Afte the sheate of the Fore saile
it is to make her fall off from the wynd for the
sheate doth tryme the after Leech of the saile 
by the winde when the shipp will not fall off from
the wynd they foreflatt in the Fore sheate That is pull
the saile Flatt in by the sheate, as neere vnto 
the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan> as may bee: Ease the sheate of 
anie saile That is to veere out or lett go alittle
of it Let flye the sheate, That is lett it runn 
out soe farr as it will and there the sayle 
will hould noe wynd, but lye Flatteringe loose
And then if it be an extraordinarie wynd, it will
Spritt the sail in peeces But this wee doe <correction type="i">both</correction> wth
Top saile sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>, and the other Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> when we
suspect the wynd wilbe soe greate that it will
Carry our Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> by the Board or over sett the
shipp Alsoe in greate Stiffe gales we vse 
to bynde an other Rope to the Clewe of the 
saile, a bove the Sheate Blocke to succour
and ease the Sheate least it should breake 
and that Rope wee calle a false sheate, and
this is only vsed to the Mayne and fore sailes
Those Planckes vnderwater <expan type="wch">which</expan> come alonge
the Runne of the ship and are Closed to the 
Sterne Port are Called sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>, and that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
wthin Bord Abast in the Runn of the shipp
is Called the Sterne sheate,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sheath(v)" lexeme="sheathing(n)">Sheathing</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is as it were Caseinge a ship It is donne
wth thinne board and haire and tarr laid
betwixt the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan> and those Bord<expan type="es">es</expan> 
This is donne only vnder water or alittle 
above The vse whereof is to keepe the wormes
</page>

<page no="156">
from eatinge through the Planckes as generally
in all places to the Southward they doe The 
thynner the Bord<expan type="es">es</expan> the better: For then the 
worme will presently be att the Tarr <expan type="wch">which</expan> he 
cannot a bide, and soe hath not meanes <correction type="i">nor roome</correction> to worke
out of the Plancke, and soe will eate alwaye 
more when it is thicke, then when it is thynne;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sheepshank(n)">Sheepe-shanke,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a kinde of Knott <expan type="wch">which</expan> they Cast vpon a Runner
when it is to longe, soe that they cannot hoyse 
in the good<expan type="es">es</expan> over the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan> vnlesse it be shortned
and by this Knott they can quickly shorten it vpp
asmuch as they lift and instantly vndoe it a gaine
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sheer hook(n)">Sheere-hookes;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are greate hookes of Iron, about the biggness of
a smale sickle and more: They are lett into the
yard armes of the fore yard<expan type="es">es</expan>: The vse whereof
is That if a shipp vnder sale, Come to Board
her that hath these hookes: She will cutt her 
Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> or teare her sailes down <expan type="wth">with</expan> these 
hookes: Some doe vse them, but they are most
vnusefull and vnnecessarie thing<expan type="es">es</expan> &amp; dangerouse
for the breakeinge of a yard if the hooke should
Catch in the other ships Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sheer(n)">Sheeres;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When Two Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> or yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, if it be but Poles
are sett vp an end a pretty distance off att the
Bottom, but seased a Crosse on an other a loft neere
the Topp we call them a payre of sheeres; To this
seasinge is fastened a double Blocke <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Strap
They are placed att the Bottome vpon the 
Chaine walls of the Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> and there are 
Lashed fast to the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> tackels a loft
</page>

<page no="158">
wch Come down in the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan> to keepe them
Steddy a loft: The vse of them is either to sett in 
a Mast or take out a Mast, or if they have noe
Mast these serve to hoise in and out good<expan type="es">es</expan>:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sheaver(n)">Sheeuers;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are two sort<expan type="es">es</expan> of Sheevers vsed either
of Brasse or wood: The Brasse sheevers 
are now little vsed, but in the Heele of the Topp
Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>: The woodden sheevers, are either of one 
whole peece, and these they vse for all smale 
pulleyes and smale Blockes, but in the Knight<expan type="es">es</expan>
and wyndinge Tackle Blockes, They vse sheevers
wch are made of Quarters of wood lett into 
each other, for these will holde when the whole
sheevers will splitt, And are called quarter 
sheevers;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shore(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Shoare/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is called the land neere the sea or the Banck<expan type="es">es</expan>
of the sea the Lee shoare is that wehreon the 
wynd blowes Seamen avoyd these by all meanes
for it is dangerous if it over blowe: The 
weather shoare is that form whence the 
winde comes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"> 
<form lexeme="shore(n)">Shores;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are anie peeces of tymber or anie thinge
else that is sett to beare vp an other from
sinckinge or fallinge: As when a ship is 
in danger of overthroweinge a ground: wee
Lash fast Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> or yard<expan type="es">es</expan> to her sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, they beareinge
on the ground and these wee calle Shoares
shoareinge her vpp: Alsoe some Tymbers
wch are sett to beare vpp a decke when it is
</page>

<page no="159">
weake or overcharged <expan type="wth">with</expan> weight are called 
Shoares;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shot(n)">Shott.</form>
<xpln lang="en">There as many kind<expan type="es">es</expan> of Shott, That <expan type="wch">which</expan> flies
farthest and peirces most is round shott: The 
next is Crosse barr <expan type="wch">which</expan> is good for Ropes and 
sailes soe farr, but is very good for the rigginge and
the like and for men Soe is Chaine shott, and Chesse
shott or Burr shott <expan type="wch">which</expan> is good to playe a mongst 
men <expan type="wch">which</expan> stand naked plyinge of the Smale 
Shott;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shot of cable(n)">Shott of Cabell</form>
<xpln lang="en">Two Cabells splyced togeather make a shott
and the vse of them is greate in deepe waters 
and greate Rodes: For a shipe doth ride much 
easier by one shott: Then by Three short Cabells
a head Vide <xref>Ride</xref>,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shoal(n)">Showle,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Showle and shallowe are all one: when they say 
there is a very good showlinge It is meant that
the water doth growe shallower by degrees and
not soddainely, nor somtymes deepe and 
somtymes soddainely a Showle or Bancke 
It is very safe and C<expan type="o+_m+_">omm</expan>odious goeinge in wth
a Shoare where there is good Showleinge for
by that wee have some Certeyntie whereabout
wee are and how farr distant from the land
of the Showlinge be first knowne: And 
Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly where there is good showleinge the 
Coast is not dangerous;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="160">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="shroud(n)">Shrowds</form>
<xpln lang="en">The shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> are the Ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> Come
From either side of all the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>: The Missen
Mayne Mast, and Fore Mast shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> have 
att the Lower end<expan type="es">es</expan> dead-men-eyes seased into
them and are sett vpp taught by Lannyers to
the Chaines <expan type="wch">which</expan> have alsoe dead men-eyes 
in them: Att the other end they are fastened
to the Head of the Mast: The Pendant<expan type="es">es</expan> 
Fore tackle and swifters beinge fast putt
on vnder them att this vppermost <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te they
are served for gallinge against the Mast
The Topp Mast shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> are in the same
manner fastened <expan type="wth">with</expan> deadmen-eyes, and 
Lannyers to the Puttockes or the plates of 
Iron <expan type="wch">which</expan> belonge to them, and a loft over the
head of the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> as the other: Ease the 
shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>, Slacke the Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>, That is when
they are to stiffe sett vpp; Sett taught the 
shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>, sett vp the Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>; That is make
them Stiffer: Some ships desire to have the
Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> tough, some slacke: The Lannyers are
to sett vpp the Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>: Vide <xref>Lannyers</xref> 
The boult spritt hat noe shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="cylinder(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Sillender,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Bore or hollowe Concave of the Peece
of Ordinance is called the Sillender;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sinnet(n)">Synnett,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a lyne or stringe made of Rope yarne
Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly of Two, Sixe, or Nine, <expan type="wch">which</expan> are
divided in three part<expan type="es">es</expan>; and plated one over
an other, as they platt horses maynes, and
soe is beaten smooth, and flatt <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Mallett
The vse of it is to serve Ropes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="161">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="skeg(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Skegg</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a little <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Keele, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Cutt slannt&shy;
nigne, and is left a little wthout the Sterne post
The reason and vse whereof is only intended to 
be that it should save the Rudder from beatinge
off if the shipp should Chance to beate aground
but these Skegg<expan type="es">es</expan> are very vnvsefull and incon&shy;
venient: For First they are apt to snape off and
endanger the Sterne post Next in a Harboure 
or Ryver where rid<expan type="es">es</expan> many shipps, They are apt
to Catch an other ships Cables betwixt that
and the Rudder; And lastly when the shipp
is vnder saile they hold much dead water betwixt
them and the Rudder: Therefore it is better
to have noe Skegg but to hange the Rudder 
downe Close to the Sterne post <expan type="wth">with</expan> the 
bottome even to the bottome of the Keele only
parde a waye a little sloapinge toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the
aftermost side of it;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="skiff(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Skyff;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Boate</xref>
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="scupper(n)" lexeme="scupper-hole(n)">Skuppers or 
Skupper-holes;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the holes Close to all the deckes through
the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan> where at the water doth runn 
through out of the shipp from the deckes and
many shipps have them of lead:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="scupper latcher(n)">Skupper
Latchers;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the Round Leathers <expan type="wch">which</expan> are nayled 
over the Skupper holes, that belonge to the
Lower decke <expan type="wch">which</expan> will keepeout the Sea water
for Cominge in, and yet give leave to anie 
water to runne out of the decke: These are alsoe
over the Skuppers of the Manger;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="162">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="skupper-nail(n)">Skupper
nayles;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are little shorte nailes <expan type="wth">with</expan> broade head<expan type="es">es</expan> made
of purpose to naile on the Skupper leathers
wth these alsoe they naile one the Coat<expan type="es">es</expan> of the
Mast and Pumps;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="slatch(n)">a Slatch</form>
<xpln lang="en">When anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of a Cabell or Rope that is 
ment of the Middle not of th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan> doth hange
slacke wthout the shipp, as the Cabell when it is
slacke in the water, or the Lee Tacke, Sheats, 
Brases, or the like doe hange in the water or loose
by the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, then they saye, hale vp the Slatch
of the Rope or Cabell: Alsoe when it hath beene
a sett of Fowle weather, and that there comes an
intrim or smale tyme of faire weather to 
serve the Turne: They call it a little Slatch
of faire weather or the Contrary:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sleeper(n)">Sleepers;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the tymbers <expan type="wch">which</expan> lye fore and Aft the
Bottome of the shipp on either side of the Keeleson
iust as the Runghead<expan type="es">es</expan> doe goe; The lower 
most of these is bolted to the Runghead<expan type="es">es</expan> and
the vppermost to the Futtockes, and soe these
betweene them doe strengthen and bynd fast 
the Futtockes and the Rung<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are lett 
downe one by an other, and have noe other 
byndinge but those Sleepers: These doe lyne
out (as it weare) and discribe the Narrowinge
of the ships floare;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sling(n)">Slings;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">There are slinges to Slinge Caskes in 
when wee hoise them in, or anie the like
wch are made of a Rope spliced at either
end into it selfe makeinge an eye at 
</page>

<page no="163">
either end soe large as they thinke fitt to
receive into it the Casle and then the 
Middle <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Rope: Alsoe they sease 
togeather and soe make an other eye, for to
Hitch into the Hooke of the Tackle or Garnett
An other sorte are made longe <expan type="wth">with</expan> a smale 
eye att either end to putt the one over the 
breach of the Peece: The other to come over
thend of a Crowe of Iron <expan type="wch">which</expan> is putt into 
the mouth of the peece: And soe by these 
they hoyse it in: A third sorte is anie rope or 
Chayne where <expan type="wth">with</expan> we bynde fast the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> 
a loft to the Cross-trees, and the head of the Mast
to th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd; That if the trees should breake the 
yard may not come downe: These are called
sling<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are cheifely vsed when wee come to 
feight for feare of Cuttinge the Tyes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sling(n)">Slinge;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to make fast anie Caske, Ordinance, 
Yard<expan type="es">es</expan> or the like in a paire of Sling<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="smiting line(n)">Smittinge 
lyne;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast to the 
Missen yard Arme belowe next the decke
And when the Missen saile is Farthelled vpp
This is made vp alongst <expan type="wth">with</expan> it to the vpper
end of the yard The sayle beinge made vpp wth
Rope yarnes, and soe comes downe to the 
Poope, The vse where of is to loose the Missen
saile wthout strikeinge downe <expan type="y+e">the</expan> yard For they
pull this Rope and that breakes all the Rope 
yarnes &amp; soe <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sailes come downe: This lyne
is called a smittinge lyne; soe they saye smyte
the Missen, That is Pull the Rope <expan type="y+t">that</expan> the sayle
may come downe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="163">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="snatch block(n)">a Snatch Blocke</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a greate blocke <expan type="wth">with</expan> a sheever in it and a
notch cutt through on of the Cheekes of it, by
wch notch they reeve anie Rope into it: For
by this notch one maye Reeve the midle <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te 
of a Rope into a Blocke wthout passinge it
in by th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> would belonger a doeinge: It
is made fast Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly <expan type="wth">with</expan> a strapp a bout 
the Maine Mast close to the vpper deckes
and is Cheifely vsed for the falle of the 
wyndinge Tackle, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is reeved into that 
blocke and soe brought to the Capstaine, 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="socket(n)">Socketts</form>
<xpln lang="en">The holes into <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Pintells of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Murderers
Fowlers or the like doe goe, are Called Sockett<expan type="es">es</expan>
alsoe some doe call the gudging<expan type="es">es</expan> wherein the
Pintells of the Rudder doe hange by the name
of Sockett<expan type="es">es</expan>
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sound(n)">a Sownde,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Anie greate indraught of the sea betwixt 
two headland<expan type="es">es</expan> where there is noe passage
through may be called a Sownd as Ply<expan type="">mmo</expan>uth 
Sownd &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> But when they name the Sound
it is meant of that of the Lowe Cuntries
beinge the most Famous and greatest sea
that is knowne by the name of a Sownde,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sound(v)">to Sownd,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to trye <expan type="wth">with</expan> a lyne a pole or anie thinge
else the depth of the water: Alsoe when wee
would knowe what water is in the well of the
Pumpe, we putt downe a smale lyne wth
some waight to it, and that is called 
Sowdinge the Pumpe vide <xref>deepe sea-lyne</xref>
if you would knowe anie more of sowndinge
In steede of byddinge one sownde they saye
heave the lead;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="165">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sounding lead(n)">Sowndinge Leade</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is as the deepe Sea-lead only it is Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly 
but 7 <term lang="la"><expan type="li+_">liber</expan></term> weight and a boute 22 inches longe
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sounding line(n)">Sowndinge lyne</form>
<xpln lang="en">The difference betwixt the Soundinge lyne
deepe-sea lyne as these; The soundinge lyne is
bigger then the deepe sea-lyne a Soundinge 
lyne is Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly cutt to 20 faddome or a little
more The other wilbe 100 or 200 faddome: 
The one is vsed in Showle: The other in deepe 
waters: The deepe sea lyne is first marked
at 20 faddom, and soe to 30.40 &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> But the
Sowdinge lyne is thus marked at 2 faddom
next to the leade, it is marked <expan type="wth">with</expan> a peece 
of Blacke leather putt into it betwixt the
stroud<expan type="es">es</expan> and at 3 faddome the like: Att 5 
Faddam a peece of white wollen Cloth; At
7 Faddome a peece of Redd Cloath Att 10
a peece of Lether, att 15 faddom either a white
Cloth or a peece of lether, and soe it is 
marked noe further, This may be vsed when
the ship is vnder saile: But the deepe-sea
cannot <expan type="wth">with</expan> anie Certayntie;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="speek(n)">Speekes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are as it were greate longe Iron nailes wth
flatt head<expan type="es">es</expan>, and are of diu<expan type="+er">er</expan>s lengths, a foote 
or two longe, some of them are ragged Speekes
that they may not drawe out againe; They 
are vsed in many places for fasteninge of 
tymbers and Planckes: In Fowle wether
they vse to Speeke the Ordinance, That is 
Naile downe the Quoyne and the like to the
decke, Close to the Breech of the Carriage 
to helpe to keepe the Ordinance stronge vp
to the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> least they should breake 
loose when the shipp Rowles, and for the farther
Ease the vse to Take of the after truckes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="166">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="spell(n)">a Spell,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is as you would saye the doeinge of anie 
Laboure for ashorte tyme and soe seaseinge
for others to take their turnes; as when they
Pumpe 100 stroakes or a glasse they call it
a Spell: Afresh spell: That is others come
to worke, as roweinge in the Boate, when 
one saies to an other he will give him 
a Spell: That is Rowe or Pumpe in his 
place and this word is Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed to
Pumpinge and Roweinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="spell(v)">to Spell,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a saile hath much wynd in it and <expan type="y+t">that</expan>
for anie occasion either to take in, or for
feare of wronging the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> we lett the 
wynd out of it soe that it can have noe force
in it, wee saye spell the saile, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is done
by lettinge goe the sailes, and Bolting<expan type="es">es</expan>, &amp;
Braseinge the wether Brasse in the wynd
then the saile wilbe all loose in the wynd: 
But this word is most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly vsed to the
Missen saile, when they take in the Missen
<correction type="d" deleted="saile"></correction> or Speeke it vp they saye Spell 
the Missen,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="spend(v)">Spent</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a Mast or yard is broke by fowle
wether or anie the like occasion They
saye they have spent their Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> or yard<expan type="es">es</expan>
But if it come by feight or soe, they doe
not vse the word spent: But shott by the
Board or carryed a waye by the Bord, <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
a shott, or <expan type="wth">with</expan> an other shipps yard<expan type="es">es</expan> or Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
that may be bigger or stronger;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="167">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="spindle(n)">a Spindle;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the smallest <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Capstaine, wch
is betwixt two deck<expan type="es">es</expan>: To the Sindle of the
ieere Captaine are whelpes to heave the
voyald/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="splice(v)">to Splice;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to make fast the end<expan type="es">es</expan> of Ropes one into
the other by openinge the Strond<expan type="es">es</expan>, att
the end<expan type="es">es</expan> of both the Ropes, and then wth
a Fidd layinge every strond orderly one into
an other Alsoe when wee would make an eye
at th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of a Rope, wee take th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan> of the Rope
and vndoe the Strond<expan type="es">es</expan>, and soe openinge 
the strond<expan type="es">es</expan>, where wee would have the splice
wth a fidd we drawe in th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Strond<expan type="es">es</expan> 
and soe weaveinge of them orderly wee make
the splice, and soe we sease <correction type="i">th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd</correction> downe <correction type="d" deleted="the"></correction> wth
some synnett, or the like: There are these 
sort<expan type="es">es</expan> of Splyces: The round Splyce That is
the splyceinge of th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd<expan type="es">es</expan> of Two Ropes one into
an other, as I discribed: The Cunt splice 
That is when th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of either ropes are 
spliced into the other Ropes some distance
from th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd, and not one end into the other
as the first, first they will make a longe 
slitt, as it were betwixt them <expan type="wch">which</expan> is the
reason of the name;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="split(v)">Splitt;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a wynd hath blowne a saile to peeces
we saye the sayle is Splitt Alsoe when
Sheevers breake, we saye they splitt, when 
shott comes and breakes a Carriage of a 
Peece, It splitt<expan type="es">es</expan> the Carriage;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="167">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="spoon(v)">to Spoone,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to putt a shipp right before the wynd and
the sea wthout anie saile: And that is 
called Spooninge afore: This is done most
Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly when in a greate storme,a shipp
is soe weake <expan type="wth">with</expan> age or laboureinge that
we dare not laye her vnder the sea, For
that a shipe when she spoones afore doth
rowle more, yet she strives not soe much 
yett if she be a dangerous rowleinge shipp
then <expan type="p-_">par</expan>force she must be layd vnder the 
sea, or else she will rowl her Mast by the 
Bord, and alsoe it is dangerous, for if a Sea
should overtake her, when she hath a des<expan type="p-_">par</expan>ate 
Seele, it maye Chance to breake in and
founder her some tymes, then to make her goe
the steddier, they sett the foresaile (wch is 
alsoe called Spooninge <expan type="wth">with</expan> the foresaile) 
when they doe this; they are sure of sea 
roome enough;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="spring(v)">to Springe,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a Mast is but Cracket in anie <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te
As at the Honnd<expan type="es">es</expan> partners, or else where<emend err=")"></emend>
we saye it is Sprunge, as they sprunge
their Mast <expan type="wth">with</expan> beareinge a saile &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan> To 
Springe ones Loofe: Vide <xref>Loofe</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="spring(n)" lexeme="spring tide(n)""><expan type="y+e">the</expan> springe or
Springe tydes;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When after <expan type="y+e">the</expan> dead neapes, the tyd<expan type="es">es</expan> beginne 
to lift, and growe higher, we saye it is springe
wherevpon Three dayes before the full and 
Change of the Moone, the springe begins, &amp;
the Topp or highest of the springe is 3 daies
after: Then the water doth heigh most <expan type="wth">with</expan> the
flood, and lowe mast <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Ebb <expan type="wch">which</expan> is the 
reason, that att these tymes wee Launch 
</page>

<page no="169">
and ground all our greate shipps: The 
tydes alsoe runn much stronger &amp; swifter
then in the Neapes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
