<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="sponge(n)">Spunge,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Spunge of a Peece of Ordinance is 
That <expan type="wch">which</expan> make it Cleane: The are Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly 
Shipp skinnes putt att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of a Staffe, wch
is made some what bigger there accordinge
to the Bore of the Peece, soe as the Spunge
may goe in full and Close, but not to str<correction type="i">a</correction>ight
But we have it alsoe fitted to the<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd<expan type="es">es</expan> of a stiffe
roope, soe is the Rammer alsoe to spunge &amp;
lade wthin bord: wee ever Spunge a peece
of Ordinance before we putt in powder 
In feight when the Ordinance is plyed fast
to keepe it from heatinge we wett the 
spunges: Vrin is the best, but else <expan type="wth">with</expan> vynneger,
Water or what we have;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="170">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="spun yarn(n)">Spunn yarne,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is rope yarne end<expan type="es">es</expan> scraped Thynne
and Spunn on to the end of an other wth
a wynch, and make it as longe as they lift
This serves to sarve some Rope wth: But
most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly it <correction type="i">is</correction> made to make Caburne of 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="spurket(n)">Spurketts</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the holes or spaces betwixt the Futtock<expan type="es">es</expan>
or betwixt the Rung<expan type="es">es</expan> by the shipsid<expan type="es">es</expan> before
and Aft, above and belowe, To the Spurketts
belowe in Holde, <expan type="wch">which</expan> are belowe the Sleepes
there are bord<expan type="es">es</expan> fitted <expan type="wch">which</expan> they take vp to
Cleere the Spurkett<expan type="es">es</expan> if anie Ballast
gett in betwixt the tymbers: But for those
allof there is noe vse, only it were good they
were in all shipps fitted vpp <expan type="wth">with</expan> light wood
or olde Iunckes to keepe the ships sid<expan type="es">es</expan> aloft
Muskett free;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="standing-parts-of-running-ropes(n)">Standinge parts
of Runninge ropes/</form>
<xpln lang="en">The standinge <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> are those <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> of 
Runninge ropes, or rather that end of
a Runninge rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast to anie
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipp to distinguish it from the
other <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te whereon wee vse to hale as 
the standinge <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the Sheate is that
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>te <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made fast by a Clynch into a 
Ringe, at the shipps quarter and the 
like for when wee saye hale the Sheate 
That is ment by the runninge parte: But
if they saye over hale the Sheate: Then
they hale vpon the standinge <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te: The 
same is of all Tackles &amp; Runninge Ropes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stand-in rope(n)">Stand in ropes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are counted all those Ropes, as <expan type="y+e">the</expan> shrowdes
</page>

<page no="171">
stayes and Backstayes <emend err=")"></emend> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are not vsed to bee
removed, or to runn in anie Blockes: But 
are only sett taught, or slacker as they have 
occasion;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stay(v)" lexeme="bring a ship a stay(v)">bringe a shipp<doubtful type="u">a</doubtful>
Staye;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When wee Tacke the shipp before it can bee 
ready to be Tacked, she must come a stayes:
That is when the wynd comes in att the Bowe
wch was the Lee bowe before; and soe drives all
the sailes backward against the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> and
Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, soe that the shipp hath noe waye, but
drives <expan type="wth">with</expan> the broad side: The manner of doeinge
it is att one tyme, and togeather to beare vpp
the Helme; Lett flie the Sheate of the fore saile
and lett goe the fore bowlinge, and brase the 
weather Brases of the foresaile; The same to
the Top saile, and Top gallant saile, only they
keepe fast their sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>, If the Spritt saile be
out, then they lett goe the Spritt saile sheate
wth the fore sheate, and Brase the weather 
Brase: The Tackes; Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>, Brases, Bowling<expan type="es">es</expan>
of the Mayne saile, Mayne top saile, &amp; Myssen
standinge fast as they did: To be taken a Stayes
That is when the wynd, comes contrary on the
soddaine, <expan type="wch">which</expan> happens most vpon headland<expan type="es">es</expan>
or Calme weathers, and soe bring<expan type="es">es</expan> the ship
a Stayes: Some tymes by the Negligence
of them att the Helme: Sometymes if it be 
little wynd and a head sea, on the weather 
bowe a shipp may Mysse Stayinge that is to 
fall backe, and fill againe The best Condi<expan type="">cion</expan>ed
ships are those <expan type="wch">which</expan> staye <expan type="wth">with</expan> least sayles 
As <expan type="wth">with</expan> two Top sailes, or Fore top sailes and
Myssen: But noe shipp will staye <expan type="wth">with</expan> lesse
sailes then those, and fewe <expan type="wth">with</expan> soe little;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="172">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stay(n)" lexeme="backstay(n)">Staies &amp; Backstaies;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">All the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, and Top Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> and Flagg
staves have stayes exceptinge the Spritt
saile Top <correction type="d" deleted="saile"></correction> Mast, the Mayne Mast 
staye is made fast by a Lannyere to Coller
wch comes a bout the Knee of the head: The
Mayne Top Mast staye is made fast to 
the head of the Fore Mast, by a Strap and
dead-man-eye there: The Mayne Top gallant
Mast is <correction type="i">in</correction> like manner to the head of the 
Fore top Mast: The Fore mast, and Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
belonginge to it are in the same manner
staid att the Boltspritt. The Myssen saile 
comes ot the Mayne Mast by the halfe decke
and the Top Mast stayes come to the Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>
wth Crowe feete: The vse of these stayes is
to keepe the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> from fallinge Aftward
to ward<expan type="es">es</expan> the Pumpe there is much difference 
in stayinge of Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> in respect of ships 
sayleinge, or workinge generally: The 
more Afte the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> hange, The more a 
shipp will keepe in the wynd and the 
forwarder the lesse: The Flemminges 
staye their Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> much Afte because else 
their shipps beinge longe floatie shipps, 
would neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> keepe a wynd: But short and
deepe shipps rather Covett vpright Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
ther are may differences of Condico<expan type="n+_s+_">ions</expan> in 
shipps for their sayleinge accordinge as they 
are stayes: For some will have the staye taught
some slacke: The Buckstayes of all Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
wch have them Those are only the Mayne Mast
and fore Mast, and the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> belonginge to 
them, goe downe on either side the shipp and
are to keepe the Mast from Pitchinge Fore&shy;
ward on over Bord;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="173">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stern(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Sterne,</form>
<xpln lang="en">All the After most <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the shipp is called
the Sterne by a generall appella<expan type="">cion</expan>: But
most exactly considered only the very outward&shy;
most <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te Abast is the Sterne: For the 
Quarter is Counted to be from the Steeridge
to the Transome and Fashion peeces of the
Sterne;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stern sheet(n)">Stearne sheate,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Sheats;/</xref>
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="steer(v)">Steere,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to governe the ship <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Helme Hee
Steeres best, that keepes the Shipp evenmost
from yawning in and out; and alsoe that vses
least Motion in puttinge the Helme too farr over
There are three kinde of direcc<expan type="">ions</expan> to Steere by
The one is by the Land, that is to Steere by anie
marke on the land, and soe to keepe the shipp
even by the marke: This is easie: The next is by
the Compasse That is to keepe the shipp vpon
a poynt of the Compasse This is hardder because
the ships head will come before the Compasse:
The third is to Steere as they are directed and 
Conded: This is easiest of all: If you would knowe
The termes belonginge to Steereinge Vide <xref>Conde</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="steerage(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Steeridge</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the place where they steere out of <expan type="wch">which</expan> they
Maye see the Letch of the sayles to sea: If 
They be to the wynd or not;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stem(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Stem;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">The steme of the shipp is that greate 
Tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> comes Compassinge from the
Keele where in it is skarfed vp before the 
</page>

<page no="174">
fore Castles: This it may be is not all one
Tymber, as in greate shipps it Cannot: And
this doth give the Rake of the shipp when 
shipps stand stem for stem they come right
wth their head<expan type="es">es</expan> one a gainst an other: To 
give a shipp <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Stem: That is to runne right
vpon her <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Stem: To goe St<expan type="e+_m+_i+_n+_">emmin</expan>ge 
a bord a shipp: That is the same as giveinge
the shipp the st<expan type="e+_m+_">emm</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="step(n)">a Stepe:/</form>
<xpln lang="en">they Call the peece of tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made
fast to the Keelson wherein the Mayne Mast
doth stand a Stepe: Alsoe those places and
tymbers wherein the Mysson Mast, Fore Mast
and the Cap staines doe stand, are Called 
Steps:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="steeve(v)">to Steue or Steuinge;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Wee saye the Bolt spritt or Beackehead steves 
when it stand<expan type="es">es</expan> too vpright, and not straight
forward enough Alsoe the Marchant<expan type="es">es</expan> Call
the stoweinge of their Cottons, <expan type="wch">which</expan> they force
in <expan type="wth">with</expan> Scrimes soe much that the decke will
rise 6 or 8 inches, steuinge of Cottons;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="steward's room(n)"> Stewards roome,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te of the holde wher the victualls
are Stowed;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stoak(a)">Stoaked,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the water cannot come to the well, 
Wee saye the shipp is Stoaked, and that is
When the Lymber holes have some Ballast
Or anie thinge else gott into them soe as
</page>

<page no="174">
that the water cannot passe, wee saye the
Lymbers are Stoaked: Alsoe when anie tying
is gott in or a bout the Bottome of the Pumpe
soe that it cannot drawe water, we say the 
Pumpe is Stoaked: Corne and the like
Is very badd for this,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stop(v)">Stopp;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When they come to an Anchor, and have
lett runn out as sufficient quantitie as will
make the shipp wide, or that the shipp be in a
Current, wher it is best to stope her alittle
by degrees then they saye Stopp the shipp
and soe hold fast the Cabell, and then veere
out a little more and soe stopp her fully to lett
her ride: for Stoppinge leake Vide <xref>Leakes</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stopper(n)">Stopper;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a peece of a Rope haveinge a wale knote
att one end and a Lannyer splyced to it And the
other end made fast to some porte, as <expan type="y+e">the</expan> stoppe<expan type="+r">r</expan>s
to the Cablles the Bottomes of the shipps by
the decke: The Stoppers for the Maine&shy;
Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> to the knight: The vse of them is 
Cheifely for th Cabells, to stopp the Cabells
when wee come to an Anchor, that it may goe
out by little and little: The manner is, but,
<emend err="but"></emend> byndinge the wale Knott a bout the Cabell
wth the Lannyer, and it will instantly Catch
hold in it, soe that it cannot slipp a waye 
as the nippers doe <expan type="wth">with</expan> holde of the Cabell 
The terme is layinge on the Stoppers, and 
Castinge of the stoppers, alsoe wee vse them
to the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> when <expan type="y+e">the</expan> yard is hoised aloft, to
stopp it til the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> are belayd: A shipp
rid<expan type="es">es</expan> by the stoppers when <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Cabell is not bytted
but only held fast by them: But this is not
safe rideing in stresse;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="176">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="stow(v)">to Stowe;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to putt anie good<expan type="es">es</expan> in hould in order: For
else we saye it is not Stowed, but lyes in holde
Alsoe we call it the Stoweinge betweene the
deckes if anie good<expan type="es">es</expan> or victualls be placed
in order vpon the deckes: But it is not vsed
in this kinde to smale thing<expan type="es">es</expan>, as to a Chest
or the like: Alsoe the placeinge or layinge
of the Top sailes, in the Topp, is called the
Stoweinge of the Top sailes; /
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="strake(n)">a Streake,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is their terme for a Seame betwixt two 
Planckes as the Garbord-a-streake, Or the
Heeles a streake, that is one Steame some 
shipps are built <expan type="wth">with</expan> a standinge streake
or two: that is when they have the whole
breadth of a Plancke or Two ryseinge from
the Keele before they come to the Floare 
tymbers These shipps are made lye wth
the ground for wringinge their Keeles 
But this doth make them keepe an excellent
Wynd: This buylt is most vsed a mongst
The Fleming<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="strap(n)">a Strapp;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">A rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is spliced a bout anie blocke,
that the Blocke may thereby be made fast
to anie place where they have occation
to vse it, by the eye <expan type="wch">which</expan> is made in the 
Strape in the Arse of the Blocke;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stream anchor(n)">Streame Anchor,</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale Anchor <expan type="wch">which</expan> we vse to the
Streame Cabell;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="177">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stream cable(n)">Streame Cabell</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a smale Cabell <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee ride wthall in Stremes
or ryvers, or in faire weather, when wee stopp
a tyde for ever wee vse the smalest ground, 
Tackle that we have if it will serve, both for
lightnesse to waigh, and to save the best from
wettinge;/ 
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stretch(v)">Stretch;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">They vse this word not as it is Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly to strayne
a Rope: But thus, when they goe to hoise a 
yarde or hale the sheate; they say Stretch
forward the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> or the Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> That
is deliu<expan type="+r">er</expan> a longe that <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te <expan type="wch">which</expan> they must hale
into the mens hand<expan type="es">es</expan> that they may be 
ready to hoise or hale;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="strike(v)">to strike;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">is to pull downe the sailes; when one shipp
strikes to an other, it is a signe of respect 
vnlesse it be fore occasions of stayinge for one
If a man of warr come vp <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Marchant
or anie other: If he strike it is intended that
he yeeld<expan type="es">es</expan> himselfe Alsoe when a shipp is beate
vpon the ground, they saye she strikes; soe 
when wee take downe the top mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, we say strike
them downe soe when wee Lower anie thinge
into the Houlde <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Tackles or anie other
rope wee call it strikeinge downe into the
houlde;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="studding sail(n)">Studdinge
sailes;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Boome</xref>;
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="stirrup(n)">Sturrop;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp by anie Mischance hath lost 
a peece of her Keele, and that we cannot come
</page>

<page no="178">
well to mend it; but as it were Patch a newe
peece vnto it: the bynde it <expan type="wth">with</expan> an Iron wch
comes vnder the Keele and soe vpon either
side the shipp, where it is nayled very strong
wth speekes to Strengthen it: This peece
soe putt to the Keele wee call a Storrop;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="suck(v)">Suck;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When all the water is Pumped out, and
that the Pumpe doth drawe wynd, we saye
she suckes Alsoe when a shipp doth drawe 
downe the Helme and doth (as it were) sucke the
Helme staffe out of the hand att the helme: A
shipp Gripps when she doth thus, The Reason
maye be either much fowleness, The stayinge of
her Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> too <correction type="d" deleted="farr"></correction> much Aft, or that she may
be out of her Trymm;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="surge(n)">Surge</form>
<xpln lang="en">We call a wave a Surge, but it is vsed in this
sence when they heave att the Capstaine, and
the Cabell slipps back a gaine, they say the
Cabell Surges: To p<expan type="+r">re</expan>vent <expan type="wch">which</expan> Vide <xref>Nippers</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="swifter(n)">Swifters,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Doe belonge to the Mayne and Fore Mast
and are to Souccour the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> and keepe
stiffe the Mast: They have Pendant<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
are made fast vnder the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> att the 
head of the Mast <expan type="wth">with</expan> a double Blocke through
wch is reeved the swifter, <expan type="wch">which</expan> att the stand&shy;
inge <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te hath a single Blocke <expan type="wth">with</expan> a hooke wch
is hitched in a Ringe, by the Chaine wales
and soe the Fale beinge halde doth helpe
to strengthen the Mast, and this Fale is
belayed a bout the Tymbers head<expan type="es">es</expan> of the 
lower Railes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="179">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="swift(v)">Swiftinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">When we bringe shipps a ground or Carrene
them we vse to Swift the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> to ease them
and strengthen them <expan type="wch">which</expan> is done in this 
manner: They Lash fast all the Pendant<expan type="es">es</expan>
of the Swifters and Tackells, <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Rope
Close to the Mast, as neere their Blockes
As they came: there they carry forward the
Tackles, and soe Bowse them downe, as hard
and as Taught as they came, and this 
Eases the Mast soe that all the waight of 
the Mat doth not hange by the head as other
wise it would: And alsoe doth helpe to 
keepe it from riseinge out of the Stepps;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="180">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="T"><alpha>T</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tack(n)">Tacks;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are greate Ropes haveinge a wale knott
att one end <expan type="wch">which</expan> is seased into the Clewe of
the saile, and soe reeved first thorugh the 
Chestrees, and then comes in att a hole 
att the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>: the vse of this is to carry
forward the Clewe of the saile, and to make
it stand Close by a wynd, and then the sayles
are thus Trymmed: The Maine Tacke
foresaile and Missen Tackes are Close 
a bord, or haled as forward on as may be
soe are the Bowling<expan type="es">es</expan> of the weather side
The Lee sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> are haled Close aft but 
the Lee sheae of the fore saile, not soe much
vnlesse the shipp gripp: the Lee Brases 
of all the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> are brassed aft, And the
Top sailes are governed, as the sailes where
vnto they belonge And hence they saye 
a shipp stand<expan type="es">es</expan> or sayles Closse vpon a Tacke
that is Close by a wynd Hale a board The
Tacke; that is to have it Close to the Chestrees
Ease the Tacke, that is not soe Close abord
Lett rise the Tacke, That is lett it goe all 
out: It is Co<expan type="">mon</expan>ly belayed to the <correction type="d" deleted="shipps"></correction>
Bitt<expan type="es">es</expan> or else there is a Kennell that belong<expan type="es">es</expan>
to them: These Tackes doe only belonge 
to the Maine saile, Fore saile, and Missen
and they are ever more Taperinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tack a ship(v)">to Tack a 
Shipp;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">To tacke the shipp is to bringe her head
a boute to lye the other waye: As if her
head laye First, west Northwest, Now
it must lye East-North-east, the wynde 
beinge att North, then supposeinge a ship
hath all her sails out <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee vse by a 
w<expan type="y+_">yn</expan>de, this wee doe first we make her staye
</page>

<page no="181">
(for <expan type="wch">which</expan> Vide <xref>a Staye</xref>) when she is stayed then 
wee saye she is payd and soe lett rise and hale
That is lett the Lee Tacke rise and hale Aft 
the sheates: And soe tr<expan type="y+_m+_e+_">ymme</expan> all the sailes by 
a wynd as they were before: That is Cast of
the Bowlinge, <expan type="wch">which</expan> was the weather Blowing
and now sett vpp taught the other: And soe 
all sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>, Brases and Tackes as a shipp that
is tr<expan type="y+_m+_">ymm</expan>ed by a wynd must have;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tackle(n)">Tackels</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are smale ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> runne in three <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> 
haveinge either a Pendant <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Blocke to 
it or a Runner: and att the other end a 
Blocke and hooke to Catch holde and heave
in good<expan type="es">es</expan> into the shipp: There are these 
maine sort<expan type="es">es</expan> vsed That is the Boate tackles
wch stand on the Maine Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> shrowde, the
other in the Fore Mast shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> to hoise in 
the Boate, and doe serve alsoe for other 
vses: The Tackles <expan type="wch">which</expan> belonge to Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
wch serve in the nature of shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> to keepe
the Mast from strayinge; The Gunners tackles
wth <expan type="wch">which</expan> they hale in and out the Ordinance,
And lastly a wyndinge Tackle, <expan type="wch">which</expan> Vide <xref>wyndinge Tackle</xref>
The rope of a Tackle, is called the fall:
That part <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee hale vpon But the end
wherevnto the Blocke is seased is called
the standinge parte: to hale vpon a Tackle
is termed to Bouse vpon the Tackle;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tally(v)">Tallee</form>
<xpln lang="en">When they hale Aft the sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> of the 
Maine or fore-saile, they say Talley aft
the sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="182">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="tampkin(n)">Tamkin;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is anie smale peece of wood turned fitt 
for the mouth of anie Peece: <expan type="wch">which</expan> is putt
in there to keepe out the rayne or Sea&shy;
water from washinge in when the Peeces
lye wthin Bord;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tapering(a)">Taperinge,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when anie Rope or anie thinge else is made
bigger att the one end then at the other as 
the Tackes are made taperinge, <expan type="wch">which</expan> makes
them purchase the better, and save a greate
deale of stuffe, because the Rope att one end
beares little or noe stresse; I have seene in
Flemming<expan type="es">es</expan> the Top saile sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> taperinge,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="taper bore(n)">Taper-bore,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when a Peeces Bore is wyder att the mouth
then toward<expan type="es">es</expan> the Breech: Some are of 
<emend err="of"></emend> oppinion that these peeces doe not
recoyle soe much: But they are not soe 
good: for some tymes if the shott be too thicke
it may be it will not come home to the 
Powder, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is dangerouse for the Peece;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tarpaulling(n)">Tar-Powlinge;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a peece of Canvas that is all Tard over
to laye vpon a Decke or Gratinge to 
keepe the rayne from soakinge through;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="taut(a)">Taught;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is sett a Rope stiffe, and fast, as
we saye sett taught the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>; The
Staye or anie other Rope when it is
too slacke;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="183">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="taunt(a)">Taunt</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a Mast is very high for the <expan type="p-_">per</expan>por<expan type="">cion</expan>
of the shipp, we saye it is a Taunt Mast
The Flemminges have them soe for the most
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>te For Taunt Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> and Narrow yard<expan type="es">es</expan>
are best to saile by a wynde, for the sailes
stand soe much the sharper: But yet they 
wringe a shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan> more then a short 
Mast, and a broad yard, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is the reason
our shipps vse short Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> &amp; Broad yard<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tempest(n)">Tempest;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When it overblowes soe exceedingly that
it is not possible to beare anie saile, and
that is is a wynd mixt <expan type="wth">with</expan> raine or haile,
they call it a Tempest <expan type="wch">which</expan> they Count 
a degree a bove a storme;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="thought(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> thoughts</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the seat<expan type="es">es</expan> whereon those that Row
in the Boates doe sitt;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="thight(a)">Thight;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp is Staunch and makes but 
little water she is Thight, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is quickly 
knowne by the smell of the water, for if the
water stinke much it is a signe it hath layne
longe in the shipe: and if it be sweete 
it is a signe it comes in newely;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="thole(n)">Thowles;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the smale Pinnes, <expan type="wch">which</expan> they beare 
against <expan type="wth">with</expan> their Oares when they Rowe: And
stand in holes vpon the vpperside of the 
Gunnwall of the Boate: They are 
Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly made of Ash wood for Toughnes;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="184">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="thwart-ship(adv)">Thwart-ships;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is anie thinge that is done or lyes a Crosse 
the shipp from one side to the other, we saye it
lyes Thwart shipps and the Contrary in Longst
shipps: That is alonge the shipp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tide(n)">Tides</form>
<xpln lang="en">This word <correction type="i">Tyde</correction> is Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> both to the Ebb and Flood
it is called Tyde of Ebb as well of as Tyde of
Flood a wyndward Tyde is when the Tyde runns
against the Sea: then the sea breakes most and
goes highest but a shipp att Anchor Straines 
the Cabells least: A Leeward tyde, that is
when the tyde and wynde goe both one waye: Then
the sea is smoother, a Tide gate, That is where
the tyde runnes stronger, To tyde it over or vp
to a place That is to goe <expan type="wth">with</expan> the tyde of Flood
or Ebb; and soe stope the Contrary tyde wth
an Anchor till the same tyde come againe, 
and this is vsed when the wynd is contrary 
butt doth not overblowe, for then they cannot
stopp att an Anchor, and if they keepe <correction type="i">vnder</correction><correction type="d" deleted="att"></correction>
a saile they <emend err="they"></emend> will leese more in one Leeward
tyde, then they shall gett in two wyndward tydes
when they saye flowesflowes tyde and halfe tyde
in anie place, the meaninge of it is thus (for
the speech is most vnproper to C<expan type="">ommon</expan> vnder&shy;
standinge, imployinge asmuch as if it did 
flowe a tyde and ahalfe in some places 
togeather and but halfe an ebb) that the 
tyde doth runn Three howers <expan type="wch">which</expan> is 4 poynt<expan type="es">es</expan>
longer in the Offinge then it doth by the 
Shoare: by longer is not meant fower 
howers for it doth ever Ebb and Flowe
Sixe howers: But this If it be high water
at the Shoare by Twelve a Clocke, it shall
not be high water in the Offinge till it bee
Three a Clocke, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is the Compasse and
</page>

<page no="185">
tyme for the runninge of halfe a Tyde: Soe
accordinge as it Ebbs or flowes more
they saye it runnes, Tide halfe and halfe
quarter that is Five poynt<expan type="es">es</expan>: when they
come into an Harbour or over a sand,
They saye they will bringe their Tide
wth them: That is to come <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Flood
wch may carry them over: Note that when
it flowes tyde and halfe tyde; that though
the tyde of Flood runne aloft yett the
tyde of Ebb runns vnder foote, that is close
by the ground, and soe the tyde of Ebb it
will flowe vnder foote;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tie(n)">Ties;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are Fower strond Ropes Hauser layde wch
is in respect that this kinde of layinge 
doth not stretch soe much as Three strond
ropes and besid<expan type="es">es</expan> runne smoother in the 
honnd<expan type="es">es</expan>: these are the Ropes by <expan type="wch">which</expan> the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> doe hange, and doe carry vp the yard<expan type="es">es</expan>
when the Halliard<expan type="es">es</expan> be strayned to hoise the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, The Maine yard and fore yard ties 
are first reeved through the Ram-head<expan type="es">es</expan>, then
through the honnd<expan type="es">es</expan> att the head of the Mast
and soe <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Turne in the eyes of the sling<expan type="es">es</expan>
<correction type="d" deleted="par"></correction> <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made fast in the yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, they are
seased fast and Close in the yard: The 
Missen yard and Top mast-yard have but
single Ties, That is one doe runn in one
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>te: The Sprit saile yard hath none for
it is made fast <expan type="wth">with</expan> a parie of sling<expan type="es">es</expan> to 
the Bolt spritt;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="186">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="tiller(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Tiller;</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Helme and the Tiller is allone There&shy;
fore Vide <xref>Helme</xref> only the word Tiller is 
most properly vsed for that <correction type="i">wch</correction> wee Steere
the Boate by, as they say give me the Tiller
of the Boate not the Helme, yett it is all
one in vse;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tier(n)">a Tire;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a decke hath Ordinance for and Aft
though there want some, wee call that 
a tyre of Ordinance; Some shipps 
have two Tyre or Three; The forecastle
and the halfe decke beinge furnished make
halfe a Tyre, The Cabell Tyre; That is 
the Rowe <expan type="wch">which</expan> is in the Middle of the Cabell
when it is Quoyled vp:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="top armer(n)">Top armers,/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the Clothes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are tyed about the
Ropes of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> for shewe, and alsoe for
to hide men in feight, <expan type="wch">which</expan> lie there to 
<emend err="linge">slinge</emend> fire bole<expan type="es">es</expan>, vse smale shot or the like;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="top gallant(n)">Top gallant;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the Mast a bove the Top mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, these
sailes doe drawe very much water wynd<expan type="es">es</expan>
in a Loone or fresh gale soe it blowe not
to much;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="top mast(n)">Top mast;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Topmast<expan type="es">es</expan> are ever halfe soe longe
as the Mast to <expan type="wch">which</expan> they belonge: But
there is noe absolute propor<expan type="">cion</expan> in these 
or the like thing<expan type="es">es</expan>, for if a man will have
his Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> short, he may the bolder make
his Top mast longe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="187">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="top rope(n)">Top-ropes</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are those Ropes wherewth wee sett or 
strike the Topmast: they belonge only to
the Maine and fore Topmast: This Rope
is Reeved through agreate Blocke, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is
seased vnder the Cap, on one side, and then it
is Reeved thorugh the Heele of the Top&shy;
Mast where is a Brasse sheever <expan type="wch">which</expan> is placed
Thwart ships, and then it is brought vp and
made fast on the other side of the Cape wth
a Clinch to a Ringe <expan type="wch">which</expan> is fastened to the 
Cap, the other <expan type="p-_">par</expan>te Comes downe by the 
Tries, and soe is Reeved in the Knight and 
brought to the Cap staine when they heave
it;
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tow(v)">to Towe;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to dragg anie thinge a Sterne the shipe in
the water, as to Towe the Boate, or to Towe
a smale shipp or the like <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Hawser out a 
Stearne: The neere anie thinge is to the 
Boate or the like when it is Towed the lesse it
doth hinder the shipps waye, but the further 
off the easier it is for that <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Towed for
then the shipp will not give is such Twitches;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="transom(n)">Transom;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">That tymber <expan type="wch">which</expan> lyes a Thwart the Stearne
of the shipp, be twixt the Two fashion peeces
and doth laye out her breadth att the Buttocke
is called the Transom this is most vnder the 
Gunnroome Porte a Stearne to lye <expan type="wth">with</expan> a ship
Transom: That is to lye iust <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Planckes
where they are fastened to the fashion peece
a stearne, To come to a ships Transom That
is iust betwixt the Gunnrooome Porte and
<emend err="and"></emend> her quarter Porte: This is the safest, 
</page>

<page no="188">
cominge vp: for there shipps are most naked
and there Gulleyes doe vse to come vp But
now they begin to Cutt out Port<expan type="es">es</expan> close by the
Transom:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="traverse(n)">Trauers;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">We call the waye of the shipp in respect of
the poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> wherein wee saile, and the Angles
wch the shipp makes in goeing to and a gaine <expan type="y+e">the</expan> 
Travers of the shipp: As wee say a Man doth
Travers his ground when he goes in and out: 
wee vse to note how many howers a shipp hath 
gone vpon a Poynte, what sailes she hath forth
how neere a wynde, and soe iudge what waye she
makes This we sett downe vpon a paper besid<expan type="es">es</expan> 
the Platt <expan type="wch">which</expan> we call a Travers, and Then 
drawinge a Lyne from the place where we last
weare to that place where the alst pricke or 
marke is we see in the whole, what course &amp;
how farr wee are gone: This we call a dead
recconinge: Then if we can observe and finde
the observa<expan type="">cion</expan>, and this meete we are sure we 
are right otherwise we trust more to the obser&shy;
va<expan type="">cion</expan> and reforme our reconinge by that Alsoe
the layinge and removeinge of a peece of 
Ordinance till it come to lye <expan type="wth">with</expan> the marke 
Is called the Traversinge of the Peece;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="traverse board(n)">Trauers-bord;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a board <expan type="wch">which</expan> they keepe in the Steeridge 
haveinge the 32 poynt<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Compasse marked
in it <expan type="wth">with</expan> little holes on every poynt like a noddy
board: This is for him att the Helme to keepe
as it were a score<emend err=")"></emend> how many glasses they 
have gon vpon of the Compasse and soe 
sticke a pinne of that poynt This is to save
the Master a laboure whoe Cannot <expan type="wth">with</expan> soe
</page>

<page no="189">
much Curiositie watch every wynd and Course
soe exactly as he hath att Helme, especially
when wee goe by a wynd, and the wynd veers
and hales;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tree nail(n)">Tree nailes;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Quasi Nailes made of a Tree are the long
woodden Pinnes made of the harte of Oake
wherewth they fasten all the Planckes vnto
the Tymbers for though wee Bolt the 
Butthead<expan type="es">es</expan> for the better asureance and 
strenght yett the Tree nayles are they wch
doe most fasten the Planckes for wee doe
vse as little Iron vnder water as we may
Conveniently, least the shipp should growe 
Iron sicke: These tree nayles must be well 
seasoned and not sappie for then the shipp
wilbe Continually Leakie and it wilbe hard
to finde: If a shipp by anie beatinge of 
the ground doe make or give backe, or 
come a little out a gaine: They terme it 
Startinge of a Tree naile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="trestle tree(n)">Tressell-trees</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are ioyned to the Crosse trees and doe
lie Close each other and serve to the same
vse They differ only That the Tressell&shy;
trees are those <expan type="wch">which</expan> goe longst ships The 
other Thwart Ships Vide <xref>Cross trees</xref>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="190">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="trice(v)">To Trice;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to hale vp anie thinge <expan type="wth">with</expan> a dead Rope
That is when wee hale by a Rope that doth not
runn in anie Blocke, or hold vp by anie 
device but by hand As if an emptie Caske
be made fast to a Rope, that is noe Tackle
they saye Trice it vpp: Or anie Chest or like
good<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wch">which</expan> is fastened to a Rope, and soe haled
vpp by hand into a shipp, we call it haleinge
by hand when wee have not the helpe of anie
Capstaine, Tackle or the like, <expan type="wch">which</expan> might pur&shy;
chase easier, but only doe it by the ymediat 
and only force of hand<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="try(v)">To Trie;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Tryinge is to have noe more saile fore 
but the Maine saile the Tacke a Bord, the Bow&shy;
linge sett vpp, the sheate close Aft and the 
Helme tyded downe close a Board Some trye
wth their Missen only, But that is when it blowes
soe much that they cannot maynteyne the Maine
saile A shipp a Trye <expan type="wth">with</expan> her mayne saile 
except it be an extraordinarie growne Sea,
Beame, But <expan type="wth">with</expan> her Myssen not soe much;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="trim(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Trim</form>
<xpln lang="en">Though Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly by the Trymm of the shipp 
is vnderstood the Swymminge of her either a head
or a stearne, or on an even Keele, In whether
of these the shipps goes best, that they Call her
trymm But that is not only to be Counted
her Trymm for some shipps will goe well or ill
accordinge to the stayinge of the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, the 
slacknesse of the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> or the like: Therefore
in my mynd, That order of her Swyminge con&shy;
sidered <expan type="wth">with</expan> this fittinge of her Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> and Ropes
wherein the shipp sailes best should bee counted 
</page>

<page no="191">
<emend err="">her</emend> Tryme and not only the Lyne of her swym&shy;
minge in water: The wayes of fyndinge a 
shipps Tryme must be in saileinge <expan type="wth">with</expan> an
other shipp to bringe her a head soe many 
glasses, Then a sterne as many, then one
even Keele and that waye <expan type="wch">which</expan> she goes
best is her Tryme in respect of her mould
vnder water then to make her goe better
ease the stayes or sett them vpp alsoe the
shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> Then wedge the Mast or give it 
leave to playe, and soe in tyme it is eased
wth a little diligences, to Fynd, the Tryme 
of a shipp next to man of warr to finde
it whose daylie practice it is, The 
Scotchmen are the best in the world 
for they will neu<expan type="+r">er</expan> be quiett butt trye her
allwayes, and if there be anie goodnesse
in her they will make her goe;/
</xpln>
<lemenote>
Catchword on the previous page, "her tryme", not completely carried over.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="trough of the sea(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Trough
of the Sea;</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is in the hollow betwixt two 
waves when wee laye a shipp vnder the
Sea: That is when wee laye her Broad
side to the Sea wee saye she lyes in 
the Trough of the Sea;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="truck(n)">Tru<correction type="d" deleted="n">n</correction>cks,</form>

Made wthout anie Spoakes that the
Carriages of the Ordinance doe 
runne on: Alsoe those little round 
thing<expan type="es">es</expan> of wood <expan type="wch">which</expan> belonge to the

</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="truss(n)">Trusses;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Trusses, are Ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> are made fast to
the <expan type="p-_">par</expan>parcell of the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> and are vsed to
two vses, one to bynd fast the yard<expan type="es">es</expan> to the
Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> when she rowles either a Hull or at
Anchor; The other is to hale downe the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> in a Storme or Gust These be&shy;
longe only to the Mayne yard<expan type="es">es</expan> and fore
yard<expan type="es">es</expan>, and they are brought too but vpon
Occation And alsoe to the Myssen <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
hath ever a Trusse;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="tuck(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Tuck,</form>
<xpln lang="en">The word is significant for it is as you
would saye the gatheringe vp of the ships
quarter vnder water: If it lye lowe that
makes the shipp have a flatt quarter
and hinders the water from passinge 
swiftly to the Rudder: If it lye high the 
shipp must be well layed out in the quarte<expan type="+r">er</expan>
else she will want beareinge for her after
workes <expan type="wch">which</expan> beinge soe high and weightie
doe charge a shipp much;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Board</xref>:
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="veer(v)">Veere;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">To veere out a Rope is to putt it out by 
hand or to lett a runn out when as you may 
stop it as Veere more Cabell That is lett
more runne out: Veere it is generally vsed
to the letting out more ropes to those ropes
wch are vsed wthoutboard as to the Boate rope
logg lyne or anie Rope whereby wee towe any
thinge but it is not vsed to anie Runninge Rope
but only to the Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan>: Veere more sheate
that is putt out, when the wynd doth goe
in and out That is some tymes to one 
poynt some tymes to an other and that 
soddaynlie as in the storme it will very 
much they say the wynd doth Veere and
Hull/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="veer(v)" lexeme="veering(a)">Veereinge</form>
<xpln lang="en">When a shipp sayles and <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Sheate is Veered
Out wee saye she goes Veereinge, Vide 
<xref>large</xref> and <xref>Quarter wynd<expan type="es">es</expan></xref> for it is all 
one;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="viol(n)">a Violl</form>
<xpln lang="en">When <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan> is in such stiffe ground that
we cannot weigh it, or else that the sea goes
soe high that the Mayne Capstayne cannot 
purchase in the Cabell: Then for more helpe
we take a Hawser &amp; open one Strond And soe
putt into it Nippers some 7 or 8 faddome 
distant from each other, and <expan type="wth">with</expan> these Nippers
wee bynd fast the Hawser to the Cabell, and
soe bringe the Hawser to the Ieere Capstayne
and heave vpon it; and this will purchase
more then the Mayne Capstayne cann:
The vyall is fastened togeather att both
end<expan type="es">es</expan> <expan type="wth">with</expan> an eye and a walle Knott or else
wth two eyes seased togeather;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="194">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="W"><alpha>W</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="waft(v)">To Waft</form>
<xpln lang="en">To waft is to guard anie shipp or fleete at
Sea; As wee call men of warre <expan type="wch">which</expan> Conduct
Marchant<expan type="es">es</expan> safe a longe, wafters Alsoe es 
Are tearmed when they make signes to have <expan type="y+e">the</expan> 
Boate come a board <expan type="wch">which</expan> is a Coate, gowne or
the like hunge vpp in the Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> Alsoe it
is a Co<expan type="">mmon</expan> signe of some extremitie when 
a shipp doth hange a waft vpon the Mayne 
staye anie blanckett<expan type="es">es</expan> <correction type="i">gowne</correction> or the like hanginge out
for a signe is called a waft <expan type="wch">which</expan> if it be hunge
vpon the Mayne staye is a signe that the shipp
hath sprunge a Leake or is in some distresse,/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wake(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Wake;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">The wake of the shipp is the smoth water wch
the shipp doth make a Stearne her, showinge 
the waye that the shipp hath gone in the 
sea: By this wee give a Iudgement what waye
the shipp doth make: for if the wake be right
the Stearne, then we knowe she makes her
waye good as she lookes But if the wake
be apoynt two or more to Leeward, Then the
shipp goes to Leeward of her Course 
when a shipp doth staye a weather her 
wake: That is when shee doth not fall to 
Leeward it is a signe she feeles her helme
well and is a nimble shipp: In Chaseinge
they saye wee have gott her wake; that
is wee are gott as farr into the wynd as
shee, and soe goe right after her as she
goes
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wale(n)">Wale;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Vide <xref>Bend</xref>;
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="195">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="wale reared(a)">Wale reared;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is when ashipp is buylt right vp After
she comes to her beareinge: This is vnsightly
and as they terme it not shipp-shapen, But
it makes a shipp wthin board much more 
Roomyer and not the lesse wholsome shipp in
The Sea if her beareinge be well layd out;
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="walt(a)">Walt</form>
<xpln lang="en">A ship is said to be walt when she hath not
Ballast enough to keepe her stiffe to beare
a saile:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="warp(n)">Warpe</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is anie <correction type="d" deleted="thinge"></correction> Rope <expan type="wch">which</expan> is vsed to warpe
wth most Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly is a Hawser:
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="warp(v)">to Warpe;</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to have a Hawser or anie other Rope 
sufficient to Hale vpp the shipp, and an Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>
bent to it and soe they laye that out over the
Barr, over <expan type="wch">which</expan> wee are to goe, and soe by that
to hale the shipp foreward It is vsed when
wee want a wynd to carry vs out or into
a Harbo<expan type="">r</expan>, and this is called warpinge;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wash-a-ship(v)">to Wash a ship</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is vsed at Sea when wee cannot come
a ground to Carrene her, Then wee make
her heeld over <expan type="wth">with</expan> the Ordinance, and men
vpon the yard Armes to a side, and soe 
wash that side and scrape it soe much as it
is out of the water <expan type="wch">which</expan> is Co<expan type="">mmon</expan>ly some
Five or Sixe strookes: This is done in 
Calmes or in a Smooth roade;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="196">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wash of the shore(n)">Wash of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> shoare;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is Close by the Shoare;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="waist(n)">Wast</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that part of <expan type="y+_">the</expan> Ship <expan type="w+ch">which</expan> is betweene <expan type="y+_">the</expan>
Maine-mast and the fore-castle.
</xpln>

Word-entry absent from British Library Sloane MS 207 but found in British Library Additional MS 21571, fol. 148r.
</lemenote>

</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="waist board(n)">Wast board<expan type="es">es</expan>;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the Boards <expan type="wch">which</expan> are sett vpp in the wast
of a shipp betwixt the Gunnwall &amp; the Mast&shy;
trees: but they are most vsed for boates to
be sett vp a longst the sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, to keepe the sea
from breakinge into them;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="waist clothes">Wast Clothes;</form>
<xpln lang="en">By a generall tearme all the Clothes wch
are Round a bout the Cage worke of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Hull
of the shipp are called wastclothes and are
the same that wee call the feight of the
shipp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="watch(n)">Watch;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">At the sea the ships Company is divided
into two <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan>, the one called the Starbord
watch, the other the Larbord watch, The
Master is the Cheife of the Starbord and
his right hand Mat<expan type="es">es</expan> of the Larbord: these
are in their turnes to watch, Tryme sailes
Pumpe, and doe all duties for Fower
howers, and then the other watch is to 
releive them, Fower howers they call 
a whole watch: In Harbor and Road<expan type="es">es</expan>
they watch but Quarter watch; that
is when one quarter of the Company
doe watch at a tyme;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="water borne(a)">Water borne;</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is when a ship is iust even off 
the ground, that she float<expan type="es">es</expan>: Then she is 
water borne;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="197">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="water line(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> water-lyne</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that tyme which <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Ship wright<expan type="es">es</expan> doe p<expan type="+r">re</expan>tend
should be the depth <expan type="wch">which</expan> the shipp should swyme
in when she is laden both a head and Starne
for you must knowe a shipp never drawes soe
much a head as she doth a Starne, for if she
should, she would never Steere well;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="watershot(n)">Water shott</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a kinde of Mooreinge, that is to laye the Ancho<expan type="+r">r</expan>s
not Crosse the tyde, nor right vp and downe 
the tyde, but as you would saye betwixt both 
That is quarteringe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="water way(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Water waye</form>
<xpln lang="en">The smale peece of lead or Tymber wch
has fore and Aft on all the ships deckes close
by the sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is to keepe the water from 
runninge downe there is Called the water&shy;
waye
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wave(v)">Waueinge;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is makinge a signe for a shipp or a Boate 
to come toward<expan type="es">es</expan> them, or else to goe from them
as the signe is made, either toward<expan type="es">es</expan> or fromward<expan type="es">es</expan>
the shipp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="way of the ship(n)">Way of a ship</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Rake and runne of a shipp is called her
waye forward on, or aftward on; Alsoe when she
sayles a place they will saye the ship hath good 
waye, fresh waye or the like: likewise in castinge
the dead <emend err="renynge">recnynge</emend>, they allowe her Leeward waye;
That is soe much as she drives to Leeward
</page>

<page no="198">
from that she seems to goe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="weather(v)">to Weather;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is to goe to wyndward of a place or 
shipp: Somtymes wee are embayed soe that
wee cannot weather a headland to gett cleere
and then we must doe our best to turne in and
out till wee can have a faire wynd to Clawe
it off;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="weather bow(n)">Weather-bowe</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is the Bowe next weather and soe of
all part<expan type="es">es</expan> of the ship or anie thinge that is to 
the wyndward most side wee saye it is the weather
parte or the weather &amp;<expan type="c'">c.</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="weather coil(v)">to Weather
Coyle;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is when a shipp is a Hull to laye her head 
the other waye, wthout looseinge anie sayle wch
is only donne by beareinge vpp the Helme, It
is an excellent Condi<expan type="">cion</expan> in a shipp; for 
most shipps will not weather coyle: the vse
of it is: that when wee desire, to drive <expan type="wth">with</expan> her
head the other way a Hull, then wee neede not
open anie sayle, wherewth before the shipp can
come neere she will runne a greate waye 
to Leeward when once she is before the 
wynde and sea vnder saile;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wedge(n)">Wedges;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Wee vse to make fast the Mast in the 
Partners <expan type="wth">with</expan> wedges, and alsoe to putt
A wedge into the heeles of the Top Mast, to
</page>

<page no="199">
beare vpp the Top mast vpon the Tressell
trees;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="whelp(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Whelps;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Are the Brackett<expan type="es">es</expan> sett to the body of the
Capstaine close vnder the Barres downe
to the decke, and are they <expan type="wch">which</expan> give the sweepe
to the Capstayne: These are made soe in 
<expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> that the Cabell may not bee apt to surge,
as it would if it did Runn vpon a whole round
body:/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="whip(n)"><expan type="y+e">the</expan> Whip;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is that staffe <expan type="wch">which</expan> the Steeres man doth holde in
his hand whereby it governes the Helme and
doth port it soe from one side to an other: It
hath a Ringe att one end <expan type="wch">which</expan> is putt over the
end of the Helme, and soe comes through the
Rowle vp into the Steeridge: In greate&shy;
ships they are not vsed for by reason of the
weight of the Rudder and the water <expan type="wch">which</expan> lyes
vpon it a fowle weather, they are not able
to governe the Helme <expan type="wth">with</expan> a whipp because 
conveniently there can stand but one Man
att the shipp;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="whooding(n)">Whoodding;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">The Planckes <expan type="wch">which</expan> wch are ioyned and fastened
a gainst the shipp sid<expan type="es">es</expan> into the Stem are called
the whoodding<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wind(v)">wynde;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">To wynde a shipp is to bringe her head aboute
either <expan type="wth">with</expan> the boate or some oares out of her 
Hawse, or Sterne post, if she be a smale ship:
The shipp wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> vpp, That is when she comes
</page>

<page no="200">
to ryde by her Anchore, when they are vnder sayle
they vse to aske, how wynd<expan type="es">es</expan> the shipp: that is 
vpon what poynt the Compasse doth lye <expan type="wth">with</expan> her
head;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="winding tackle(n)">Wyndinge tackl;</form>
<xpln lang="en">The wyndinge Tackle is thus fitted A great
double bocke <expan type="wth">with</expan> three sheevers in it, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is fast
seased to th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of a smale Cabell <expan type="wch">which</expan> is brought
about the head of the Mast, and soe serves for
a Pendant this hath a Guye brought to it, from
the fore mat to the Blocke there is reeved
a Hawser, <expan type="wch">which</expan> is alsoe reeved through an other
double bocke haveinge a Strapp att the<expan type="e+'">e e</expan>nd of it
wch strapp beinge putt through the eye of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> 
sling<expan type="es">es</expan> is locked into it <expan type="wth">with</expan> a Fidd and soe hoises
the good<expan type="es">es</expan>: the fall is reeved into the Snatch blocke
and soe brought to the Cap stayne whereby 
they heave in the good<expan type="es">es</expan>;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="windlass(n)">a Wyndlasse;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is a peece of tymber haveinge some sixe or 
eight squares, and is placed from one side of 
the shipp to the other close abast the Stem 
aloft where the Cabells come in ; These are 
neuer vsed in our greate shipps: But the 
Fleming<expan type="es">es</expan> doe vse them in good ships: The reason
is for that they goe very slightly Mand: And 
the wyndlas doth purchase much <emend err="noore">moore</emend> then 
a Cap stayne, and noe danger to the Men: For
the wyndlasse they heave about <expan type="wth">with</expan> hand speakes
putt into holes made at either end And though
they cannot heave foreward, or one should faile
the windlasse will Paule it selfe: But att
the Capstayne if anie faile: It may be the 
rest wilbe throwne from the Capstayne, and
their braynes beates out a gainst the shipp
</page>

<page no="201">
sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, if they way in a Sea gate: But the
Chap stayne doth purchase faster by much
And therefore, wee haveinge men enough
To Man it doe vse that; They have a wyndlasse
Alsoe in the head of the Boate to wey the 
Anchor by the Buoy Rope;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wind taut(n)">Wynd-taught</form>
<xpln lang="en">Anie thinge <expan type="wch">which</expan> hold<expan type="es">es</expan> wynd a loft, <expan type="wch">which</expan> 
may p<expan type="+r">re</expan>iudice the shipps sayleinge or ryding 
is said to be wynd taught, as too much 
rigginge, high Poopes and the like, Alsoe
when wee ride in anie greate stresse wee
bringe our yard<expan type="es">es</expan> alongst shipps strike downe
our Top Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> and the like because they 
hold wynd taught, is as much as stiffe, in
the sence of sea langwage, as sett taught
the Shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan>: That is sett them stiffe;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="wood and wood">Wood &amp; wood,</form>
<xpln lang="en">That is when two tymbers are lett into 
each other soe close that the wood of the one
doth ioyne close to the other;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="worm(n)">a Worme;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is an Iron on the end of a Staffe where&shy;
wth wee drawe out the shott of a Peece if 
there be anie occasion;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</page>

<page no="202">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="worm(v)">Worminge;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is the layinge of a smale Rope or lyne a longst
the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> of a Cabell or hawser: the vse 
whereof is to helpe to strengthen the Cabell
or Rope to <expan type="wch">which</expan> it is vsed: The Flemming<expan type="es">es</expan>
vse this to newe Ropes, others to old Ropes 
that are allmost decayed;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="woold(v)">Would or woulding</form>
<xpln lang="en">Is to bynd Ropes a bout anie Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>, yard or
the like, to keepe on a fish, or somewhat to
strenghten it: Sometymes when the whood&shy;
ing<expan type="es">es</expan> give waye, by the overcharginge of 
the Bolt spritt, they are fayne to would
to the Bowes, <expan type="wch">which</expan> they doe by passeinge a Cabell
through both sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, and soe bringing it in a gaine
and <expan type="wth">with</expan> hand speekes to twist it to geather
as stronge as may be, we neuer fish anie
mast, or yard but wee would it alsoe, And 
that is called the wouldinge of the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan>
or yard<expan type="es">es</expan>: Alsoe those Ropes <expan type="wch">which</expan> come 
from the Beakhead, over the Boltspritt
and lash it fast downe, from ryseinge att
the Pillowe, are called the woulding<expan type="es">es</expan> of 
the Bolt-spritt;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</page>

<page no="203">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="Y"><alpha>Y</alpha>
<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="yard(n)">yard;/</form>
<xpln lang="en">As the length of the Mast<expan type="es">es</expan> are proportioned
by the breadth of the Beame, soe contrary&shy;
wise the length of the yard is proportioned
by the length of her Keele: The propor<expan type="">cion</expan>
of this is not absolute: For he whoe will have
a Taunt Mat may have the narrower 
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> and soe contrary, But the best and
most absolute that is agreed on is this: The
Mayne-yard of the ship is to be 5/6 <expan type="p-_">par</expan>t<expan type="es">es</expan> of the 
Length of her Keele, The Topsayle yard to be
3/7 of the Mayne yard, and the Mayne yard for
bignesse is to be 3/4 of an inch for a yard in 
length, the length of the fore yard is to be 4/5
of the Mayne yard: The Crosse iacke yard, and
Spritt-saile yard is to be all of a length: But
allowe the Myssen yard<expan type="es">es</expan> and Spritt sayles
yard<expan type="es">es</expan> 1/2 an inch thicknesse to a yard in length
Topp the yard That is make them hange
even: the Clewe lynes doe properly topp
the Mayne and fore yard<expan type="es">es</expan>: but when the
topp sailes are Stowed Then the topp saile
Sheat<expan type="es">es</expan> will topp them: Brase the yard: That
is travers aft that yard Arme whose Brase
is Haled, And by the Brases wee square the
yard<expan type="es">es</expan>: That is make them hange right a Crosse,
and one yard arme, not Traversed more then 
an other. If the shrowd<expan type="es">es</expan> be sett to forward they
will hinder the Traversinge of the yard: Trau<expan type="+r">er</expan>singe
the yard is to brase aft the yard;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="yaw(v)">a yawe;</form>
<xpln lang="en">When the shipp is not steered Steddy but 
she goes in and out <expan type="wth">with</expan> her head, they say she
yawes: This doth much hinder a shipps waye
and therefore when a Man of warr is in Chase
he doth putt such to the Helme whoe can keepe
</page>

<page no="204">
her steddyest, and <correction type="d" deleted="vn"></correction>evenest vpon a poynt; wch
is done only by care and iudgment to meete 
her <expan type="wth">with</expan> the helme before the head fall of or
else come to;/
</xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="yoke(n)">a Yoake;/</form> 
<xpln lang="en">When the sea is soe rought that men cannot
governe the Helme <expan type="wth">with</expan> their hand<expan type="es">es</expan>; Then 
they sease two blockes to the Helme on each
side att th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan> and Reevinge two falls through
them like Gunners tackles bringe them to
the shipps sid<expan type="es">es</expan>, and soe hange some att one 
Tackle, and some at an other, they gou<expan type="+r">er</expan>ne
the Helme as they are directed There is
alsoe an other waye of takeinge a double 
turne aboute the End of the Helme <expan type="wth">with</expan> 
a single Rope, Th<expan type="e+'">e e</expan> beinge belayd fast to the
shipp sid<expan type="es">es</expan> By this they maye guide the Helme
but not <expan type="wth">with</expan> soe much ease as the other waye
now ether of these is Called a yoake to 
Steere by
</xpln>
</wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</section>
